The Primary Meaning of 'Hatta' and Its Legal Implications
Explaining the word 'hatta' as a particle for ghaya, 'atf, and other usages

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the primary meaning of 'hatta' as ghaya and apply the Hanafi interpretive rule to distinguish between its usages when analyzing legal texts.

Core Idea
The particle 'hatta' primarily denotes a limit (ghaya); other uses are metaphorical but retain the sense of extremity.

You have likely encountered the Arabic word 'hatta' in Qur'anic verses and hadith, often translated as 'until' or 'even'. But which meaning should be given priority when deriving legal rulings? This passage from Usul al-Bazdawi establishes that 'hatta' is fundamentally a particle of limit (ghaya), and only secondarily used for conjunction or purpose. Understanding this hierarchy is essential for correctly interpreting commands and prohibitions in the sacred texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary meaning of 'hatta'
Locate the statement in the text that declares the original (asl) meaning of 'hatta' is 'ghaya' (limit). Note the Qur'anic example 'hatta matla' al-fajr' provided as evidence.
The Hanafi school holds that this is the haqiqa (literal meaning).
2
Recognize the secondary use for conjunction
Find where the text says 'hatta' can be used for 'atf (conjunction), as in 'the people came, even Zayd'. Understand that in this usage the sense of limit still applies—Zayd is the extreme (best or worst).
The sign of ghaya is that the beginning can bear extension and the end indicates termination.
3
Distinguish the two syntactic patterns
Analyze the difference between 'akaltu al-samakata hatta ra'saha' (nasb) vs. 'hatta ra'suha' (raf'). The nasb indicates the head was included in the action (eaten); the raf' indicates it is the subject of a supplied predicate.
This affects legal interpretation: was the head also eaten or not?
4
Apply the conditional (mujaza) meaning
If the beginning can be a cause and the end is not a limit but a result, 'hatta' takes the meaning of 'li' (so that). Example: 'hatta taghtasilu' (until you wash yourselves) as a limit, but if context demands purpose.
This is only when other meanings fail.
5
Implement the Hanafi rule in legal parsing
Given a legal text containing 'hatta', first test if it can bear extension (the action before 'hatta' can continue to the point after). If yes, interpret as ghaya. If not, test for mujaza. If impossible, it is pure conjunction (but this is rare and considered metaphor).
The Hanafi school applies this order consistently in works like 'Al-Ziyadat'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب كلمة : (حتى)
هذه كلمةٌ أصلها للغاية في كلام العرب، وهو حقيقة هذا الحرف لا يسقط ذلك عنه إلا مجازاً؛ ليكون الحرفُ موضوعاً لمعنى يخصه، وقد وجدناها تُستعمل للغاية لا يَسقط عنها ذلك، فعلمنا أنها وضعت له. فأصلها : كمال معنى الغاية فيها، وخُلوصها لذلك بمعنى: إلى، كقول الله عزّ وجلَّ: حَتَّى مَطْلَعِ الْفَجْرِ (القدر / ٥). وتقول : أكلتُ السمكة حتى رأسها - أي إلى رأسها ، فإنه بقي ـ أي بقي الرأس .. وهذا على مثال سائر الحقائق.
ثم قد تُستعمل للعطف ؛ لما بين العطف والغاية من المناسبة، مع قيام معنى الغاية تقول : جاءني القومُ حتى زيد، ورأيتُ القومَ حتى زيداً، فزيدٌ إما أفضلهم، وإما أرذلهم؛ ليصلح غايةً. ألا يُرى إلى قولهم: استنت الفصالُ حتى القرعى، فجعل عطفاً، وهو غاية ، فكانت حقيقةً قاصرةً.
وعلى هذا : أكلتُ السمكة حتى رأسَها : بالنصب : أي أكلته أيضاً. وقد تدخل على جملة مبتدأة على مثال واو العطف إذا استعملت ذلك. لعطف الجُمَل ، وهي غاية مع أن الخبر مذكوراً فهو خبره ، وإلا : فيجب إثباته من جنس قبله ، تقول : ضربتُ القومَ حتى زيد غضبان، فهذه جملة مبتدأة، وهي غاية.
ما - ومن ذلك : أكلتُ السمكة حتى رأسها بالرفع، إلا أن الخبر غيرُ مذكور ها هنا، فيجب إثباته من جنس ما سبق، على احتمال أن يُنسب إليه، أو إلى غيره، أعني : حتى رأسها مأكولي، أو مأكولُ غيري ومواضعها في الأفعال أن تُجعل غاية بمعنى : إلى، أو غاية جملة مبتدأة.
وعلامة الغاية أن يحتمل الصدرُ الامتداد، وأن يَصلح الآخِرُ دلالةً على الانتهاء، فإن لم يستقم : فللمجازاة بمعنى لام: كي، وهذا إذا صلح الصدر سبباً، ولم يصلح الآخر غاية، وصلح جزاء.
وهذا نظير قسم العطف من الأسماء، فإن تعذر هذا جعل مستعاراً للعطف المحض، وبطل معنى الغاية.
وعلى هذا مسائل أصحابنا في «الزيادات».
ولهذه الجملة ما خلا المستعار المحضَ ذِكْرٌ في كتاب الله تعالى، قال الله تعالى: ﴿ حَتَّى يُعْطُواْ الْجِزْيَةَ عَن يَدٍ وَهُمْ صَغِرُونَ ﴾ التوبة / ٢٩ . وقال تعالى: ﴿ حَتَّى تَغْتَسِلُواْ ﴾ النساء / ٤٣ : هي بمعنى : إلى.
Chapter: The word 'hatta'. This word originally denotes 'ghaya' (limit/end) in the speech of the Arabs, and the true meaning of this particle does not drop except metaphorically; so that the particle is assigned a specific meaning. We find it used for 'ghaya' without that dropping, so we know it was assigned for that. Its origin: completeness of the meaning of 'ghaya' in it, and its exclusivity for that in the sense of 'to', like the saying of Allah Almighty: 'until the breaking of dawn' (Qadr:5). And you say: 'I ate the fish until its head' meaning to its head, so it remained—that is, the head remained. And this is like other true meanings. Then it is sometimes used for 'atf' (conjunction) because of the appropriateness between conjunction and 'ghaya', while the meaning of 'ghaya' remains. You say: 'The people came, even Zayd' and 'I saw the people, even Zayd', so Zayd is either the best of them or the worst of them to be suitable as a limit. Do you not see their saying: 'The weaned camels leaped, even the mangy ones'? They made it a conjunction while it is a limit, so it was a limited true meaning. And upon this: 'I ate the fish, even its head' with nasb: meaning I ate it also. And it may enter a subject-predicate sentence like the waw of conjunction when used that way for conjoining sentences, and it is a 'ghaya' while if the predicate is mentioned it is its predicate, otherwise its predicate must be derived from what preceded. You say: 'I hit the people until Zayd was angry'—this is a subject-predicate sentence, and it is a 'ghaya'. And from that: 'I ate the fish, even its head' with raf', except that the predicate is not mentioned here, so its predicate must be derived from what preceded, on the possibility that it is attributed to it or to others, meaning: 'even its head is eaten by me' or 'eaten by someone else'. And its places in verbs are that it is made a 'ghaya' meaning 'to', or a 'ghaya' of a subject-predicate sentence. The sign of 'ghaya' is that the beginning can bear extension and the end can indicate termination; if not proper, then it is for 'mujaza' (conditional) in the sense of 'lam: kay' (so that), and this when the beginning is a cause and the end is not suitable as a limit but suitable as a result. This is similar to the section of conjunction among nouns. If this is impossible, then it is borrowed for pure conjunction, and the meaning of 'ghaya' becomes void. Upon this are the issues of our companions in 'Al-Ziyadat'. For this sentence, except for the pure borrowed [usage], there is mention in the Book of Allah Most High. Allah Most High said: 'until they pay the jizya willingly and they are humbled' (Tawbah:29). And He said: 'until you wash yourselves' (Nisa:43): it is in the sense of 'to'.
Source pages p. 273

Commentary
This passage is a usul al-fiqh analysis of the Arabic particle 'hatta' (even, until). The author states that its primary, literal meaning is 'ghaya' (limit or endpoint), like in the Qur'anic verse 'hatta matla' al-fajr' (until the breaking of dawn). It can also be used for conjunction ('atf), as in 'the people came, even Zayd', where 'hatta' introduces the last item which is either the best or worst. In this case, the meaning of limit is still present, but it functions like 'waw' of conjunction. The author then distinguishes between two syntactic patterns: (1) 'hatta' + noun in nasb (accusative) indicating it was included in the action, e.g., 'akaltu al-samakata hatta ra'saha' (I ate the fish, even its head) meaning it was also eaten. (2) 'hatta' + noun in raf' (nominative) when it is the subject of a following predicate, e.g., 'akaltu al-samakata hatta ra'suha' meaning 'its head was eaten' but the predicate must be supplied. The author gives a rule: if the beginning of the sentence can bear extension and the end indicates termination, it is 'ghaya'. If not, it may be for 'mujaza' (conditional meaning 'so that') when the beginning is a cause and the end is a result. If that also fails, it is pure conjunction. The author notes that the Hanafi scholars applied these principles in the book 'Al-Ziyadat'.

Key Terms
حتى (hatta): A particle that primarily denotes the end limit (ghaya) of an action or state, and may also be used for conjunction ('atf) or conditional purpose (mujaza).
غاية (ghaya): The endpoint or limit of an action, corresponding to the meaning of 'to' or 'until'.
عطف ('atf): Grammatical conjunction, linking elements with a sense of inclusion. The text notes that 'hatta' can be used for 'atf' while still carrying the connotation of being the ultimate item.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the 'sign of ghaya' for the particle 'hatta' according to the text?
A) The beginning can bear extension and the end indicates termination.
B) The beginning is a cause and the end is a result.
C) The noun after 'hatta' is in the accusative case.
D) The verb after 'hatta' is in the jussive mood.

Key Takeaway
When interpreting 'hatta' in legal texts, the Hanafi rule is to first test for ghaya (limit) by checking if the beginning can be extended and the end marks termination; only if this fails should other meanings be considered.

Qur'anic Use of 'Hatta' as Ghaya (Limit)
Analyzing 'hatta' in the Qur'an as a limit particle

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two grammatical functions of 'hatta' (ghaya as limit vs. ghaya as purpose) in Qur'anic verses and apply the Hanafi usul principle that 'hatta' as ghaya is definitive in indicating a temporal limit unless context indicates otherwise.

Core Idea
'Hatta' in the Qur'an can indicate either a pure limit or a purpose — the difference changes the legal implication.

In this lesson, we examine how the particle 'hatta' is used in three Qur'anic verses. The Hanafi school treats 'hatta' as a ghaya (limit) that definitively marks the end of an action. However, the Qur'an also uses 'hatta' to express purpose, which does not necessarily imply termination. By parsing these verses, we learn how to identify whether 'hatta' indicates a temporal limit or a causal purpose, which has significant implications for deriving legal rulings from the text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the particle 'hatta' in each verse
Locate 'hatta' in each of the three verses and note the verb that follows. Determine if the following verb is in nasb (subjunctive) or raf (indicative).
The grammatical form of the verb after 'hatta' gives clues about its function.
2
Classify the function of 'hatta'
For each occurrence, decide if 'hatta' indicates a pure limit (the action ends at that point) or purpose (the action occurs so that something happens).
The default in Hanafi usul is that 'hatta' indicates a limit.
3
Analyze the two interpretations of Q2:214
The verse 'وَزُلْزِلُوا حَتَّى يَقُولَ الرَّسُولُ' has two readings in nasb: one as limit (until the Messenger would say) and one as purpose (so that the Messenger would say). Explain the difference.
The alternative reading in raf' treats 'hatta' as initiating a new clause, also indicating termination.
4
Apply the Hanafi rule
State that according to Hanafi usul, when 'hatta' is ghaya, it is definitive for termination, but when it is for purpose, it does not necessitate termination. Which reading is preferred for legal derivation?
The Hanafi default is the limit reading, which provides a clear legal boundary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذلك : حَتَّى تَسْتَأْنِسُواْ النور / ۲۷ ، ومثله كثير .
وقال تعالى: ﴿وَقَاتِلُوهُمْ حَتَّى لَا تَكُونَ فِتْنَةٌ﴾ البقرة / ۱۹۳ : أي كي لا تكون.
وقال تعالى: ﴿وَزُلْزِلُوا حَتَّى يَقُولَ الرَّسُولُ﴾ البقرة / ٢١٤: بالنصب على وجهين
أحدهما : إلى أن يقول الرسولُ، فلا يكون فعلهم سبباً لمقالة الرسول، عند مقالته على ما هو موضوع الغايات أنها إعلام الانتهاء وينتهي فعلهم من غير أثر .
والثاني : وزُلْزِلوا لكي يقول الرسول: فيكون فعلهم سبباً لمقالته. وهذا لا يوجب الانتهاء.
وقُرىء : حَتَّى يَقُولُ الرَّسُولُ : بالرفع ، على معنى جملة مبتدأة: أي حتى الرسول يقول ذلك، فلا يكون فعلهم سبباً له، ويكون متناهياً به.
And similarly: 'until you seek permission' (an-Nur 24:27), and many like it. And He said the Exalted: 'And fight them until there is no fitnah' (al-Baqarah 2:193), meaning: in order that there be no fitnah. And He said the Exalted: 'And they were shaken until the Messenger says' (al-Baqarah 2:214) with nasb in two ways: one: until the Messenger would say, so their action is not a cause for the Messenger's statement at his statement according to what is established about goals that they indicate termination, and their action ends without effect. And the second: and they were shaken so that the Messenger would say: so their action is a cause for his statement. And this does not necessitate termination. And it was recited: 'hatta yaqulu ar-rasulu' with raf', on the meaning of an independent clause: i.e., until the Messenger says that, so their action is not a cause for it, and it terminates with it.
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage is a textual analysis of the particle 'hatta' in the Qur'an, demonstrating its function as a ghaya (limit/purpose). The author cites three verses: an-Nur 24:27, al-Baqarah 2:193, and al-Baqarah 2:214. For the third verse, he explains two possible readings in nasb: one where 'hatta' indicates a pure temporal limit ('to the extent that'), and another where it indicates purpose ('so that'). The second reading does not imply termination of the action. The alternative reading in raf' treats 'hatta' as initiating a new clause, making the action terminate at the occurrence of the statement. This shows the Hanafi usul principle that 'hatta' as ghaya is definitive in indicating a limit, but other interpretations (like purpose) are possible depending on context.

Key Terms
غاية (ghaya): A particle indicating the end or purpose of an action, often 'hatta'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the verse 'وَزُلْزِلُوا حَتَّى يَقُولَ الرَّسُولُ', what does the second interpretation (nasb as purpose) imply?
A) The shaking caused the Messenger to speak
B) The shaking continued until the Messenger spoke
C) The Messenger's speech caused the shaking
D) The shaking ended before the Messenger spoke

Key Takeaway
The particle 'hatta' in the Qur'an is a ghaya (limit) by default, indicating the end of an action, unless context strongly indicates purpose.

Interpreting 'Hatta' in Oaths: Ghaya vs. Conjunction
Deriving fiqh rulings from the interpretation of 'hatta' in conditional oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule for interpreting 'hatta' in oaths and identify whether it functions as a ghaya (limit) or a conjunction ('atf) based on the nature of the subsequent action.

Core Idea
In Hanafi usul, 'hatta' in oaths is a ghaya unless the action cannot be a limit; then it becomes a conjunction.

The particle 'hatta' (until) in conditional oaths presents a key usul challenge: does it mark the end of a condition or merely link two actions? Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani, in al-Ziyadat, works through several oath scenarios to illustrate when 'hatta' is a definitive limit and when it must be reinterpreted as conjunction. This lesson walks through those rulings and the linguistic reasoning behind them.

Guided Path
1
Identify the role of 'hatta' in the oath
In each example, determine whether the action after 'hatta' is a natural stopping point (ghaya) for the preceding action or something else.
Ghaya actions are indicators of cessation, like screaming or nightfall.
2
Classify the action after 'hatta'
If the action is a valid indicator of cessation (e.g., screaming when beaten), it is a ghaya. If it is a favor or reward (e.g., being fed lunch), it is not a ghaya but a purpose or conjunction.
Favors cannot be limits because they are ends in themselves.
3
Apply the ruling based on classification
If ghaya: stopping before the action violates the oath. If not ghaya: the oath is fulfilled by the primary action (e.g., coming), and the secondary action is separate.
When 'hatta' is not ghaya, Hanafis treat it as conjunction ('atf).
4
Derive the usul principle
The Hanafi position is that 'hatta' is metaphorically extended to conjunction when its literal meaning as limit fails, based on qiyas from Arab usage.
This extension is only for 'fa' (sequential) meaning, not 'waw'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال محمد رحمه الله في الزيادات: في رجل قال لرجل : عبدي حرّ إن لم أضربك حتى تصيح أو حتى تشتكي يدي، أو حتى يشفع فلان، أو حتى يدخل الليل : إن هذه غايات، حتى إذا أقلع قبل الغايات: حنث ؛ لأن الفعل بطريق التكرار يحتمل الامتداد في حكم البر، والكف عنه محتَمَلُه في حكم الحنث، لا محالة. وهذه الأمور دلالات الإقلاع عن الضرب، فوجب العمل بحقيقتها، فصار شرط الحنث : الكف عنه قبل الغاية.
ولو قال: عبدي حرِّ إن لم آتكَ حتى تُغديني، فأتاه فلم يُغده: لم يحنث ؛ لأن قوله : حتى تغديني : لا يصلح دليلاً على الانتهاء، بل هو داع إلى زيادة الإتيان، والإتيان يصلح سبباً، والغداء يصلح جزاء، فحمل عليه ؛ لأن جزاء السبب غايتُه فاستقام العمل به، فصار شَرْطُ بِرِّه فعلَ الإتيان على وجه يصلح سبباً للجزاء بالغداء، وقد وجد.
ولو قال: عبدي حرّ إن لم آتك حتى أتغدى عندك : كان هذا للعطف المحض؛ لأن هذا الفعل إحسان، فلا يصلح غايةً للإتيان، ولا يصلح إتيانه سبباً لفعله، ولا فعله جزاءً لإتيان نفسه، فإذا كان كذلك : حمل على العطف المحض.
وكذلك لو قال: إن لم آتك حتى أغديك : فصار كأنه قال: إن لم آتك فأتغدى عندك، حتى إذا أتاه فلم يتغد، ثم تغدى من بعد غير متراخ: فقد بر، وإن لم يتغدَّ أصلاً حنث.
وهذه الاستعارة لا يوجد لها ذكر في كلام العرب، ولا ذَكَرَها أحد أئمة النحو واللغة فيما أعلم لكنها استعارة بديعة اقترحها أصحابنا على قياس استعارات العرب ؛ لأن بين العطف والغاية مناسبة من حيث تُوصَلُ الغايةُ بالجملة كالمعطوف ، وقد استعملت بمعنى العطف مع قيام الغاية بلا خلاف، فاستقام أن يُستعار للعطف المحض إذا تعذرت حقيقته.
وهذا على مثال استعارات أصحابنا في غير هذا الباب.
وينبغي أن يجوز على هذا جاءني زيد حتى عمرو، وهذا غير مسموع من العرب، وإذا استُعير للعطف : استعير لمعنى الفاء، دون الواو؛ لأن الغاية تجانس التعقيب والله أعلم.
And Muhammad, may Allah have mercy on him, said in al-Ziyadat: concerning a man who said to a man: My slave is free if I do not strike you until you scream or until you complain of your hand, or until so-and-so intercedes, or until night enters: these are goals, so if he stops before the goals, he is in violation; because the action in a repetitive manner may extend in the ruling of fulfillment, and refraining from it is necessarily a violation. And these things are indicators of stopping the beating, so it is necessary to act upon their reality, so the condition of violation became: refraining before the goal. And if he said: My slave is free if I do not come to you until you feed me lunch, and he came to him and he did not feed him: he is not in violation; because his saying 'until you feed me' is not suitable as an indication of termination, rather it is an incentive for additional coming, and coming is suitable as a cause, and lunch is suitable as a reward, so it is interpreted accordingly; because the reward of the cause is its goal, so it worked, so the condition of his fulfillment became the act of coming in a way that is suitable as a cause for the reward of lunch, and that has occurred. And if he said: My slave is free if I do not come to you until I have lunch at your place: this is pure conjunction; because this act is a favor, so it is not suitable as a goal for coming, nor is his coming a cause for it, nor is the act a reward for the coming itself, so if that is the case, it is interpreted as pure conjunction. And similarly if he said: If I do not come to you until I feed you lunch: so it becomes as if he said: If I do not come to you and have lunch at your place, so if he came to him and did not have lunch, then had lunch later without delay: he has fulfilled; and if he never had lunch, he is in violation. And this metaphor is not mentioned in the speech of the Arabs, nor did any of the imams of grammar and language mention it as far as I know, but it is a fine metaphor proposed by our companions on the analogy of Arab metaphors; because there is suitability between conjunction and goal in that the goal is connected to the sentence like a conjunctive, and it has been used in the sense of conjunction while the goal is established without dispute, so it is appropriate to metaphorically use it for pure conjunction when its literal meaning is impossible. And this is on the pattern of our companions' metaphors in other chapters. And it is appropriate to permit based on this: 'Zaid came to me, even Amr', and this is not heard from the Arabs, and when it is metaphorically used for conjunction, it is used for the meaning of 'fa', not 'waw'; because the goal is similar to succession, and Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage presents a series of rulings from Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani's al-Ziyadat, illustrating how the particle 'hatta' is interpreted in conditional oaths. The key distinction is whether 'hatta' functions as a ghaya (limit) or as a conjunction ('atf). In the first scenario, the speaker lists specific signals indicating the cessation of beating; these are true ghayat, so if he stops before them, he breaks his oath. In the second scenario, 'hatta' with 'tughdini' is not a limit but a purpose (da'in), so stopping before feeding does not cause violation; the oath is fulfilled by the act of coming. In the third and fourth scenarios, 'hatta' cannot be interpreted as a limit because the actions are favors, so it is treated as pure conjunction, similar to 'waw'. The author then explains this usage as a metaphorical extension (istiaara) unique to the Hanafi school, arguing that when the literal meaning of 'hatta' as a limit is impossible, it can be used metaphorically as a conjunction, specifically with the meaning of 'fa' (sequential) rather than 'waw' (coordinative). This shows the Hanafi method of usul: applying linguistic principles to derive practical fiqh rulings.

Key Terms
غاية (ghaya): A limit or purpose that marks the cessation of an action; interpreted literally in oaths.
عطف محض ('atf mahd): Pure conjunction, where 'hatta' is equivalent to 'waw' (and) or 'fa' (then).
استعارة (istiaara): Metaphorical transfer of a word from its literal meaning to a related meaning by analogy.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the oath 'If I do not come to you until you feed me lunch', according to Hanafi fiqh, why does not feeding not break the oath?
A) Because feeding is not a valid ghaya
B) Because 'hatta' here is a conjunction
C) Because coming alone fulfills the oath
D) Because the speaker didn't specify punishment

Key Takeaway
When 'hatta' cannot be a limit due to the nature of the action (e.g., favor), Hanafis treat it as a conjunction, and stopping before that action does not violate the oath.

6 min
The Hanafi Metaphor: Extending 'Hatta' from Limit to Conjunction
Explaining the usul principle of istiaara in the context of 'hatta'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi justification for using 'hatta' as a conjunction via istiaara (metaphorical extension) and evaluate the usul reasoning behind it.

Core Idea
Hanafi jurists extend 'hatta' to conjunction via qiyas on Arab metaphors, even without attestation.

The Hanafi school's treatment of 'hatta' as a conjunction in oaths rests on a bold usul move: metaphorical extension (istiaara). Imam Muhammad admits this usage is not found in classical Arabic, yet he defends it as a 'fine metaphor' based on qiyas. This lesson focuses on the usul principle of istiaara as applied to particles, and how the Hanafis navigate between linguistic conservatism and legal necessity.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the problem
In some oaths, 'hatta' as a limit (ghaya) is impossible. The school must find a coherent interpretation that preserves the oath's meaning.
The literal meaning fails because the action after 'hatta' cannot indicate cessation.
2
Identify the Hanafi solution
They treat 'hatta' as a conjunction ('atf) by metaphorically extending its meaning.
This is justified by the similarity between ghaya and 'atf: both connect clauses.
3
Analyze the qiyas
The analogy is drawn from known Arab metaphors where words shift meaning based on appropriateness. They argue if Arabs can use 'hatta' for conjunction when ghaya exists, it can be extended when ghaya is impossible.
This is a qiyas on the pattern of other istiaara examples in the madhhab.
4
Note the restriction
When used as conjunction, 'hatta' takes the meaning of 'fa' (sequential), not 'waw' (coordinative), because ghaya implies succession.
This preserves the temporal nuance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The Preposition 'Ila' as Ghayah: Inclusion or Exclusion of the Endpoint
Defining 'Ila' as ghayah with intra-madhhab disagreements

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that when the ghayah (limit) stands independently, it does not enter the ruling, and identify the exception when the beginning refers to the whole (al-jumlah).

Core Idea
If the limit stands alone, it excludes itself; if the beginning covers the whole, the limit is included.

In Usul al-Fiqh, the preposition 'ila' is defined as a marker of the end limit (ghayah). The critical question is whether the named endpoint is included in or excluded from the ruling. This passage from al-Bazdawi lays down the rule and its exception, citing Quranic evidence and exploring intra-Hanafi disagreements on its application to divorce, debts, and oaths. Understanding this nuance is essential for deriving correct rulings from texts that set limits.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core function of 'ila'
The text states that 'ila' is placed for the termination of the limit (li intiha' al-ghayah). Understand that this is its primary linguistic function, and it is used in setting terms for debts.
2
Distinguish the two scenarios for ghayah
There are two scenarios: (1) When the limit stands on its own (qa'iman bi nafsihi), it does NOT enter the ruling. (2) When the beginning statement falls on the whole (al-jumlah), the limit is included and serves to exclude what is beyond.
The exception is when the beginning of the statement covers the entire range.
3
Apply the rule to the divorce example
Analyze the husband's statement 'You are divorced until a month' with no intention. According to Zufar, divorce occurs immediately because 'ila' does not delay. According to the Hanafis, the divorce is postponed because 'ila' entered upon the very act of divorce, necessitating delay.
The dispute is about whether 'ila' delays the act or only the effect.
4
Examine the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on 'from one dirham to ten'
Abu Hanifa says the tenth dirham does not enter because the absolute noun 'dirham' does not encompass it. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad disagree, arguing that the phrase does not constitute an independent limit. This illustrates the application of the rule to numbers.
The key difference lies in whether 'ten' is considered an independent limit.
5
Note the exception driven by necessity
The text notes that the first limit (in the garden sale example) entered due to necessity (_lid-darurah_). This indicates that in certain contractual contexts, the standard rule may be overridden to avoid ambiguity.
Necessity here refers to the need for clarity in transactions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[حرف : إلى :] وأما إلى فلانتهاء الغاية، لذلك وضعت، ولذلك استعملت آجال الديون. وإذا دخلت في الطلاق في قول الرجل : أنت طالق إلى شهر : فإن نوى التنجيز: وقع. وإن نوى الإضافة: تأخر. وإن لم تكن له نية وقع للحال عند زفر رحمه الله ؛ لأن إلى: للتأجيل، والتأجيل لا يمنع الوقوع. وقلنا نحن : إن التأجيل لتأخير ما يدخله، وها هنا دخل على أصل الطلاق، فأوجب تأخيره والأصلُ في الغاية إذا كان قائماً بنفسه : لم تدخل في الحكم، مثل قول الرجل : بعتُ من هذا البستان إلى هذا البستان. وقول الله تعالى: ﴿ثُمَّ أَتِمُّوا القِيَامَ إِلَى الَّيْلِ ﴾ البقرة / ۱۸۷ . إلا أن يكون صدرُ الكلام يقع على الجملة : فيكون غايةً لإخراج ما وراءها ، فيبقى داخلاً بمطلق الاسم، مثل ما قلنا في المرافق. ولهذا قال أبو حنيفة رحمه الله في الغاية في الخيار إنه يدخل. وكذلك في الآجال في الأيمان في رواية الحسن بن زياد رحمه الله. وقال أبو حنيفة رحمه الله : في قوله : لفلان عليَّ من درهم إلى عشرة: لم يدخل العاشر ؛ لأن مطلق الاسم لا يتناوله. وقالا : يدخل؛ لأنه ليس بقائم بنفسه. وكذلك هذا في الطلاق. وإنما دخلت الغاية الأولى؛ للضرورة.
[Letter: Ila] As for 'ila', it is for the termination of the limit (ghayah). For that it was placed, and for that it is used in the terms of debts. And if it enters into divorce in the statement of a man: 'You are divorced until a month': if he intends immediate occurrence (tanjiz), it occurs; if he intends postponement (idafah), it is delayed; and if he has no intention, it occurs immediately according to Zufar (may Allah have mercy on him) because 'ila' is for postponement (ta'jil), and postponement does not prevent occurrence. But we say: postponement is to delay what it enters upon, and here it entered upon the very act of divorce, so it necessitated its delay. And the rule regarding the limit (ghayah): if it stands on its own (qa'iman bi nafsihi), it does not enter into the ruling, like the statement of a man: 'I sold from this garden to this garden.' And the saying of Allah Most High: 'Then complete the fast until the night' (Al-Baqarah 2:187), except that the beginning of the statement may fall upon the whole (al-jumlah), so the limit becomes a limit for what is beyond it, and what is within remains included by the absolute noun (mutlaq al-ism), like what we said concerning the elbows. And for this, Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him) said concerning the limit in options (khiyar): it enters. And likewise concerning terms in oaths in the narration of al-Hasan ibn Ziyad (may Allah have mercy on him). And Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him) said concerning his statement: 'So-and-so owes me from one dirham to ten': the tenth does not enter, because the absolute noun (mutlaq al-ism) does not encompass it. And the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: it enters, because it is not standing on its own. And likewise this applies to divorce. And the first limit entered only due to necessity.
Source pages p. 281

Commentary
This passage discusses the preposition 'ila' and its function as a limit/endpoint (ghayah). The author begins by stating that 'ila' was placed to indicate the termination of a limit, and it is used in the timing of debts. He then examines its application in divorce: if a man says 'You are divorced until a month', the ruling depends on his intention — if he intends immediate occurrence (tanjiz), it happens; if he intends postponement (idafah), it is delayed. If he has no intention, Zufar rules that it occurs immediately because 'ila' is for postponement but postponement does not prevent immediate effect. The Hanafi position (expressed as 'we say') is that postponement delays that which it enters upon; here 'ila' entered upon the very act of divorce, thus necessitating its delay. The author then lays down the general rule: when the limit (ghayah) stands on its own (qa'iman bi nafsihi, i.e., the limit is a noun phrase that can stand independently), it does not enter into the ruling. He gives the example of selling a garden from this garden to that garden — the end garden is excluded. He also cites the Quranic verse 'complete the fast until the night' — the night itself is not included in the fast. However, there is an exception: if the beginning of the statement falls upon the whole (the entire range), then the limit serves to exclude what is beyond it, and what is within remains included by the absolute noun. He references the elbows in the verse of wudu (wash your hands up to the elbows) where the elbows are included. Based on this principle, Abu Hanifa rules that in options (khiyar) the limit enters (the end point is included). Similarly, according to a narration by Hasan ibn Ziyad, in oaths the limit also enters. Abu Hanifa then applies the rule to the example 'Owed from one dirham to ten': the tenth does not enter because the absolute noun 'dirham' does not encompass it; but the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say it does enter because it does not stand on its own (since 'ten' is an amount, not an independent limit). The same applies to divorce. The author notes that the first limit (the garden example) entered out of necessity.

Key Terms
إلى (ila): This text defines it as indicating the termination of a limit (li intiha' al-ghayah).
الغاية (al-ghayah): The limit or endpoint; the text discusses whether it enters the ruling or not based on its structure (standing alone vs. beginning from the whole).
التنجيز (al-tanjiz): Immediate occurrence of a legal effect (here divorce).
الإضافة (al-idafah): Postponement; adding the divorce to a future time.
قائماً بنفسه (qa'iman bi nafsihi): Standing on its own; a noun phrase that is independent and does not rely on the beginning of the sentence.
الجملة (al-jumlah): The whole; when the beginning of the statement covers the entire range, the limit is included.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the general rule when the limit (ghayah) stands on its own (qa'iman bi nafsihi)?
A) It enters into the ruling.
B) It does not enter into the ruling.
C) It always enters only in divorce.
D) It depends on the intention of the speaker.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi usul rule on 'ila' as ghayah: if the limit stands independently, it is excluded; if the beginning covers the whole, the limit is included.

Adverbs of Time and Place in Legal Utterances
Textual analysis of adverbs in legal rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that when an adverb's noun is omitted, the entire phrase is a single adverbial unit, and when retained, each unit is separate, to determine the number of divorces or occurrences in vows.

Core Idea
Omission of the adverb's noun unifies; retention multiplies.

This passage from al-Bazdawi's Usul examines how adverbs like ma‘a, qabla, ba‘da, and ‘inda affect legal rulings in divorce and debt. The key distinction is whether the noun of the adverb (e.g., 'yawm') is explicitly mentioned or not, which determines whether the phrase describes multiple acts or a single one. Understanding this linguistic principle is essential for correct legal outcomes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the adverb and its default meaning
For each of the four adverbs (ma‘a, qabla, ba‘da, ‘inda), state its primary significance: conjunction, precedence, delay, or presence.
Example: ma‘a indicates simultaneity; qabla indicates precedence.
2
Determine if the adverb is qualified by a pronoun
If the adverb is followed by a pronoun (kināyah), it becomes a description of what follows; if unqualified, it describes what precedes.
e.g., 'qablahā' makes the adverb describe the second divorce.
3
Analyze the debt example
The adverb 'inda implies presence, so 'I have a thousand dirhams with you' indicates a trust (wadī‘ah), not a debt.
Presence suggests safekeeping, not obligation.
4
Apply the omission/retention principle to divorce
When the noun of the adverb is omitted (e.g., 'every day' without a preposition), the phrase is a single adverbial condition. When retained (e.g., 'at every day'), each day is a separate condition.
This determines one vs. multiple divorces.
5
Derive the correct ruling
Use the principle to decide the number of divorces or occurrences of ẓihār in each example.
Example: 'You are divorced every day' → one divorce; 'You are divorced at every day' → three.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما : هذا الجنس : أسماء الظروف وهي: مع، و بعد، و قبل،
مع: فللمقارنة، في قول الرجل : أنتِ طالق واحدةً مع واحدة،
أما : مع أو معها واحدة : أنه تقع ثنتان معاً قبل الدخول.
و قبل : للتقديم ، حتى إن من قال لامرأته : أنت طالق قبل دخولك الدار: طلقت للحال.
ولو قال لامرأته قبل الدخول : أنت طالق واحدة قبلها واحدة: تقع ثنتان.
ولو قال: قبل واحدة: تقع واحدة.
و بعد : للتأخير، وحكمها في الطلاق ضد حكم قبل ؛ لما ذكرنا أن الظرف إذا قيّد بالكناية : كان صفةً لما بعده، وإذا لم يُقيَّد : كان صفةً لما قبله، هذا الحرف أصل هذه الجملة.
و
عند : للحضرة حتى إذا قال: لفلان عندي ألف درهم: كان وديعةً ؛ لأن الحضرة تدل على الحفظ دون اللزوم والوقوع عليه.
وعلى هذا قلنا : إذا قال : أنت طالق كل يوم: طلقت واحدة.
ولو قال: عند كل يوم، أو: مع كل يوم: طلقت ثلاثاً.
وكذلك إذا قال : أنت طالق في كل يوم.
ولو قال : أنت علي كظهر أمي كل يوم فهو ظهار واحد.
ولو قال: في كل يوم أو مع كل يوم ، أو عند كل يوم: يتجدد عند كل يوم ظهار.
وهذا لما قلنا إنه إذا حذف اسم الظرف كان الكل ظرفاً واحداً، فإذا أثبته : صار كل فرد بانفراده ظرفاً على نحو ما قلنا في مسألة: الغد.
As for this type: the words called adverbs (aẓ-ẓurūf) which are: ma‘a, ba‘da, and qabla. Ma‘a indicates conjunction; in a man's saying 'You are divorced once along with once', or 'along with it one' (meaning one more) – that two divorces occur together before consummation. Qabla indicates precedence; so if a man says to his wife 'You are divorced before your entry into the house', she is divorced immediately. If he says to his wife before consummation 'You are divorced one before it one', two occur. If he says 'Qabla wāḥidah', one occurs. Ba‘da indicates delay; its ruling in divorce is opposite to that of qabla. This is because when the adverb is qualified by a pronoun (kināyah), it becomes a descriptive attribute of what follows it; when unqualified, it becomes a descriptive attribute of what precedes it. This point is the basis of this entire discussion. ‘Inda indicates presence; so if someone says 'I have a thousand dirhams with you', it is a trust (wadī‘ah) because presence implies preservation without obligation and liability. Based on this, we say: if someone says 'You are divorced every day', one divorce occurs. If he says 'at every day' or 'with every day', three divorces occur. Similarly, if he says 'You are divorced in every day'. If he says 'You are upon me like the back of my mother every day', it is one ẓihār. If he says 'in every day' or 'with every day' or 'at every day', a new ẓihār occurs with every day. And this is based on what we have said: when the noun of the adverb is omitted, the entire expression is treated as a single adverbial phrase; when it is retained, each individual unit becomes a separate adverb, analogous to what we stated in the case of 'al-ghad' (tomorrow).
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage is a textual analysis of how adverbs (أسماء الظروف) function in legal utterances, particularly in divorce (ṭalāq) and oaths (ẓihār). The author explains the meanings of four key adverbs: ma‘a (with), qabla (before), ba‘da (after), and ‘inda (at). He shows how the presence or absence of the noun of the adverb (e.g., explicitly saying 'yawm' or omitting it) changes the ruling. For example, with 'ma‘a', if one says 'You are divorced once with once', two divorces occur. 'Qabla' signals immediacy: 'You are divorced before entering the house' effects divorce immediately. 'Ba‘da' is the opposite; if qualified by a pronoun, it describes what follows; if not, it describes what precedes. 'Inda' implies presence and thus in debt statements indicates a trust, not a loan. The key principle: if the noun of the adverb is omitted, the entire phrase is treated as one adverbial unit; if retained, each term is a separate adverbial condition. This is a Hanafi legal linguistic analysis, demonstrating how careful interpretation of wording leads to different legal consequences.

Key Terms
ظرف (ẓarf): An adverb of time or place (here: مع, قبل, بعد, عند) that indicates the context of an action.
كناية (kināyah): A pronoun or indirect reference; when a ظرف is qualified by a kināyah, it becomes a صفة (attribute) of what follows.
حضرة (ḥaḍrah): Presence; the meaning of the adverb 'inda, implying physical presence which in legal debt indicates a trust (وديعة) rather than a binding obligation.

A) One divorce
B) Two divorces
C) Three divorces
D) It depends on intention

Key Takeaway
The presence or absence of the noun of an adverb is decisive: omission unifies the condition, retention multiplies it.

Exception Particles and Their Legal Effect in Hanafi Usul
Usul rule on exception particles (ghayr) and intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the grammatical cases of ghayr (raf‘ vs. nasb) and apply the corresponding legal ruling on debt liability, including the intra-Hanafi disagreement.

Core Idea
The grammatical case of ghayr determines whether it is an adjective or an exception, altering the owed amount.

In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, linguistic precision is paramount. Exception particles (ḥurūf al-istithnā’) can drastically change legal obligations depending on how they are inflected. This lesson unpacks the rule for ghayr (غير) and the disagreement within the school about its effect when the excepted amount is ambiguous.

Guided Path
1
Identify the particle and its inflection
Determine whether ghayr is in nominative (raf‘) or accusative (nasb). The case dictates its function: raf‘ makes it an adjective (ṣifah), nasb makes it a true exception (istithnā’).
In Arabic, ghayr is a noun that can take either case ending.
2
Apply the default rule
If raf‘: ghayr is an adjective, so no deduction from the stated amount. If nasb: it is a true exception, so deduct the excepted amount.
Example: 'dirham ghayru dānaq' (raf‘) = full dirham; 'dirham ghayra dānaq' (nasb) = dirham except a dānaq.
3
Handle ambiguous amounts
When the excepted amount lacks specificity (e.g., 'ten' without denomination), note the ikhtilaf: Imam Muḥammad treats the exception as invalid (full amount due), while Abū Ḥanīfa and Abū Yūsuf estimate value based on context.
This ikhtilaf arises because 'ten' is ambiguous (could be dirhams or dīnārs).
4
Extend to other particles
The same principles apply to siwā (سوى), which functions like ghayr in both cases.
The text cites al-Jāmi‘ as authority for this extension.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن هذا الباب : حروف الاستثناء، وأصل ذلك: إلا.
ومسائل الاستثناء من جنس البيان فنذكره في بابه إن شاء الله تعالى.
ذلك : غيرُ : وهو من الأسماء، تُستعمل صفةً للنكرة، وقد تُستعمل استثناء، تقول: لفلان عليَّ درهم غير دانق بالرفع صفة للدرهم، فيلزمه درهم تام.
ولو قال: غير دائق بالنصب : كان استثناء، فيلزمه درهم إلا دانقاً.
و كذلك لو قال : لفلان علي دينارٌ غيرُ عشرة بالرفع لزمه دينار، ولو نَصَبَه : فكذلك عند محمد رحمه الله.
وعند أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف رحمهما الله : يلزمه دينار إلا قدر قيمة عشرة دراهم منه.
وما يقع من الفصل بين البيان والمعارضة : نذكره في باب البيان إن شاء الله تعالى.
وأما سوى فمثلُ غير، وذلك في «الجامع»: إن كان في يدي دراهم إلا ثلاثة أو غير ثلاثة أو : سوى ثلاثة : على ما قلنا.
And from this chapter are the letters of exception (ḥurūf al-istithnā’), the fundamental one being illā. Issues of exception belong to the category of bayān (clarification); we will mention them in its chapter if God wills. That is ghayru: it is a noun, used as an adjective for an indefinite noun, and may be used as an exception. You say: 'I owe so-and-so a dirham minus a dānaq' with nominative (raf‘) as an adjective for dirham, so he is liable for a full dirham. If he said 'ghayra dāniq' with accusative (nasb), it is an exception, so he is liable for a dirham except a dānaq. Similarly, if he says 'I owe so-and-so a dīnār minus ten' with nominative, he is liable for a dīnār; if he puts it in the accusative, according to Muḥammad (may God have mercy on him) similarly; but according to Abū Ḥanīfa and Abū Yūsuf (may God have mercy on them both), he is liable for a dīnār except the value of ten dirhams from it. What occurs of separation between bayān and mu‘āraḍa we will mention in the chapter of bayān if God wills. As for siwā, it is like ghayr, and that is in al-Jāmi‘: 'If I have dirhams in my hand except three, or ghayru thalātha, or siwā thalātha', according to what we said.
Source pages p. 287

Commentary
This passage is a Hanafi usul rule about exception particles (حروف الاستثناء). The primary particle is إلا (illā). The author explains the word غير (ghayr), which can function either as an adjective (صفة) or as an exception depending on its grammatical case: nominative (رفع) indicates it is an adjective, so the entire amount is owed; accusative (نصب) indicates a true exception, so the excepted amount is deducted. For example, 'a dirham minus a dānaq' in the nominative means a full dirham is owed; in the accusative, it means a dirham except a dānaq. There is a disagreement (ikhtilāf) within the Hanafi school regarding 'a dīnār minus ten (dirhams)': Imam Muḥammad holds that both cases (رفع and نصب) yield the same ruling (i.e., a dīnār), while Abū Ḥanīfa and Abū Yūsuf hold that with nasb it means a dīnār except the value of ten dirhams (i.e., a fraction). The particle سوى (siwā) is treated like غير in all respects. This lesson is foundational for understanding how linguistic nuances affect financial and other obligations in Islamic law.

Key Terms
استثناء (istithnā’): A grammatical construction that excludes something from a statement; its letters include إلا، غير، سوى.
غير (ghayr): A noun that can be used as an adjective (with raf‘) or as an exception (with nasb); its legal effect depends on its grammatical case.
رفع (raf‘): Nominative case; when used with ghayr, it indicates that ghayr is an adjective, so no exception is made.
نصب (nasb): Accusative case; when used with ghayr, it indicates a true exception, so the excluded item is subtracted.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) A full dīnār
B) A dīnār except the value of ten dirhams

Key Takeaway
The grammatical case of exception particles like ghayr determines whether they act as adjectives or true exceptions, directly affecting legal obligations; ambiguity in the excepted amount leads to intra-school disagreement.

Ghayr as Adjective vs Exception: The Danique Example
Linguistic parsing of ghayr in legal statements

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish ghayr as an adjective (nominative) from ghayr as an exception (accusative) and state the resulting legal obligation.

Core Idea
The nominative case of ghayr gives an adjectival restriction; the accusative gives an exceptive exclusion.

In Islamic legal texts, the precise meaning of a statement often hinges on grammatical case endings (i'rab). The word ghayr is a classic example: its case ending determines whether it functions as an adjective (describing the previous noun) or as an exception particle (excluding a part). This lesson unpacks the distinction using the example of 'a dirham, not a daniq'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the case ending of ghayr
Look at the i'rab (case ending) of ghayr: if it is in the nominative (ghayru), it functions as an adjective; if in the accusative (ghayra), it functions as an exception particle.
The case ending is the key linguistic indicator.
2
Classify as adjective or exception
If ghayr is nominative and follows an indefinite noun, it is a na't (adjective) describing the previous noun. If accusative, it is an istithna (exception) that excludes the following word from the rule.
This classification determines the legal effect.
3
Determine the obligation
As an adjective: the previous noun is fully obligated (e.g., a full dirham). As an exception: the obligation is reduced by the excepted amount (e.g., dirham except a daniq).
The ruling changes based on grammar.
4
Apply to the daniq example
Say: 'I owe him a dirham, not a daniq' with ghayru (nominative) → obligation is a full dirham. With ghayra (accusative) → obligation is a dirham minus a daniq.
The daniq is 1/6 of a dirham, so 5/6 dirham owed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن هذا الباب : حروف الاستثناء، وأصل ذلك: إلا. ومسائل الاستثناء من جنس البيان فنذكره في بابه إن شاء الله تعالى. ذلك : غيرُ : وهو من الأسماء، تُستعمل صفةً للنكرة، وقد تُستعمل استثناء، تقول: لفلان عليَّ درهم غير دانق بالرفع صفة للدرهم، فيلزمه درهم تام. ولو قال: غير دائق بالنصب : كان استثناء، فيلزمه درهم إلا دانقاً. وكذلك لو قال : لفلان علي دينارٌ غيرُ عشرة بالرفع لزمه دينار، ولو نَصَبَه : فكذلك عند محمد رحمه الله. وعند أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف رحمهما الله : يلزمه دينار إلا قدر قيمة عشرة دراهم منه. وما يقع من الفصل بين البيان والمعارضة : نذكره في باب البيان إن شاء الله تعالى. وأما سوى فمثلُ غير، وذلك في «الجامع»: إن كان في يدي دراهم إلا ثلاثة أو غير ثلاثة أو : سوى ثلاثة : على ما قلنا.
And from this category: the particles of exception, and the origin of that is 'illa'. And the issues of exception are of the type of clarification (bayan); we will mention it in its chapter, God willing. That is 'ghayr'; it is one of the nouns, used as an adjective for an indefinite, and may be used as an exception. You say: 'I owe him a dirham, not a daniq' in the nominative, as an adjective for the dirham, so he is liable for a full dirham. And if he said: 'ghayra daniqin' in the accusative, it is an exception, so he is liable for a dirham except a daniq. Similarly, if he said: 'I owe him a dinar, not ten' in the nominative, he is liable for a dinar; and if he put it in the accusative, it is the same according to Muhammad (may God have mercy on him). According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (may God have mercy on them): he is liable for a dinar except the value of ten dirhams from it. And what occurs of the separation between clarification and counter-argument (mu'arada): we will mention it in the chapter of clarification, God willing. As for 'siwa', it is like 'ghayr', and that is in al-Jami': 'If I have dirhams except three, or ghayr three, or siwa three' as we said.
Source pages p. 287

Commentary
This passage discusses the particles of exception and focuses on the word 'ghayr'. The author explains that 'ghayr' can be used either as an adjective (when in the nominative case) or as an exception (when in the accusative case). The legal effect differs: as an adjective, it describes the preceding noun and does not exclude anything from the obligation; as an exception, it excludes a part. For example, saying 'I owe him a dirham, not a daniq' (with ghayr in nominative) means a full dirham is owed because 'ghayr' merely describes the dirham as not being a daniq. But if one says 'ghayra daniq' with accusative, it means 'a dirham except a daniq', so only 5/6 of a dirham is owed. The passage then presents a disagreement among Hanafi imams regarding the phrase 'I owe him a dinar, not ten' (ghayru 'ashara). If ghayr is in the accusative, Muhammad (al-Shaybani) holds that it is an exception, and the obligation is the same as if in nominative (a full dinar) because 'ashara (ten) in this context is ambiguous—it could mean ten dinars or ten dirhams. But Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf consider that the accusative indicates exception, and they interpret 'ten' as ten dirhams (since the debt is a dinar), so a dinar minus the value of ten dirhams is owed. The note about 'siwa' says it is like 'ghayr' in usage, as recorded in al-Jami'.

Key Terms
استثناء (istithna): A grammatical construction that excludes a part from the general statement, using particles like إلا, غير (in accusative), or سوى
غير (ghayr): A noun that can function as an adjective (when in nominative, meaning 'not') or as an exception particle (when in accusative, meaning 'except')

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone says 'I owe Ahmad a dirham, not a daniq' with 'ghayr' in the nominative case, how much is owed?
A) A full dirham
B) Five-sixths of a dirham (dirham except daniq)
C) Nothing
D) A daniq

Key Takeaway
The case ending of ghayr determines whether it is an adjective (full obligation) or an exception (reduced obligation); Hanafi usul uses this linguistic rule to derive fiqh rulings.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Ghayr with Numbers and the Rule of Siwa
Intra-Hanafi dispute on interpreting 'ghayra 'ashara'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the interpretation of 'dinarun ghayra 'ashara' with the accusative case, and identify the ruling for siwa as analogous to ghayr.

Core Idea
When ghayr excepts a number, the interpretation of the number's unit creates disagreement among Hanafi imams.

Having established the basic rule that accusative ghayr signals exception, the passage presents a disagreement among the founders of the Hanafi school. When a debtor says 'I owe him a dinar, not ten' with ghayra, how do we interpret 'ten'? Is it ten dirhams, ten dinars, or something else? The answer differs between Muhammad al-Shaybani and the two senior imams. This lesson also covers the particle siwa, which functions identically to ghayr.

Guided Path
1
Recall the rule: accusative ghayr = exception
From the previous lesson, we know that ghayra (accusative) is an exception particle that excludes the following word from the obligation.
This rule is undisputed among Hanafis.
2
Identify the ambiguous element
In the statement 'dinarun ghayra 'ashara', the excepted word is 'ashara (ten). The ambiguity is: ten of what? Dinars or dirhams? The context is a debt of a dinar.
The unit of the number is not specified.
3
State Muhammad's position
Imam Muhammad holds that the ruling is the same as if ghayr were nominative (adjective), because 'ashara could refer to ten dinars, which would make an exception of ten dinars from one dinar meaningless. Therefore, he interprets it as a full dinar owed.
He prioritizes logical consistency over the literal reading.
4
State Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf's position
They hold that the accusative is decisive for exception, and the number ten is interpreted as ten dirhams (a smaller unit commonly paired with dinars). Thus, the debtor owes a dinar minus the value of ten dirhams.
They give priority to the grammatical form and a practical interpretation.
5
Understand the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: under Muhammad's view, a full dinar is owed; under Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf's view, a dinar minus ten dirhams is owed (a smaller amount).
This is a genuine ikhtilaf within the school.
6
Note the rule for siwa
The passage states that siwa (except) is like ghayr, meaning it can also function as an adjective or exception depending on context.
Al-Jami' gives examples with dirhams.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And from this category: the particles of exception, and the origin of that is 'illa'. And the issues of exception are of the type of clarification (bayan); we will mention it in its chapter, God willing. And what occurs of the separation between clarification and counter-argument (mu'arada): we will mention it in the chapter of clarification, God willing. Commentary

The note about 'siwa' says it is like 'ghayr' in usage, as recorded in al-Jami'.

Key Terms
سوى (siwa): A noun meaning 'except' that can function like ghayr as an adjective or exception depending on context.

Key Terms from Source Passage

According to the passage, what is the ruling of Muhammad al-Shaybani on the phrase 'dinarun ghayra 'ashara' with accusative case?
A) The debtor owes a dinar minus ten dirhams
B) The debtor owes a full dinar
C) The debtor owes ten dirhams
D) The debtor owes nothing

Key Takeaway
When ghayr in accusative excepts an ambiguous number, Hanafi imams disagree on whether to strictly apply the exception rule or override it to avoid absurdity; siwa is analogous to ghayr.

The Conditional Particle 'in' and Its Legal Effect
Linguistic and legal analysis of 'in' as a conditional particle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions for a valid conditional sentence with 'in' and apply the ruling that 'in' suspends the cause, using the Hanafi divorce example.

Core Idea
'In' requires an uncertain condition and blocks the ruling until the condition is realized.

This lesson unpacks the foundational particle 'in' (if) in legal stipulations. In Hanafi usul, 'in' is the prototype for conditionality, and its correct application is critical for understanding suspended legal consequences—like divorce or promises. By mastering its requirements and effects, you will see how seemingly simple conditional statements can carry profound legal weight.

Guided Path
1
Define the particle 'in'
Identify that 'in' is placed for condition (shart) and only enters upon non-existent matters that are at risk (not necessarily happening).
The condition must be uncertain; certain events cannot be conditions with 'in'.
2
Examine valid and invalid examples
Analyze the given examples: 'If you visit me, I will honor you' (valid) vs. 'If tomorrow comes, I will honor you' (invalid because tomorrow's coming is certain).
The invalidity is due to the certainty of the event, not the particle itself.
3
Understand the legal effect of 'in'
Grasp that 'in' prevents the cause (illah) from producing the ruling entirely until the condition is fulfilled (suspension/ta'liq).
This is a key difference from other particles that might merely delay or qualify.
4
Apply to the divorce case
Work through the scenario: a man says 'If I do not divorce you, you are thrice divorced'. Trace why divorce is suspended until death (the condition of non-divorce is only confirmed at death) and the two narrations on timing.
The condition of 'not divorcing' is a negative which is established at death or the wife's death.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإنما نذكر في هذا الكتاب من هذه الجمل ما تُبتنى عليه مسائل أصحابنا رحمهم الله على الإشارة. وأما حرف : إن : فهو الأصل في هذا الباب، وضع للشرط، وإنما يدخل على كلِّ أمرٍ معدوم على خطر، ليس بكائن، لا محالة. أمثلته. تقول : إن زُرتَني أكرمتك. ولا يجوز : إن جاء غد أكرمتك. وأثره: أن يمنع العلةَ عن الحكم أصلاً حتى يبطل التعليق، وهذا تكثر. وعليه قلنا : إذا قال الرجل لامرأته : إن لم أطلقكِ فأنت طالق ثلاثاً: إنها لا تطلق حتى يموت الزوج، فتطلق في آخر جزء من أجزاء حياته ؛ لأن العدم لا يثبت إلا بقرب موته. وكذلك : إذا ماتت المرأة : طلقت ثلاثاً قبل موتها، في أصح الروايتين. وأما إذا : فإن مذهب أهل اللغة والنحو من الكوفيين فيها: أنها تصلح للوقت وللشرط على السواء، فيُجازى بها مرةً، ولا يُجازى بها أخرى، فإذا جُوزي بها : فإنما يُجازى بها على سقوط معنى الوقت عنها ، كأنها حرف شرط، وهو قول أبي حنيفة رحمه الله. وأما البصريون من أهل اللغة والنحو فقد قالوا: إنها للوقت، وقد تُستعمل للشرط من غير سقوط معنى الوقت عنها، مثل: متى، فإنها للوقت، لا يسقط عنها ذلك بحال، والمجازاة بها لازمة في غير موضع
We only mention in this book from these sentences that on which the issues of our companions (may God have mercy on them) are built, by way of indication. As for the letter 'in': it is the origin in this chapter, placed for condition, and it only enters upon every non-existent matter that is at risk, not necessarily happening. Its examples: you say: 'If you visit me, I will honor you.' And it is not permissible: 'If tomorrow comes, I will honor you.' Its effect: it prevents the cause from the ruling entirely until the suspension is nullified, and this is frequent. Upon this we said: when a man says to his wife: 'If I do not divorce you, then you are thrice divorced', she is not divorced until the husband dies, then she is divorced in the last part of his life; because non-existence is not established except near his death. Similarly, if the woman dies, she is divorced thrice before her death, in the more sound of the two narrations. As for 'idha': the school of the linguists and grammarians among the Kufans regarding it is that it is suitable for time and for condition equally, so it is used for jaza' sometimes and not used for jaza' at other times, and when it is used for jaza', it is only used by dropping the meaning of time from it, as if it is a particle of condition, and that is the statement of Abu Hanifa (may God have mercy on him). As for the Basrans among the linguists and grammarians, they said: it is for time, and it may be used for condition without dropping the meaning of time from it, like 'mata' which is for time and that does not drop from it at all, and jaza' with it is necessary in many places.
Source pages p. 288

Commentary
This passage explains conditional particles, focusing on 'in' (if) and 'idha' (when/if). The particle 'in' is the original conditional particle. It requires the condition to be something non-existent and uncertain (at risk) — for example, 'If you visit me, I will honor you' is valid because the visit is possible and uncertain. But 'If tomorrow comes' is invalid because tomorrow's coming is certain. The legal effect of 'in' is that it suspends the cause (the action that would trigger the ruling) entirely, blocking the ruling until the condition is fulfilled. The marriage example: a man says to his wife, 'If I do not divorce you, you are thrice divorced.' According to the principle, this condition (non-divorce) is non-existent and uncertain. The ruling (divorce) is suspended. The condition of 'not divorcing' can only be confirmed at the moment of death, so the divorce occurs just before death. If the wife dies first, she is divorced just before her death (in the more sound narration). The particle 'idha' (when) is discussed with a linguistic disagreement: Kufans (including Abu Hanifa) hold that 'idha' can function both as a time and as a condition, but when used for condition, the time meaning is dropped. Basrans say 'idha' always retains its time meaning even when used conditionally, like 'mata' (whenever). The practical difference: for Abu Hanifa, a conditional sentence with 'idha' might be treated as purely conditional without temporal commitment, while for Basrans it implies a temporal connection.

Key Terms
إن (in): A particle of condition (harf al-shart) that enters upon a non-existent matter that is at risk (not necessarily happening), suspending the ruling until the condition occurs.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is 'If tomorrow comes, I will honor you' considered an invalid conditional sentence using 'in'?
A) Because 'tomorrow' is a time word and cannot be a condition
B) Because the coming of tomorrow is certain, not uncertain
C) Because the honor is not a legal consequence
D) Because the sentence lacks a subject

Key Takeaway
The particle 'in' signifies pure condition, suspending the ruling until an uncertain condition is fulfilled, as demonstrated by the divorce example.

The Dispute over 'idha': Condition or Time?
Linguistic dispute over the particle 'idha' and its implications for legal interpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the linguistic disagreement between Kufans (Abu Hanifa) and Basrans regarding the particle 'idha' and evaluate its effect on legal interpretation.

Core Idea
'Idha' can function purely as a condition, dropping its time meaning, according to the Hanafi school.

The particle 'idha' (when/if) is more complex than 'in'. This lesson delves into the scholarly debate between Kufan and Basran grammarians, a dispute that has direct implications for interpreting legal statements in the Hanafi school. Understanding this disagreement sharpens your ability to classify conditional texts and apply the correct usul principle.

Guided Path
1
State the two linguistic positions on 'idha'
Identify the Kufan view (followed by Abu Hanifa) that 'idha' can function equally for time and condition, dropping the time meaning when used conditionally. Contrast with the Basran view that 'idha' always retains time meaning even when used conditionally.
This is a grammatical disagreement with legal consequences.
2
Analyze the evidence for each position
The Kufan evidence: 'idha' is sometimes used for jaza' and sometimes not, indicating time meaning is not inherent. The Basran evidence: compare to 'mata' which always retains time meaning.
The Hanafi position relies on the flexibility of 'idha'.
3
Understand the Hanafi response
Abu Hanifa argues that if 'idha' always had time meaning, it would be consistently used for jaza', but since it is not, the time meaning can be dropped.
This is a tarjih (preference) based on linguistic usage.
4
Apply the disagreement to a legal scenario
Consider the statement 'When you enter the house, you are divorced' using 'idha'. Under Hanafi view, it is a pure condition with no timing; under Basran view, it might imply the divorce is tied to the time of entry (closer in time).
The practical difference (thamarah) is in the legal interpretation of the condition's temporal scope.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإنما نذكر في هذا الكتاب من هذه الجمل ما تُبتنى عليه مسائل أصحابنا رحمهم الله على الإشارة. وأما حرف : إن : فهو الأصل في هذا الباب، وضع للشرط، وإنما يدخل على كلِّ أمرٍ معدوم على خطر، ليس بكائن، لا محالة. أمثلته. تقول : إن زُرتَني أكرمتك. ولا يجوز : إن جاء غد أكرمتك. وأثره: أن يمنع العلةَ عن الحكم أصلاً حتى يبطل التعليق، وهذا تكثر. وعليه قلنا : إذا قال الرجل لامرأته : إن لم أطلقكِ فأنت طالق ثلاثاً: إنها لا تطلق حتى يموت الزوج، فتطلق في آخر جزء من أجزاء حياته ؛ لأن العدم لا يثبت إلا بقرب موته. وكذلك : إذا ماتت المرأة : طلقت ثلاثاً قبل موتها، في أصح الروايتين. وأما إذا : فإن مذهب أهل اللغة والنحو من الكوفيين فيها: أنها تصلح للوقت وللشرط على السواء، فيُجازى بها مرةً، ولا يُجازى بها أخرى، فإذا جُوزي بها : فإنما يُجازى بها على سقوط معنى الوقت عنها ، كأنها حرف شرط، وهو قول أبي حنيفة رحمه الله. وأما البصريون من أهل اللغة والنحو فقد قالوا: إنها للوقت، وقد تُستعمل للشرط من غير سقوط معنى الوقت عنها، مثل: متى، فإنها للوقت، لا يسقط عنها ذلك بحال، والمجازاة بها لازمة في غير موضع
We only mention in this book from these sentences that on which the issues of our companions (may God have mercy on them) are built, by way of indication. As for the letter 'in': it is the origin in this chapter, placed for condition, and it only enters upon every non-existent matter that is at risk, not necessarily happening. Its examples: you say: 'If you visit me, I will honor you.' And it is not permissible: 'If tomorrow comes, I will honor you.' Its effect: it prevents the cause from the ruling entirely until the suspension is nullified, and this is frequent. Upon this we said: when a man says to his wife: 'If I do not divorce you, then you are thrice divorced', she is not divorced until the husband dies, then she is divorced in the last part of his life; because non-existence is not established except near his death. Similarly, if the woman dies, she is divorced thrice before her death, in the more sound of the two narrations. As for 'idha': the school of the linguists and grammarians among the Kufans regarding it is that it is suitable for time and for condition equally, so it is used for jaza' sometimes and not used for jaza' at other times, and when it is used for jaza', it is only used by dropping the meaning of time from it, as if it is a particle of condition, and that is the statement of Abu Hanifa (may God have mercy on him). As for the Basrans among the linguists and grammarians, they said: it is for time, and it may be used for condition without dropping the meaning of time from it, like 'mata' which is for time and that does not drop from it at all, and jaza' with it is necessary in many places.
Source pages p. 288

Commentary
This passage explains conditional particles, focusing on 'in' (if) and 'idha' (when/if). The particle 'in' is the original conditional particle. It requires the condition to be something non-existent and uncertain (at risk) — for example, 'If you visit me, I will honor you' is valid because the visit is possible and uncertain. But 'If tomorrow comes' is invalid because tomorrow's coming is certain. The legal effect of 'in' is that it suspends the cause (the action that would trigger the ruling) entirely, blocking the ruling until the condition is fulfilled. The marriage example: a man says to his wife, 'If I do not divorce you, you are thrice divorced.' According to the principle, this condition (non-divorce) is non-existent and uncertain. The ruling (divorce) is suspended. The condition of 'not divorcing' can only be confirmed at the moment of death, so the divorce occurs just before death. If the wife dies first, she is divorced just before her death (in the more sound narration). The particle 'idha' (when) is discussed with a linguistic disagreement: Kufans (including Abu Hanifa) hold that 'idha' can function both as a time and as a condition, but when used for condition, the time meaning is dropped. Basrans say 'idha' always retains its time meaning even when used conditionally, like 'mata' (whenever). The practical difference: for Abu Hanifa, a conditional sentence with 'idha' might be treated as purely conditional without temporal commitment, while for Basrans it implies a temporal connection.

Key Terms
إذا (idha): A particle that can function for time and/or condition; in Hanafi view (Kufan), it can be used purely for condition by dropping the time meaning.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school (following Kufan grammarians), when is the time meaning of 'idha' dropped?
A) It is never dropped
B) It is dropped when 'idha' is used for jaza' (conditional response)
C) It is dropped when the sentence is in the past tense
D) It is dropped when the condition is negative

Key Takeaway
Understanding the linguistic disagreement over 'idha' is crucial for correctly interpreting conditional statements in Hanafi legal texts.

The Particle 'إذا' in Usul al-Fiqh: Between Condition and Time
Analyzing the dual meaning of 'إذا' as time and condition in divorce.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the dual meaning of the particle 'إذا' and apply the Hanafi usul principle that ambiguity of meaning prevents the occurrence of a legal effect (divorce).

Core Idea
When a particle has two conflicting meanings, doubt arises, and the ruling (divorce) is not established.

In Hanafi fiqh, the wording of a conditional divorce is critically important. The particle 'إذا' (idha) can mean either 'if' (pure condition) or 'when' (time). This ambiguity leads to a key usul issue: when a word is shared between two possible meanings, how do we derive the ruling? This lesson unpacks the linguistic and legal analysis of 'إذا' in the context of divorce, illustrating the usul principle that doubt prevents enforcement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the particle and its two meanings
Recognize that 'إذا' can function as a conditional particle (like 'إن') or a temporal adverb (like 'متى'). This dual function is the root of the legal ambiguity.
In the divorce phrase 'إذا شئت', both meanings are possible.
2
Examine the evidence for each meaning
The Kufan grammarians, including al-Farra', cited a line of poetry where 'إذا' is used as a pure conditional. This establishes that 'إذا' can mean 'if'. On the other hand, common usage shows it often means 'when'.
The poetry evidence is a form of istishhad (linguistic proof).
3
Apply the principle of doubt (shakk)
Since 'إذا' has two equally plausible meanings in the divorce context, ambiguity (ijmal) arises. According to Hanafi usul, when a legal text is ambiguous and no preference can be determined, doubt (shakk) exists. The legal effect—here, divorce—is not established because certainty is required.
The principle: 'الطلاق لا يقع بالشك' (divorce does not occur due to doubt).
4
Conclude the ruling
Therefore, when a husband says 'أنت طالق إذا شئت', divorce does not occur. The wife's wish does not trigger the divorce because the condition is not certain.
This is the mufta bihi (operative ruling) in the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوقت، فهذا أولى، فصار الطلاق مضافاً إلى زمان خال عن إيقاع الطلاق. الا يُرى أن من قال لامرأته: أنت طالق إذا شئت : لم يتقيد بالمجلس، مثل : متى، بخلاف إن. ولا يصح طريق أبي حنيفة رحمه الله إلا أن يُثبت أن : إذا: قد يكون حرفاً بمعنى الشرط، مثل: إن. وقد ادعى ذلك أهل الكوفة، واحتج الفراء لذلك بقول الشاعر : واستغنِ ما أغناك ربُّك بالغنى وإذا تُصِبْكَ خَصَاصَةٌ فَتَجَمَّل وإنما معناه : وإن تُصِبْكَ خَصَاصةٌ، بلا شبهة. وإذا ثبت هذان الوجهان في : إذا على التعارض، أعني : معنى الشرط الخالص، ومعنى الوقت وقع الشك في وقوع الطلاق، فلم يقع بالشك، ووقع بالشك. الشك في انقطاع المشيئة بعد الثبوت فيما استشهدا به، فلا يبطل وكذلك : إذا ما .
The time, so this is more appropriate, so the divorce is attached to a time devoid of the occurrence of divorce. Do you not see that whoever said to his wife: 'You are divorced if you wish' is not restricted to the session, like 'mata', unlike 'in'. And the way of Abu Hanifa may Allah have mercy on him is not valid unless he proves that 'idha' can be a particle meaning condition, like 'in'. And the people of Kufa claimed that, and al-Farra' cited as evidence the words of the poet: 'Be content with what your Lord has given you, and if poverty afflicts you, then be patient.' And its meaning is 'and if poverty afflicts you' without doubt. And if these two aspects are established in 'idha' in conflict, I mean: the meaning of pure condition and the meaning of time, doubt occurs in the occurrence of the divorce, so it does not occur due to doubt, and it occurs due to doubt. The doubt about the cessation of the will after establishment in what they cited, so it does not nullify. And similarly 'idha ma'.
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage analyzes the Arabic particle 'إذا' (idha). In Hanafi fiqh, when a husband says 'you are divorced if you wish' (أنت طالق إذا شئت), the ruling depends on whether 'إذا' is interpreted as a conditional particle (like 'إن' meaning 'if') or as a time adverb (meaning 'when'). Abu Hanifa holds that 'إذا' can be conditional, but this must be proven. The Kufan grammarians, including al-Farra', argued this using a line of poetry where 'إذا' clearly means 'if'. However, because 'إذا' can also mean 'when', there is a conflict in meaning. When a word has two possible interpretations, doubt arises, and according to the Hanafi principle, divorce does not occur due to doubt (الطلاق لا يقع بالشك). The passage also mentions that the doubt about the wife's will (mashi'a) after its establishment does not invalidate the original ruling. The particle 'إذا ما' (idha ma) follows the same rule.

Key Terms
إذا (idha): A particle that can denote either pure condition (like إن) or a vague time (like متى).
شرط (shart): A condition that suspends the occurrence of an action.
وقت (waqt): Time; here the meaning of 'when' as a temporal adverb.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Divorce does not occur because 'إذا' is ambiguous between condition and time, causing doubt.
C) Divorce occurs only if the wife wishes in the same sitting.
D) Divorce occurs after one menstrual cycle.

Key Takeaway
When a particle like 'إذا' has two conflicting meanings, the divorce is not valid because certainty is required in establishing a legal effect.

The Conditional Particle 'Law' and Its Equivalent to 'In' in Hanafi Usul
Analyzing legal effect of 'law' as conditional particle

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that 'law' is equivalent to 'in' in conditional legal effects and derive the suspended ruling for divorce.

Core Idea
'Law' in conditional divorce is treated like 'in' because it implies anticipation.

This lesson examines the Hanafi position on the particle 'law' (if) used in conditional statements, particularly divorce. The passage cites a ruling from Abu Yusuf and Muhammad that 'law' is equivalent to 'in' when it contains anticipation, thus it creates a valid conditional suspension of the divorce. We will analyze the linguistic and usul basis for this ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the particle 'law' in the phrase
Locate the word 'law' in the statement 'You are divorced if (law) you enter the house.' Recognize it as a conditional particle.
2
Recognize the implication of anticipation (taraqqub)
Understand that 'law' carries a sense of waiting or anticipation for a future event, making it similar to the standard conditional 'in'.
Taraqqub is key to the Hanafi position.
3
Apply the Hanafi principle of equivalence
The principle is that 'law' with anticipation functions just like 'in' (إن) in suspending a ruling on a future condition.
This is based on the intended meaning, not mere grammar.
4
Derive the ruling
Since 'law' operates as a valid conditional, the divorce is suspended (mu'allaq) and only occurs if the condition is fulfilled.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذلك مَن و ما يدخلان في هذا الباب؛ لإبهامهما،
والمسائل فيهما كثيرة، خصوصاً في مَن
وقد روي عن أبي يوسف ومحمد رحمهما الله: فيمن قال: أنت طالق
لو دخلت الدار: إنه بمنزلة قوله: إن دخلت الدار؛ لأن فيها معنى الترقب
فعملت عمل الشرط.
وكذلك قول الرجل: أنت طالق لولا صحبتك، وما أشبه ذلك: غيرُ
واقع؛ لما فيه من معنى الشرط.
And similarly, 'man' and 'ma' enter this chapter due to their ambiguity. The issues concerning them are many, especially regarding 'man'. It has been narrated from Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on them) regarding one who said: 'You are divorced if you enter the house' that it is like his saying: 'If you enter the house,' because it contains the meaning of anticipation (taraqqub), so it operates as a condition. Similarly, a man's saying: 'You are divorced were it not for your companionship' and the like is not operative because it contains the meaning of a condition.
Source pages p. 291

Commentary
This passage is a textual analysis of the particles 'man' and 'ma' and the conditional particles 'law' and 'lawla'. The author states that 'man' and 'ma' are ambiguous (mubham) and thus lead to many legal issues. A transmitted ruling from Abu Yusuf and Muhammad establishes that when a husband says to his wife 'You are divorced if (law) you enter the house,' it is treated exactly like 'if (in) you enter the house' because 'law' carries the meaning of anticipation (taraqqub) and therefore functions as a condition. Thus, divorce is suspended upon the condition. Conversely, when a husband says 'You are divorced were it not for (lawla) your companionship' or similar phrases, these are not operative as divorces because 'lawla' implies something that has already occurred (a past cause) and negates the conditionality. Hence, no divorce occurs.

Key Terms
لو (law): A conditional particle meaning 'if', used to suspend a ruling on a future condition.
لولا (lawla): A particle meaning 'were it not for', indicating a past cause that prevents the occurrence of the conditional event.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
