A) A divorce that takes effect immediately
B) A suspended divorce like 'if (in) you enter the house'
C) A non-operative utterance
D) A divorce that takes effect only if the wife intends it

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, 'law' with anticipation functions as a valid conditional particle, making divorce suspended.

The Particle 'Lawla': Why 'Were It Not For' Does Not Trigger Divorce
Analyzing legal effect of 'lawla' as non-operative conditional

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school considers 'lawla' (were it not for) as non-operative for conditional divorce and identify the underlying principle.

Core Idea
'Lawla' implies a past cause, so it cannot suspend a future divorce.

This lesson examines the particle 'lawla' in conditional divorce statements. The passage states that when a husband says 'You are divorced were it not for your companionship,' no divorce occurs because 'lawla' indicates a past event that prevents the condition from being a genuine future possibility. We will analyze the linguistic and usul basis for this ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the particle 'lawla' in the phrase
Locate 'lawla' in the statement 'You are divorced were it not for your companionship.' Recognize it as a particle that typically introduces a past cause.
2
Understand the implication of 'lawla'
'Lawla' implies that the cause (companionship) already exists, meaning the condition is not a future event but a present or past reality.
This is key to why it does not create a valid conditional suspension.
3
Apply the Hanafi principle for conditional particles
For a conditional to be valid, the condition must be a future possibility. Since 'lawla' represents an already existing fact, it cannot function as a genuine condition.
4
Derive the ruling
Therefore, the phrase is not operative (ghayr waqi'), meaning no divorce occurs at all.

Source Text

Hanafi Linguistic Analysis of 'It'am' in Kaffarah
Textual analysis of Quranic verses to derive rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply Hanafi linguistic principles to derive the ruling that 'it'am' in kaffarah means ibaha (permission to feed) and not tamleek (transfer of ownership), and identify the legal indications (isharat) from the fasting verse.

Core Idea
The causative verb 'at'ama' means to cause to eat, not to own—linguistic reality dictates the ruling.

This passage from al-Bazdawi's usul demonstrates how meticulous linguistic analysis of Quranic verses yields precise legal rulings. The fasting verse (2:187) reveals multiple indications about equality of prohibitions, intention timing, and fasting while junub. But the core of the passage is a critical dispute over the word 'it'am' in the expiation verse (5:89): does it require giving ownership of food (tamleek) or merely permission to eat (ibaha)? We will walk through the Hanafi argument, grounded in the causative verb form, and contrast it with the opposing Shafi'i view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Quranic verses
Locate the two key verses: fasting (2:187 'thumma atimmu al-siyam ila al-layl') and expiation (5:89 'fakaffaratuhu it'am 'asharat masakin'). Also note the subordinate argument about intention and janabah.
The passage uses 'thumma' to indicate sequence and 'it'am' as the key disputed term.
2
Analyze the fasting verse's indications
Understand how the wording 'thumma atimmu' implies: (a) all fast-breaking acts are equally prohibited, (b) intention should be after dawn (but Sunnah allows before), (c) permissibility of causes of janabah until dawn implies fasting while junub is valid.
These isharat are derived from the structure and sequence, not explicit text.
3
Focus on the expiation verse: linguistic derivation of 'it'am'
Trace the Arabic root: ta'ima (to eat) → at'ama (to cause to eat). The causative hamza adds causation, not ownership. Therefore, 'it'am' literally means to make someone eat, i.e., ibaha.
This is the core Hanafi proof: the intransitive meaning dictates the transitive.
4
Understand the Hanafi position and response to opposition
The Hanafi position: feeding is fulfilled by ibaha (inviting to a meal). Opponents (Shafi'i) argue ibaha is part of tamleek in estimation, so text implies tamleek. Hanafi rebuttal: for food, ibaha is sufficient and final; for clothing, 'kiswa' is a noun requiring ownership, and lending is temporary.
The distinction between food (ibaha) and clothing (tamleek) shows consistency in the madhhab.
5
Evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical consequence: in kaffarah for a broken oath, a Hanafi may feed the poor by cooking a meal or distributing food without transferring ownership. A Shafi'i must give ownership of the food to the poor.
The ruling directly affects how expiation is performed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفيه إشارة إلى استواء الكل في الحظر ؛ لأنه تعالى قال : ثُمَّ أَتِمُّوا الصِّيَامَ إِلَى الَّيْلِ ﴾ أي الكفّ عن هذه الجملة، فكان حظر الكل بطريق واحد، فلم يكن للجماع اختصاص، ولا مزية. وفيه إشارة إلى أن النية في النهار منصوص عليها ؛ لقوله تعالى: ﴿ثُمَّ أَتِمُّوا الصِّيَامَ إِلَى الَّيْلِ ﴾ بعد إباحة الجملة إلى طلوع الفجر. وحرف ثم للتراخي، فتصير العزيمة بعد الفجر، لا محالة؛ لأن الليل لا ينقضي إلا بجزء من النهار إلا أنا جوزنا تقديم النية على الفجر بالسنة، فأما أن يكون الليل أصلاً فلا. وفي إباحة أسباب الجنابة إلى آخر الليل : إشارة إلى أن الجنابة لا تنافي الصوم فيمن أصبح جنباً. ومن ذلك قوله تعالى: ﴿فَكَفَّرَتُهُ إطعام عشرة مساكين﴾ الآية سياقها لإيجاب نوع من هذه الجملة على سبيل التخيير. وفيه إشارة إلى أن الأصل في جهة الإطعام: الإباحة، والتمليك ملحق به؛ لأن الإطعام فعل متعد، مطاوعه: طَعِمَ يَطْعَمُ وهو الأكل، فالإطعام : جَعْلُه أكلاً كسائر الأفعال، إذا تعدت بزيادة الهمزة: لم يبطل وضعها وحقيقتها ، فإذا لم يكن مطاوعه ملكاً : لم يكن متعديه تمليكاً. وهذا واضح جداً، فمن جعل التمليك أصلاً كان تاركاً حقيقة الكلام، ومعنى إلحاق التمليك به. خلافاً لبعض الناس أن الإباحة جزء من التمليك في التقدير، والتمليك كله؛ لأن حوائج المساكين كثيرة يصلح الطعام لقضاء كل نوع منها، إلا أن الملك سبب لقضائها، فأقيم الملك مقامها، فصار التمليك بمنزلة قضائها كلها باعتبار الخلافة عنها، ومن هذه الحوائج: الأكل، فصار النص واقعاً على الذي هو جزء من هذه الجملة، فاستقام تحديثه إلى الكل الذي هو مشتمل على هذا المنصوص عليه وغيره، فيكون عملاً بالنص بعينه في المعنى. وهذا بخلاف الكسوة؛ لأن النص هناك تناول التمليك؛ لأنه جعل الفعل في الأول كفارةً، وهو الإطعام، وجعل العين في الثاني كفارةً، الثوبُ؛ لأن الكسوة بكسر الكاف: اسمٌ للثوب، وبفتح الكاف: اسمٌ للفعل، فيجب أن تصير العين كفارةً، لا المنفعة. وإنما تصير كذلك بالتمليك، دون الإعارة، فصار النص ها هنا واقعاً على التمليك الذي هو قضاء لكل الحوائج في المعنى، فلم تستقم التعدية إلى ما هو جزء منها، وهو مع ذلك قاصر؛ لأن الإعارة في الثياب منقضيةٌ قبل الكمال، والإباحة في الطعام لازمةٌ لا مردَّ لفعل الأكل فيها، فهما في طرفي نقيض، مع التفاوت الذي بينا. (1) باب وجوه الوقوف على أحكام النّظم قوله : إلا أنا جوزنا تقديم النية على الفجر بالسنَّة: عن حفصة أم المؤمنين أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قال: «مَن لم يبيت الصيام قبل الفجر: فلا صيام له» رواه أصحاب السنن الأربعة، وفي لفظ: لا صيام لمن لم ينو الصيام من الليل، وفي لفظ: يُجمع بالتشديد، وفي لفظ: بالتخفيف.
And in it (the verse) is an indication of the equality of all (things) in prohibition; because He, exalted, said: 'Then complete the fast until the night' meaning refraining from this totality, so the prohibition of all was by one way, so there was no specificity or superiority for intercourse. And in it is an indication that the intention during the day is explicitly stated; because of His saying: 'Then complete the fast until the night' after permitting the totality until the dawn. And the letter 'thumma' is for delay, so the resolve becomes after dawn inevitably; because the night does not end except with a part of the day, except that we permitted the intention before dawn by the Sunnah, but as for the night being the origin, no. And in the permissibility of causes of ritual impurity until the end of the night is an indication that ritual impurity does not contradict fasting for one who becomes in that state in the morning. And from that is His saying: 'Its expiation is feeding ten poor persons.' The verse's context is to obligate one type from this totality on the basis of choice. And in it is an indication that the origin regarding feeding is permission (ibaha), and transfer of ownership (tamleek) is attached to it; because 'feeding' is a transitive verb, its intransitive counterpart is 'ta'ima ya'ta'imu' which is eating, so feeding means making (someone) eat like other verbs, when they become transitive by adding the hamza (causative form), their original meaning and reality are not invalidated. So if its intransitive is not ownership, then its transitive is not making ownership. And this is very clear. So whoever makes transfer of ownership the origin has abandoned the reality of speech and the meaning of attaching transfer of ownership to it. Contrary to some people that permission is part of transfer of ownership in estimation, and transfer of ownership is all of it; because the needs of the poor are many, and food suffices to fulfill every type of them, except that ownership is the cause of fulfilling them, so ownership was set up in its place, so transfer of ownership became like fulfilling all of them by representation from them, and among these needs is eating, so the text occurred on that which is part of this totality, so its extension to the whole which includes this and other things becomes sound, thus it is acting upon the very text in meaning. And this differs from clothing; because the text there addresses transfer of ownership; because He made the act in the first (case) the expiation, which is feeding, and made the entity in the second the expiation, the garment; because 'kiswa' with kasr of the kaf is a noun for the garment, and with fatha of the kaf is a noun for the act. So the entity must become the expiation, not the benefit. And it only becomes so by transfer of ownership, not by lending. So the text here occurred on transfer of ownership which fulfills all needs in meaning, so extension to what is part of it is not sound, and it is deficient; because lending in clothes is terminated before completion, while permission in food is necessary with no turning back of the act of eating in it, so they are at opposite extremes, with the difference we have explained. (1) Chapter on ways of understanding the rulings of texts. His saying: 'Except that we permitted the intention before the dawn by the Sunnah' from Hafsah Umm al-Mu'mineen that the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Whoever does not resolve the fast before dawn, there is no fast for him.' Narrated by the four authors of Sunan. In one wording: 'There is no fast for whoever does not intend the fast from the night.' In another wording: with emphasis (yujamma') and in another with lightening (yukhaffaf).
Source pages p. 298

Commentary
This passage is an advanced textual analysis from Imam al-Bazdawi's usul work, demonstrating how the wording of the Quran reveals legal indications (isharat). The author first takes the verse of fasting (2:187) and shows that the command to complete the fast until night implies equal prohibition of all fast-breaking acts, with no special ruling for intercourse. He then extracts that the intention for fasting is indicated to be during the day, but the Sunnah allows it before dawn. The permissibility of causes of janabah until the end of the night implies that one can be junub in the morning and still fast. The main focus, however, is the analysis of the kaffarah verse (5:89). The author argues that the word 'it'am' (feeding) linguistically means to make someone eat (ibaha), not to transfer ownership (tamleek). He supports this by the verbal pattern: the transitive form with hamza does not change the root meaning—since the intransitive 'ta'ima' means to eat, the transitive 'at'ama' means to cause to eat, not to give ownership. Therefore, feeding the poor in kaffarah is fulfilled by merely giving them food to eat, not requiring them to own it. He contrasts this with kiswa (clothing), where the word itself can mean the garment (noun), so the text requires transfer of ownership of the garment. The author refutes an opposing view (likely Shafi'i) that claims ibaha is encompassed within tamleek, arguing that such extension is valid only here due to the specific nature of food, but not for clothing since lending is temporary. This passage exemplifies the Hanafi method of grounding rulings in linguistic precision and the primary meaning of words.

Key Terms
الإطعام (al-it'am): Feeding, i.e., making someone eat; a transitive verb derived from طَعِمَ (to eat).
التمليك (al-tamleek): Transfer of ownership; making something owned by another.
الإباحة (al-ibaha): Permission to utilize without transfer of ownership.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi analysis in this lesson, why does 'it'am' not imply tamleek?
A) Because the verse only mentions feeding, not ownership.
B) Because the intransitive form 'ta'ima' means to eat, so the transitive 'at'ama' means to cause to eat.
C) Because the word 'it'am' is ambiguous and requires Sunnah to clarify.
D) Because tamleek is required only for kiswa.

Key Takeaway
The correct interpretation of Quranic words must be based on their linguistic realities; for 'it'am', the literal meaning of causing to eat (ibaha) is sufficient in kaffarah.

Dalalah: Extending Rulings Through the Meaning of the Text
Textual analysis of the Ramadan hadith to explain dalalah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish dalalah from ibarah and isharah, and apply the Hanafi method of deriving rulings from the linguistic meaning (ma'na) of the text using the Ramadan example.

Core Idea
Dalalah derives rulings from the text's linguistic meaning, not its explicit words or opinion.

In this lesson, we explore the Hanafi concept of dalalah—a method of legal derivation that lies between explicit textual statement (ibarah) and independent analogy (qiyas). The author uses the hadith of a man who had intercourse during Ramadan to illustrate how the ruling for eating and drinking is extended to intercourse through the shared meaning of 'crime' (breaking the fast).

Guided Path
1
Identify the linguistic implication of 'Ramadan'
Recognize that the questioner used ‘Ramadan’ to refer to the act of breaking the fast, which is a crime. The Prophet's answer addressed the ruling of that crime, not the month itself.
The meaning of ‘Ramadan’ in this context is the crime of eating/drinking, not the month.
2
Extract the underlying meaning (ma'na)
The Prophet's response about eating/drinking established a ruling based on the meaning of ‘crime’ (jinaayah). This meaning is even stronger in intercourse because abstaining is harder.
The same meaning of crime (breaking the fast) is present in both acts.
3
Apply the meaning to the new case
Since intercourse is the instrument of that crime and more severe in criminality, the ruling of expiation (kaffarah) applies by dalalah—not by explicit text or qiyas.
This extension is not ibarah (explicit) but dalalah based on linguistic meaning.
4
Contrast with ibarah and isharah
The author clarifies that this derivation is not ibarah (explicit wording) nor isharah (indirect indication) because it relies on the meaning of the text, not its form or a conceptual link.
Dalalah is a distinct source: derived from the text's linguistic meaning, not external reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اسم رمضان: وقع عن الجناية، والمواقعةُ عينها ليست بجناية، بل هو لفعل واقع على محل مملوك، إلا أن معنى هذا الاسم لغةً من هذا السائل : الفطر الذي هو جنايةٌ، وإنما أجاب رسولُ الله عليه الصلاة والسلام عن حكم الجناية، فكان بناء على معنى الجناية في ذلك الاسم. والمواقعةُ آلة الجناية، فأثبتنا الحكم بذلك المعنى بعينه في الأكل والشرب؛ لأنه فوقه في الجناية ؛ لأن الصبر عنه أشدُّ ، والدعوة إليه أكثر ، ومعنى المناقصة فيه أبلغ، وبذلك المعنى صار اللفظ علة)، فكان أقوى في الجناية، على نحو ما قلنا في الشتم مع التأفيف. فمِن حيث إنه ثابت بمعنى النص لا بظاهره : لم نُسمه عبارةً، ولا إشارة، ومن حيث إنه ثابت بمعنى النص لغةً لا رأياً سميناه دلالة، لا قياساً.
ومن ذلك : أن النص في عُذر الناسي وَرَدَ في الأكل والشرب، وثبت حكمه في الوطء دلالةً ؛ لأن النسيان فعل معلوم بصورته ومعناه، أما صورته فظاهرة، وأما معناه أنه مدفوع إليه خلقةً وطبيعة، فكان ذلك سماوياً محضاً، فأضيف إلى صاحب الحق، فصار عفواً. هذا معنى النسيان لغة، وهو كونه مطبوعاً عليه، فعملنا بهذا المعنى في نظيره.
فإن قيل: هما متفاوتان؛ لأن النسيان يغلب في الأكل والشرب؛ لأن الصومَ يُحوجه إلى ذلك، ولا يُحوجه إلى المواقعة، بل يُضعفه عنها، فصار كالنسيان في الصلاة لم يُجعل عذراً؛ لأنه نادر.
قلنا : للأكل والشرب مزية في أسباب الدعوة ، وفيه قصور في حاله؛ لأنه لا يغلب البشر، وأما المواقعة فقاصرة في أسباب الدعوة ولكنها كاملة في حالها، لأن هذه الشهوة تغلب البشر ، فصارا سواء، فصح الاستدلال.
ومن ذلك أن النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام قال: «لا قَوَدَ إلا بالسيف» : وأراد به الضرب بالسيف، ولهذا الفعل معنى مقصود، وهو
The name Ramadan: It occurred regarding the crime [of breaking the fast], and the act of intercourse itself is not a crime, but it is an act occurring upon a possessed place. However, the linguistic meaning of this name from this questioner is the breaking of the fast which is a crime. And the Messenger of God, peace be upon him, only answered regarding the ruling of the crime, so it was based on the meaning of crime in that name. And intercourse is the instrument of the crime, so we established the ruling by that same meaning in eating and drinking, because it is above it in criminality; since refraining from it is more severe, the inclination towards it is greater, and the meaning of deficiency in it is more pronounced. And by that meaning the word became the cause, so it was stronger in criminality, similar to what we said about insult with 'uff'. So from the perspective that it is established by the meaning of the text not by its apparent wording, we did not call it 'ibarah nor isharah, and from the perspective that it is established by the linguistic meaning of the text not by opinion, we called it dalalah, not qiyas.
And from that: the text regarding the excuse of the forgetful person came regarding eating and drinking, and its ruling was established for intercourse by dalalah, because forgetfulness is an act known by its form and meaning. As for its form, it is apparent; as for its meaning, it is that it is driven to him by creation and nature, so it is purely celestial, so it was attributed to the owner of the right, so it became waived. This is the linguistic meaning of forgetfulness, i.e., being naturally inclined to it, so we acted by this meaning in its counterpart.
If it is said: They are different, because forgetfulness is dominant in eating and drinking, because fasting necessitates that, but it does not necessitate intercourse; rather it weakens one from it, so it becomes like forgetfulness in prayer which is not made an excuse because it is rare.
We say: eating and drinking have an advantage in the causes of inclination, and there is a deficiency in their state because they do not overcome the human; as for intercourse, it is deficient in the causes of inclination but complete in its state, because this desire overcomes the human, so they become equal, so the inference is sound.
And from that: the Prophet, peace be upon him, said: "No retaliation except with the sword." He meant striking with the sword, and this act has an intended meaning, and it is...
Source pages p. 302

Commentary
This passage is a usul al-fiqh analysis of the concept of dalalah (implication) as a source of legal derivation, distinct from ibarah (explicit statement) and isharah (indication). The author uses three examples to illustrate how rulings are extended through linguistic meaning (ma'na) rather than by personal opinion or qiyas (analogy).

The first example: the hadith of the man who had intercourse during Ramadan. The inquirer used the word 'Ramadan' which linguistically implies the act of breaking the fast (a crime). The Prophet's response about expiation for eating and drinking is thus applied to intercourse because the 'meaning of crime' is present in both, and intercourse is even more severe as an act of breaking the fast. This extension is not through ibarah (the text's explicit words) nor isharah (indication), but through dalalah – a linguistic inference from the meaning of the word.

The second example: the hadith about the forgetful person who eats or drinks while fasting. The text explicitly excuses him, and the ruling is extended to intercourse by dalalah because the underlying meaning of forgetfulness (being naturally driven, not intentional) is present in both acts. The author addresses an objection: forgetfulness is more common in eating and drinking, but replies that while eating is more frequent, intercourse is more powerful in dominating human nature, so both are comparable.

The third example (incomplete): the hadith 'No retaliation except with the sword.' The author begins to analyze that the intended meaning is killing by a sword stroke, but the actual purpose is taking life – an extension through ma'na.

Throughout, the author distinguishes dalalah from qiyas: dalalah is based on the linguistic meaning of the text itself, while qiyas is an independent rational analogy. This demonstrates the Hanafi usul method of deriving rulings from texts through deep linguistic analysis.

Key Terms
دلالة (dalalah): إثبات الحكم بمعنى النص لغة دون الرأي
عبارة (ibarah): الحكم المأخوذ من ظاهر اللفظ مباشرة
إشارة (isharah): الحكم المأخوذ من مفهوم اللفظ غير المباشر
معنى (ma'na): المعنى اللغوي أو الشرعي الذي يكون علة الحكم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the author classify the derivation of the ruling for intercourse in Ramadan as 'dalalah' rather than 'qiyas'?
A) Because the ruling for eating/drinking is explicitly mentioned and intercourse is analogized to it.
B) Because the meaning of 'crime' is linguistically present in the word 'Ramadan' and extends to intercourse.
C) Because the Prophet (peace be upon him) directly stated the ruling for intercourse.
D) Because the Companions reached a consensus on this issue.

Key Takeaway
Dalalah is a valid Hanafi method of extending rulings through the linguistic meaning of the text, distinct from explicit wording and independent analogy.

Dalalah vs. Qiyas: The Case of Forgetfulness During Fasting
Explaining dalalah via the forgetfulness hadith, including an objection and response

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school extends the excuse of forgetfulness from eating/drinking to intercourse via dalalah, and evaluate the objection based on frequency.

Core Idea
The meaning of forgetfulness as a natural inclination applies equally to intercourse, making it excused via dalalah.

In this lesson, we examine how the Hanafi usul uses dalalah to extend the ruling of forgetfulness from eating/drinking to intercourse. The author also addresses an important objection: that forgetfulness is more common in eating/drinking. We will see how the author's response reveals the depth of the Hanafi method in balancing textual meaning with practical realities.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original text and its ruling
The hadith 'Whoever forgets and eats or drinks, let him complete his fast, for Allah fed him and gave him drink' establishes that forgetful eating/drinking does not invalidate the fast.
This is the explicit ruling (ibarah) for eating/drinking only.
2
Extract the linguistic meaning of forgetfulness
The author states that the meaning of forgetfulness (al-nisyan) is being 'naturally driven' (matbu' 'alayh) and purely celestial, so the action is attributed to Allah and forgiven.
This meaning is derived from the phrase 'for Allah fed him' — the action is effectively waived.
3
Notice the objection
A hypothetical objector argues: forgetfulness is more dominant in eating/drinking because fasting increases the need for them, but it does not increase the need for intercourse; therefore, the excuse should not apply to intercourse.
The objection highlights a difference in frequency, not in the nature of forgetfulness.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The author responds by comparing both acts: eating/drinking have stronger causes (reasons for inclination) but are weaker in state (they do not overcome humans), while intercourse has weaker causes but is stronger in state (it overcomes human nature). They balance out, making both equal in the meaning of forgetfulness.
Thus, the meaning of forgetfulness as 'natural inclination' is present in both, so the ruling extends by dalalah.
5
Conclude the usul lesson
This example shows that dalalah considers the underlying meaning (ma'na) across cases, even when superficial differences exist. It is not a simple qiyas but a linguistic-legal inference.
The objection is refuted without abandoning the text, demonstrating the strength of the Hanafi method.

Qiyas and Hadd: The Dispute on Liwat and Zina
Refuting the analogy of sodomy to adultery for hadd

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the validity of qiyas between liwat and zina by identifying the complete (kamil) and deficient (qasir) causes, and apply the Hanafi principle that hudud are repelled by doubts (shubuhat).

Core Idea
A deficient cause cannot justify analogy in hudud: the complete must be the origin.

This passage tackles a critical usul question: can the fixed punishment (hadd) for zina be applied to liwat by analogy? Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani argue yes, based on a shared linguistic meaning. But the author, representing Abu Hanifa, systematically refutes this, emphasizing that in hudud, only a fully complete effective cause ('illa kāmila) can serve as the basis for extension. You will learn to dissect this dispute and understand how the principle of repelling punishments by doubts (shubuhat) operates in Hanafi legal theory.

Guided Path
1
State the two opposing positions
Identify who argues that the hadd of zina applies to liwat (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) and who rejects it (Abu Hanifa). Note that this is an intra-Hanafi dispute.
2
Extract the claimed 'illa (effective cause) of zina
According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, the meaning of zina is 'satisfaction of desire by emission of water (safḥ al-mā') in a prohibited, desired place.' They claim this same 'illa exists in liwat and is even stronger in prohibition.
3
Identify why Abu Hanifa considers this 'illa deficient (qāṣir)
The author lists two deficiencies: (1) zina destroys lineage (the child is legally lost), while liwat does not; (2) zina involves mutual desire from both parties, whereas liwat typically involves desire only from the active partner, with nature preventing the passive partner's desire.
Also notes that emission can be avoided by coitus interruptus in zina, but this is not the point.
4
Apply the Hanafi principle for hudud: 'complete is the origin, deficient cannot be analogized to it'
The author argues that in hudud, which are repelled by doubts, one cannot analogize from a complete case (zina) to a deficient one (liwat) because the deficiency creates a doubt.
Also refutes the idea that mere prohibition (like drinking urine) is enough for hadd.
5
Conclude the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference)
Abu Hanifa does not impose the hadd of zina for liwat; instead, a ta'zīr (discretionary punishment) applies. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad would impose the full hadd of zina.
This shows how a usul principle directly affects fiqh outcomes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمِنْ ذَلِكَ أَيْضًا أَنَّ أَبَا يُوسُفَ وَمُحَمَّدًا رَحِمَهُمَا اللهُ أَوْجَبَا حَدَّ الزِّنَا بِاللِّوَاطَةِ بِدَلَالَةِ النَّصِّ; لِأَنَّ الزِّنَا: اسْمٌ لِفِعْلٍ مَعْلُومٍ، وَمَعْنَاهُ مَعْلُومٌ، وَهُوَ قَضَاءُ الشَّهْوَةِ بِسَفْحِ الْمَاءِ فِي مَحَلٍّ مُحَرَّمٍ مُشْتَهًى، وَهَذَا الْمَعْنَى بِعَيْنِهِ مَوْجُودٌ فِي اللِّوَاطَةِ وَزِيَادَةٌ; لِأَنَّهُ فِي الْحُرْمَةِ فَوْقَهُ، وَفِي سَفْحِ الْمَاءِ فَوْقَهُ، وَفِي الشَّهْوَةِ مِثْلُهُ، وَهَذَا مَعْنَى الزِّنَا لُغَةً. وَالْجَوَابُ عَنْ هَذَا: أَنَّ الْكَامِلَ أَصْلٌ فِي كُلِّ بَابٍ، خُصُوصًا فِي الْحُدُودِ، وَالْكَامِلُ فِي سَفْحِ الْمَاءِ: مَا يُهْلِكُ الْبَشَرَ حُكْمًا، وَهُوَ الزِّنَا; لِأَنَّ وَلَدَ الزِّنَا هَالِكٌ حُكْمًا; لِعَدَمِ مَنْ يَقُومُ بِمَصَالِحِهِ، فَأَمَّا تَضْيِيعُ الْمَاءِ: فَقَاصِرٌ; لِأَنَّهُ قَدْ يَحِلُّ بِالْعَزْلِ، وَلَا يُفْسِدُ الْفِرَاشَ، وَكَذَلِكَ الزِّنَا كَامِلٌ بِحَالِهِ; لِأَنَّهُ غَالِبُ الْوُجُودِ بِالشَّهْوَةِ الدَّاعِيَةِ مِنَ الطَّرَفَيْنِ، وَأَمَّا هَذَا الْفِعْلُ فَقَاصِرٌ بِحَالِهِ; لِأَنَّ الدَّاعِيَ إِلَيْهِ شَهْوَةُ الْفَاعِلِ فَأَمَّا صَاحِبُهُ فَلَيْسَ فِي طَبْعِهِ دَاعٍ إِلَيْهِ، بَلِ الطَّبْعُ مَانِعٌ، فَفَسَدَ الِاسْتِدْلَالُ بِالْكَامِلِ عَلَى الْقَاصِرِ فِي حُكْمٍ يُدْرَأُ بِالشُّبُهَاتِ. وَالتَّرْجِيحُ بِالْحُرْمَةِ: بَاطِلٌ; لِأَنَّ الْحُرْمَةَ الْمُجَرَّدَةَ بِدُونِ هَذِهِ الْمَعَانِي: غَيْرُ مُعْتَبَرَةٍ لِإِيجَابِ الْحَدِّ، أَلَا يُرَى أَنَّ شُرْبَ الْبَوْلِ: لَا يُوجِبُ الْحَدَّ كَمَالِ الْحُرْمَةِ.
And from that also is that Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, may Allah have mercy on them, obligated the hadd of zina for sodomy by indication of the text, because zina is a name for a known act and its meaning is known, and it is the satisfaction of desire by the emission of water in a prohibited, desired place. And this same meaning exists in sodomy and more, because in prohibition it is above it, and in emission of water it is above it, and in desire it is like it. And this is the linguistic meaning of zina. The response to this is that the complete is the origin in every chapter, especially in hadd punishments, and the complete in emission of water is that which destroys humans legally, and that is zina, because the child of zina is legally destroyed due to the lack of one who manages its interests. As for wasting water, it is deficient, because it may be permissible by coitus interruptus, and it does not spoil the bed. Likewise, zina is complete in its state because its existence is mostly due to desire from both sides. As for this act, it is deficient in its state because the motive for it is the desire of the doer, while for the other person, there is no innate motive, rather nature prevents it. So the deduction from the complete upon the deficient in a ruling that is repelled by doubts is corrupted. And weighting by prohibition is invalid, because mere prohibition without these meanings is not considered for the obligation of hadd. Do you not see that drinking urine does not obligate hadd despite the completeness of its prohibition.
Source pages p. 305

Commentary
This passage is a usul dispute about the validity of qiyas (analogy) in criminal law. The author presents the position of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, who argued that the hadd punishment for zina (adultery) should apply to liwat (sodomy) by analogy. They reasoned that the linguistic definition of zina—'satisfying desire by emission of water in a prohibited, desired place'—applies even more strongly to liwat, as the act is more prohibited and also involves emission. The author (representing Abu Hanifa's view) refutes this with several arguments: First, qiyas requires the cause ('illa) to be complete (kamil), and in hudud (fixed punishments), only the complete form of the act justifies the penalty. For zina, the complete cause includes the destruction of lineage (the child is legally lost) and mutual natural desire; both are absent in liwat. Liwat is deficient (qasir) in these aspects. Second, the authority of the text (nass) for zina cannot be extended to a deficient case, especially when the principle is that hudud are repelled by doubts (shubuhat). Third, mere prohibition does not make a valid cause for hadd; otherwise, drinking urine would also incur hadd. Thus, the analogy fails.

Key Terms
اللواطة (al-liwāṭah): Sodomy, the act of anal intercourse between males.
الزنا (al-zinā): Unlawful sexual intercourse between a man and a woman not married to each other.
كامل (kāmil): Complete; refers to the full realization of the effective cause ('illa), necessary for qiyas.
قاصر (qāṣir): Deficient; refers to a cause that lacks full effect, thus not suitable for qiyas.
الحدود تدرأ بالشبهات (al-ḥudūd tudra' bi al-shubuhāt): Fixed punishments are repelled by doubts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi rebuttal in this passage, why does the analogy between liwat and zina fail?
A) Because liwat involves a different kind of prohibition.
B) Because liwat lacks the complete cause of zina, such as the destruction of lineage and mutual desire.
C) Because the punishment for liwat is already determined by consensus.
D) Because zina is defined by vaginal intercourse only.

Key Takeaway
The hadd of zina cannot be extended by analogy to liwat because the effective cause is deficient, and hudud are repelled by doubts.

Al-Muqtada (Implied Meaning): Definition and Ruling on Generality
Defines iqtida' al-nass and presents the Hanafi-Shafi'i dispute on its generality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define al-muqtada, distinguish it from qiyas, and state the Hanafi position that it has no generality.

Core Idea
The implied meaning (muqtada) is an indispensable addition to the text, treated as part of the text — not analogy — and the Hanafis hold it has no generality.

In Usul al-Fiqh, understanding how the text communicates beyond its literal wording is essential. One such mode is iqtida' al-nass (textual implication), which supplies a condition necessary for the validity of the stated ruling. This lesson defines this concept, explains its force, and introduces the seminal debate between Hanafis and Shafi'is on whether such implied meanings carry generality.

Guided Path
1
Define al-muqtada
Identify al-muqtada as an addition to the text that is indispensable (lā yastaghni 'anhu) for the validity of the ruling. It is not derived by qiyas but considered a direct implication of the text.
The text says: 'زيادة على النص، ثبت شرطاً لصحة المنصوص لما لم يَستغن عنه'
2
Understand its force: like text, not analogy
Note that what is established by muqtada is treated as if it were established by the wording itself. It cannot be opposed by qiyas.
The text says: 'دون القياس، حتى إن القياس لا يعارض شيئاً من هذه الأقسام'
3
Learn the Hanafi position on generality
Our scholars (Hanafis) hold that al-muqtada has no generality (lā 'umūma lahu). The implied condition applies only to the specific instance mentioned in the text.
The text says: 'فقال أصحابنا رحمهم الله : لا عموم له'
4
Learn the Shafi'i position
Al-Shafi'i argued that since al-muqtada is established by the text, it should have the same generality as the text itself.
The text says: 'وقال الشافعي رحمه الله فيه بالعموم'
5
Apply the thamarah al-khilaf
Consider the practical consequence: In a purchase contract, the muqtada is that the buyer must own the item. For Hanafis, this condition applies only to that specific contract; for Shafi'is, it applies to all similar contracts unless specified otherwise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المقتضى فزيادة على النص، ثبت شرطاً لصحة المنصوص لما لم يَستغن عنه : وَجَبَ تقديمه ؛ لتصحيح المنصوص عليه، فقد اقتضاه النص، فصار المقتضى بحكمه حكماً للنص. عليه بمنزلة الشراء أوجب الملك، والملك أوجب العتق في القريب، فصار الملك بحكمه حكماً للشراء، فصار الثابت به بمنزلة الثابت بنفس النظم، دون القياس، حتى إن القياس لا يعارض شيئاً من هذه الأقسام، والثابت بهذا يعدل الثابت بالنص، إلا عند المعارضة به. واختلفوا في هذا القسم: فقال أصحابنا رحمهم الله : لا عموم له. وقال الشافعي رحمه الله فيه بالعموم ؛ لأنه ثابت بالنص، فكان مثله.
As for the iqtida' (implied meaning), it is an addition to the text, established as a condition for the validity of what is stated (al-mansus), because it is indispensable: it must be given precedence in order to correct the stated ruling. So the text requires it, and thus the implied meaning, by its ruling, becomes a ruling of the text. It is like a purchase that necessitates ownership, and ownership necessitates emancipation of a close relative. So ownership, by its ruling, becomes a ruling of purchase, and what is established by it (iqtida') is like what is established by the wording itself, not by analogy. So much so that analogy cannot oppose any of these categories, and what is established by this equals what is established by the text, except when there is opposition. And they differed regarding this category: Our scholars (the Hanafis), may Allah have mercy on them, said: it has no generality ('umum). Al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, said that it has generality, because it is established by the text, so it is like it.
Source pages p. 307

Commentary
This passage is a definition of the usul principle 'al-muqtada' (the implied meaning), which is a type of textual implication (iqtida' al-nass) where the text requires something beyond its literal wording as a necessary condition for the validity of the ruling. The text gives an example: the purchase contract necessitates ownership, and ownership necessitates emancipation of a close relative if purchased. The ruling of the implied meaning is treated like the ruling of the text itself, not based on analogy (qiyas). However, there is a dispute between the Hanafis and al-Shafi'i regarding whether such implied rulings carry generality ('umum). The Hanafis say they do not have generality, meaning the implied condition applies only to the specific case mentioned in the text and cannot be extended analogically. Al-Shafi'i argues that since it is established by the text, it should be treated like the text and thus have generality. The passage ends with this dispute.

Key Terms
المقتضى (al-muqtada): The implied meaning that is an indispensable addition to the text, necessary for the validity of the stated ruling.
القياس (al-qiyas): Analogical reasoning; muqtada is not derived by qiyas but is considered part of the text.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school in this passage, what is the status of the implied meaning (al-muqtada) regarding generality?
A) It has full generality like the explicit text
B) It has no generality (lā 'umūma lahu)
C) It has generality only if it is a condition
D) It is always general unless specified

Key Takeaway
Muqtada is an indispensable textual implication with the force of the text, but Hanafis restrict its application to the specific context — it does not carry generality.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate
6 min
Muqtada al-Nass: Definition and Ruling on Its Generality
Defining muqtada al-nass and debating its generality

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define muqtada al-nass, distinguish it from other textual indications, and apply the Hanafi rule that it has no generality while evaluating the Shafi'i counterargument.

Core Idea
Muqtada al-nass is treated like the text for validity but has no generality because it lacks wording.

In this passage from al-Bazdawi's Usul, we encounter the concept of muqtada al-nass—an implicit requirement that must exist for the explicit ruling to be valid. For example, when someone says 'Free your slave for me for a thousand dirhams,' the sale of the slave is not mentioned but is necessarily implied. The Hanafis hold that such implied requirements are binding in the specific case but do not extend to other similar cases (no generality), while al-Shafi'i argues they should be general. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying texts in istinbat.

Guided Path
1
Define muqtada al-nass
Identify what muqtada is: an addition to the text necessary for the validity of the explicitly stated ruling. It is a condition that must be present for the text to be operative.
Example: 'Free your slave for me for 1000 dirhams' implies a sale, which is the muqtada.
2
Understand its legal status
Muqtada is given the same binding force as the text for the specific case, but it is not established by its own wording. It is treated as if it were part of the text only for the purpose of validating the explicit ruling.
It is not like analogy (qiyas); it is stronger than qiyas but weaker than explicit text in terms of generality.
3
Contrast with other textual indications
Distinguish muqtada from isharat al-nass (indication of the text) and the plain text itself. Isharat al-nass is explicitly indicated by the wording, while muqtada is implied as a necessity.
Refer to the earlier part of the passage on isharat al-nass for contrast.
4
Analyze the Hanafi vs. Shafi'i dispute
State each position: Hanafis say no generality because generality is a property of wording; Shafi'is say it has generality because it is established by the text. Then examine the Hanafi proof and response.
The key difference is whether the implicit requirement carries the same linguistic generality as the explicit text.
5
Apply the principle to a case
Work through the example of 'Free your slave for 1000 dirhams' and the sub-dispute between Abu Yusuf and Abu Hanifa/Muhammad regarding delivery in a gift. See how the muqtada affects conditions.
This shows the practical thamarah al-khilaf of the generality dispute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفيه إشارة النص، وهو قوله تعالى : النَّفْسَ بِالنَّفْسِ ) المائدة / ٤٥ ، فأما الفعل فعمد محض خالص لا تردُّدَ فيه، والكفارة جزاء للفعل المحض. وفي مسألة الحَجَر : الشبهة في نفس الفعل، فعمت القود والكفارة. ولهذا قلنا: إن سجود السهو لا يجب بالعمد، ولا يصلح أن يكون السهو دليلاً على العمد ؛ لما قلنا، خلافاً للشافعي أيضاً. وقلنا نحن إن كفارة الفطر وجبت على الرجل بالمواقعة نصاً، ومعنى الفطر فيه معقول لغةً، فوجبت به الكفارة على المرأة أيضاً، استدلالاً به.
[اقتضاء النص :]
الله
19
-٤- وأما المقتضى فزيادة على النص، ثبت شرطاً لصحة المنصوص لما لم يَستغن عنه : وَجَبَ تقديمه ؛ لتصحيح المنصوص عليه، فقد اقتضاه النص، فصار المقتضى بحكمه حكماً للنص.
عليه
بمنزلة الشراء أوجب الملك، والملك أوجب العتق في القريب، فصار الملك بحكمه حكماً للشراء، فصار الثابت :به بمنزلة الثابت بنفس النظم، دون القياس، حتى إن القياس لا يعارض شيئاً من هذه الأقسام، والثابت بهذا : يعدل الثابت بالنص، إلا عند المعارضة به.
واختلفوا في هذا القسم:
فقال أصحابنا رحمهم الله : لا عموم له.
وقال الشافعي رحمه الله فيه بالعموم ؛ لأنه ثابت بالنص، فكان مثله.
وقال أصحابنا رحمهم الله : لا عموم له.
وقال الشافعي رحمه الله فيه بالعموم ؛ لأنه ثابت بالنص، فكان مثله.
وقلنا نحن إن العموم من صفات النظم والصيغة، وهذا أمرٌ لا نَظْم له لكنا أنزلناه منظوماً شرطاً لغيره، فيبقى على أصله فيما وراء صحة المذكور.
ومثال هذا الأصل: أعتق عبدك عني بألف درهم: أنه يتضمن البيع مقتضى العتق وشرطاً له، حتى يثبت بشروط العتق لما كان تابعاً له، ولو جعل بمنزلة المذكور كما قال الخصم: لثبت بشروط نفسه.
ولهذا قال أبو يوسف رحمه الله : إنه لو قال: أعتق عبدك عني بغير شيء: إنه يصح عن الأمر، و مر ، ويثبت الملك بالهبة من غير قبض ؛ لأنه ثابت مقتضى بالعتق فيثبت بشروطه، فيُستغنى عن التسليم، كما استغنى البيع عن القبول، وهو ركن فيه، فالاستغناء عن القبض وهو شرط: أولى. وهذا كما لو قال : أعتق عبدك هذا عني بألف درهم ورِطلٍ من خمر إنه يصح، ويعتق عنه وإن لم يوجد التسليم والبيع الفاسد مثل الهبة؛ لما قلنا.
وقال أبو حنيفة ومحمد رحمهما الله : يقع العتق عن المأمور؛ لأن القبض والتسليم بحكم الهبة لم يوجد ؛ لأن رقبة العبد بحكم العتق تتلف على ملك المولى في يد نفسه، وذلك غير مقبوض للطالب، ولا للعبد، ولا هو محتمل له.
وقوله : إن القبض يسقط : باطل ؛ لأن ثبوت المقتضى بهذا الطريق أمر مشروع، وإنما يسقط به ما يحتمل السقوط، والقبض والتسليم في الهبة شرط لا يحتمل السقوط بحال، ودليل السقوط يعمل في محله، وأما القبول في البيع فيحتمل السقوط ألا يُرى أن الكل يحتمل السقوط
And in it is an indication of the text, which is His statement the Exalted: 'The soul for the soul' (Al-Ma'idah 5:45). As for the act, it is pure, clear intent with no hesitation, and the expiation is a recompense for the pure act. And in the matter of the stone: the ambiguity is in the act itself, so it includes both retaliation and expiation. For this we said: prostration of forgetfulness is not obligatory by intent, nor is forgetfulness suitable as evidence for intent; contrary to al-Shafi'i as well. And we said: The expiation for breaking fast became obligatory upon the man by intercourse as a text, and the meaning of breaking fast is understood linguistically, so expiation became obligatory upon the woman by it as well, by inference.
[Requirement of the Text:]
God
19
-4- As for the requirement (muqtada), it is an addition to the text, established as a condition for the validity of the explicitly stated ruling, because it is not dispensable: it must be given precedence to validate the explicitly stated ruling. Thus the text requires it, so the requirement becomes, by its ruling, a ruling of the text.
It is like purchase necessitating ownership, and ownership necessitating manumission of a close relative. Thus ownership becomes, by its ruling, a ruling of purchase. So what is established by it is like what is established by the very wording, not analogy. For analogy does not oppose any of these categories, and what is established by this equals what is established by the text, except when there is opposition.
And they differed concerning this category:
Our scholars (may God have mercy on them) said: it has no generality.
Al-Shafi'i (may God have mercy on him) held that it has generality because it is established by the text, so it is like it.
And we said: Generality is a quality of wording and form, and this (requirement) has no wording, but we have treated it as if it were worded as a condition for something else. So it remains on its original state beyond the validity of the mentioned.
An example of this principle: 'Free your slave for me for one thousand dirhams' implies a sale as a requirement and condition for manumission, so it is established by the conditions of manumission because it is subordinate to it. If it were made like the mentioned (text) as the opponent said, it would be established by its own conditions.
For this, Abu Yusuf (may God have mercy on him) said: If one says, 'Free your slave for me for nothing,' it is valid by command, and ownership is established by gift without delivery, because it is established by requirement through manumission, so it is established by its conditions. Thus delivery is dispensed with, just as purchase is dispensed with acceptance, which is a pillar in it. So dispensing with delivery, which is a condition, is more appropriate. Likewise, if one says: 'Free your slave for me for one thousand dirhams and a pound of wine,' it is valid, and the slave is freed, even if delivery and the invalid sale are not found, like a gift; due to what we said.
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (may God have mercy on them) said: The manumission occurs for the commanded person, because delivery and taking possession under the gift have not occurred, since the slave's neck, by virtue of manumission, perishes in the possession of the master in his own hand, which is not taken possession of for the claimant, nor for the slave, nor is it capable of it.
And his statement that delivery is dropped is false, because establishing the requirement in this way is a legislated matter; only that which is capable of being dropped is dropped. Delivery and taking possession in a gift are conditions that are not capable of being dropped at all. The evidence for dropping applies in its place. As for acceptance in sale, it is capable of being dropped — do you not see that the whole is capable of being dropped?
Source pages p. 307

Commentary
This passage from al-Bazdawi's Usul defines and discusses the concept of 'muqtada al-nass' (the requirement of the text). The passage begins by referring to a previous discussion about indications in the text (isharat al-nass), citing Quran 5:45. It then distinguishes between different types of textual indications: the explicit text (nass), the indication (ishara), and the requirement (muqtada). The author defines muqtada as an addition to the text that is necessary for the validity of the explicitly stated ruling. For example, when the text says 'Free your slave for me for a thousand dirhams,' the requirement (muqtada) is that a sale must have occurred, because manumission requires ownership, and ownership is acquired through sale. Thus the sale is a condition for the manumission to be valid. This requirement, though not explicitly stated, is treated as if it were part of the text, but it does not have generality (la 'umum lahu) according to the Hanafis. Al-Shafi'i, however, argues that it does have generality because it is established by the text. The Hanafis counter that generality is a property of explicit wording (nazm wa sigha), and the muqtada has no wording of its own; it is only given the status of wording for the specific purpose of validating the text. Therefore, it remains restricted to the specific case. The passage provides examples, including a dispute between Abu Yusuf and Abu Hanifa/Muhammad about whether delivery (qabd) is required in a gift that is implied as a requirement for manumission. Abu Yusuf holds that since the muqtada is established by manumission, its conditions (like delivery) are dropped, similar to how acceptance is dropped in a sale. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad disagree, arguing that delivery cannot be dropped because it is not capable of being dropped. This illustrates the practical implications of the theoretical difference.

Key Terms
المقتضى (al-muqtadā): زيادة على النص ثبتت شرطاً لصحة المنصوص، حكمها كحكم النص في الثبوت لكن لا عموم لها
النظم (al-nazm): الصيغة اللفظية للنص، وهي شرط لثبوت العموم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
ما حكم المقتضى عند الحنفية من حيث العموم؟
A) له عموم مطلق
B) لا عموم له
C) عمومه ظني
D) يعم في الأحكام دون الأسباب

Key Takeaway
Muqtada al-nass is binding as a condition for the text but lacks generality because it is not expressed in wording.

The Core Distinction: Iqtida' vs. Hadhf Ikhtisaran
Distinguishing iqtida' and hadhf with Quran and Hadith

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between iqtida' (necessary implication) and hadhf ikhtisaran (deletion for brevity) and apply the Hanafi rule that iqtida' does not create generality.

Core Idea
Iqtida' is a legal necessity limited to the essential; hadhf ikhtisaran is a linguistic abbreviation retaining generality.

In usul al-fiqh, not every implied meaning is equal. The Hanafi school makes a critical distinction between what is necessarily implied for a ruling to be valid (iqtida') and what is omitted for brevity in speech (hadhf ikhtisaran). This lesson unpacks that distinction using Quranic evidence and a key hadith, showing how each operates differently in legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Define iqtida' and hadhf ikhtisaran
Start by understanding the two concepts: iqtida' is a legal necessity where an omitted element is required for the ruling's validity; hadhf ikhtisaran is a linguistic abbreviation where the omitted word is understood from common usage.
Iqtida' does not extend beyond the necessary; hadhf retains the generality of the omitted term.
2
Analyze the Quranic example: 'Ask the town'
Consider verse 12:82. The literal meaning is impossible (you cannot ask a town), so 'the people' is omitted. This deletion is for brevity, not necessity. When you assume 'people', the attribution changes from the town to the people.
This is hadhf ikhtisaran – linguistic and retains generality.
3
Analyze the hadith: 'My community is forgiven for error and forgetfulness'
The apparent meaning is impossible (no community is free from error/forgetfulness). Therefore, a ruling (e.g., sin punishment) is implied as omitted. This implication is necessary for the statement to be valid – it is iqtida', a legal necessity.
Iqtida' does not create generality; it is limited to what is essential.
4
Compare the outcomes when the omitted word is assumed
In the Quran example, assuming 'people' transfers attribution – the text's meaning changes. In the hadith, assuming 'sin' does not change attribution; it merely clarifies the ruling. This shows the difference: hadhf transfers, iqtida' does not.
The test: does bringing in the omitted word shift the reference?
5
State the usul rule regarding generality
Hadhf ikhtisaran retains the generality of the omitted term because it is a linguistic method. Iqtida' is a legal necessity that does not imply generality; it is restricted to what is required for validity.
This principle is applied in later examples.

Source Text

And what is omitted by way of abbreviation and is established linguistically is general, without disagreement; because abbreviation is one of the linguistic methods. As for implication, it is a necessary legal matter like the permissibility of carrion due to necessity, and it does not exceed that. Source pages p. 309

Commentary
This passage is a detailed usuli discussion distinguishing two types of implied meaning: iqtida' (necessary implication) and hadhf ikhtisaran (deletion for brevity). The author begins with fiqh examples to illustrate how implication works in contracts and divorce. For instance, in a sale where the buyer cuts the cloth without verbal acceptance, the contract is valid because the cutting implies acceptance. Similarly, when a husband tells his wife 'Observe the waiting period' after consummation, if he intends divorce, divorce occurs because the command to observe waiting period implies a preceding divorce. This is a necessary implication (iqtida') derived from the legal context, not from linguistic generality. The author then gives examples where intention does not restrict the literal meaning: if someone swears 'If I eat, my slave is free' but intends only a specific food, his intention is not accepted because the word 'eat' is general and his intention to particularize is not given effect. This contrasts with the Shafi'i school, as mentioned. The key theoretical point is that iqtida' is a legal necessity that does not create generality; it is limited to what is necessary for the ruling to be valid. In contrast, hadhf ikhtisaran (deletion for brevity) is a linguistic phenomenon where a word is omitted but understood from context, and it retains the generality of the omitted term. For example, the Quranic verse 'Ask the town' means 'ask the people of the town' — 'people' is omitted for brevity, but when assumed, it does change the reference. Similarly, the hadith 'My community is forgiven for error and forgetfulness' requires assuming a missing element (like 'the sin of') because the literal meaning is impossible. But this assumption is by iqtida', a legal necessity, not by linguistic deletion. In contrast, the hadith 'Actions are but by intentions' maintains its generality because the omitted element (like 'the reward of') is from homonymous nouns and is omitted by abbreviation, not by iqtida'. This distinction matters for divorce: if a man says 'You are divorced' intending three, his intention is invalid because the divorce is implied as necessary (one divorce) from the context, not three. The implied divorce is a necessity and does not carry generality. The author emphasizes that iqtida' is a legal tool akin to necessity (darura), and does not expand by analogy.

Key Terms
المقتضى (al-muqtaḍā): That which is implied necessarily from the wording for the validity of the ruling, without being explicitly mentioned.
المحذوف على وجه الاختصار (al-maḥdhūf ʿalā wajh al-ikhtiṣār): A word omitted from speech but understood from common linguistic usage for brevity; when assumed, it changes the attribution of the statement.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when the hadith 'رفع عن أمتي الخطأ والنسيان' is interpreted, what is the nature of the omission?
A) It is deletion for brevity (hadhf ikhtisaran) and retains generality.
B) It is necessary implication (iqtida') and does not create generality.
C) It is an example of takhsis (specification) of a general term.
D) It is an example of nasikh (abrogation).

Key Takeaway
Iqtida' is a legal necessity limited to the essential; hadhf ikhtisaran is a linguistic abbreviation that retains generality – never confuse the two.

Applying the Distinction: Oaths, Divorce, and the Shafi'i Dispute
Applying iqtida'/hadhf distinction to oaths and divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that iqtida' does not permit specification of general terms in oaths and evaluate the Shafi'i counterposition.

Core Idea
In oaths, intention to particularize is not accepted because the general term is binding under iqtida' and hadhf rules.

The distinction between iqtida' and hadhf is not just theoretical; it has direct practical consequences in oaths, divorces, and contracts. This lesson focuses on the examples given in the text where a person makes an oath using a general term but intends a specific meaning. The Hanafi school rejects such intention, and we examine the reasoning and the differing view of Imam al-Shafi'i.

Guided Path
1
List the oath examples from the text
The text gives six examples: (1) 'If I eat, my slave is free' intending specific food; (2) 'If I drink' intending specific drink; (3) 'If I go out' intending a specific place; (4) 'If I perform a ritual bath' intending specific causes; (5) 'If I perform a ritual bath tonight in this house' intending a specific doer; (6) 'If I perform a ritual bath (general).' Note the last one is different.
The text says 'لم يصدق عندنا' for the first five with subjective intention.
2
State the Hanafi position for each
In all cases where the swearer intends to restrict the general word (e.g., 'eat' to a specific food), his intention is not accepted. The general term applies as it is.
The reasoning is 'لما قلنا' – because of what we said about iqtida' and hadhf.
3
State the Shafi'i position (opposing)
Imam al-Shafi'i likely held that such intention can specify the oath, because the context or the swearer's intent can particularize the general.
The text explicitly calls this 'مثال خلاف الشافعي'.
4
Explain why Hanafi rejects intention
The Hanafi reasoning is rooted in the nature of iqtida' and hadhf. In oaths, the general term is literal and is not omitted; there is no iqtida' to remove its generality. The swearer's intention is a personal matter that does not alter the linguistic reality. Iqtida' only applies when the literal meaning is impossible, which is not the case here.
The last example 'إن اغتسل أحد' is different because it is general by wording (nahw), not by intention.
5
Derive the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
In the Hanafi school, if someone swears 'If I eat' but only intends a specific food, eating any food violates the oath. In the Shafi'i school, only that specific food would violate. This affects the atonement (kaffarah) required.
The text does not state the Shafi'i consequence explicitly, but it is deducible.

The implied divorce is a necessity and does not carry generality. The author emphasizes that iqtida' is a legal tool akin to necessity (darura), and does not expand by analogy.

Key Terms
المقتضى (al-muqtaḍā): That which is implied necessarily from the wording for the validity of the ruling, without being explicitly mentioned.
المحذوف على وجه الاختصار (al-maḥdhūf ʿalā wajh al-ikhtiṣār): A word omitted from speech but understood from common linguistic usage for brevity; when assumed, it changes the attribution of the statement.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a man says 'إن خرجتُ فعبدي حر' and intends a specific place (e.g., his house) but goes out to the market, does his slave become free?
A) Yes, because the general term 'خرجت' applies to any going out.
B) No, because his intention specifies the oath.
C) No, because going to the market is not going out in the intended sense.
D) Yes, only if he explicitly says the place.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, the general term in oaths is binding and intent to specify does not restrict it, unlike in Shafi'i school where intent can specify.

The Limits of Iqtida' in Hanafi Usul
Defining iqtida' and its scope

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between linguistic abbreviation (ikhtisar) and legal implication (iqtida') in Hanafi usul and explain why iqtida' does not create generality.

Core Idea
Iqtida' is a legal necessity, not a linguistic tool; it cannot be extended beyond its immediate case.

This passage from al-Bazdawi's usul introduces two types of omitted elements in legal texts: those omitted by conventional abbreviation and those omitted by legal necessity. The distinction is crucial for determining whether a ruling can be generalized.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of omitted elements
The text distinguishes between omission by abbreviation (ikhtisar) and omission by legal implication (iqtida'). Recognize that ikhtisar is a standard linguistic practice, while iqtida' is a legal necessity.
Watch for the phrase 'اختصار لغةً' vs 'اقتضاء'.
2
Analyze the Quranic example
In 'Ask the town' (Quran 12:82), the word 'people' is omitted by abbreviation. The meaning is clear and the ruling applies generally to all people of the town.
3
Analyze the hadith examples
The hadith on error/forgiveness and on intentions show different treatments. For the error hadith, the apparent meaning is impossible, so a ruling is implied by iqtida'. For the intentions hadith, the omitted word is linguistically established, so it retains generality.
The text says the generality of the intentions hadith is not dropped because of iqtida'.
4
State the limiting principle
Iqtida' is like eating carrion out of necessity: it permits only what is necessary and no more. Iqtida' does not create generality beyond the specific case.
Analogize: just as carrion is only allowed to satisfy hunger, iqtida' only fills the gap in an utterance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
محذوفاً فقدر مذكوراً: انقطع عن المذكور. مثل قوله تعالى: ﴿ وَاسْتَلِ الْقَرْيَةَ ﴾ [يوسف / 82]: أن الأهل محذوف على سبيل الاختصار لغةً؛ لعدم الشبهة ألا يُرى أنه متى ذكر الأهل: انتقلت الإضافة عن القرية إلى الأهل والمقتضى: لتحقيق المقتضي، لا لنقله. ومثله قوله عليه الصلاة والسلام: «رفع عن أمتي الخطأ والنسيان»: لما استحال ظاهره: كان الحكم مضمراً محذوفاً، حتى إذا ظهر المضمر: انتقل الفعل عن الظاهر. وكذلك قوله عليه الصلاة والسلام: «إنما الأعمال بالنيات»: فلم يسقط عموم هذا الحديث من قبل الاقتضاء، لكن لأن المحذوف من الأسماء المشتركة، على ما مرَّ، وما حُذف اختصاراً وهو ثابت لغةً: كان عاماً، بلا خلاف؛ لأن الاختصار أحد طريقي اللغة، فأما الاقتضاء: فأمر شرعي ضروري مثل تحليل الميتة بالضرورة، فلا يزيد عليها. ولهذا قلنا فيمن قال لامرأته: أنت طالق ونوى به الثلاث: إن نيته باطلة؛ لأن المذكور نعتُ المرأة والطلاق الواقع مقدم عليه اقتضاء، لكنه ضروري، لا عموم له؛ لأن المذكور هو المرأة بأوصافها، وقد نوى عموم ما لم يتكلم به، والعموم من أوصاف النظم، ولم يكن المصدر ها هنا ثابتاً لغةً؛ لأن النعت يدل على المصدر الثابت بالموصوف لغةً؛ ليصير الوصف من المتكلم بناءً عليه، فأما أن يصير الوصف ثابتاً بالواصف بحقيقته تصحيحاً لوصفه: فأمر شرعي، ليس بلغوي. وكذلك ضربت: بناء على مصدر ماض، وطلقتك [؟] يوجب مصدراً من قبل المتكلم فكان شرعياً. وأما البائن وما يُشبه ذلك: فمثل قولك: طالق، من حيث إنه نعت فرد مقتض للواقع، غير أن البينونة تتصل بالمرأة للحال، ولاتصالها وجهان: انقطاع يرجع إلى الملك، وانقطاع يرجع إلى الحل، فتعَدَّدَ المقتضي بتعدد المقتضى على الاحتمال، فصح تعيينه. وأما طالق فلا يتصل بالمرأة للحال؛ لأن حكمه في الملك معلّق بالشرط وحكمه في الحِلَّ معلّق بكمال العدد، وإنما حكمه للحال: انعقاد العلة، وذلك غيرُ متنوع، فلم يتنوع المقتضى إلا بواسطة العدد، فيصير العدد أصلاً. وإذا قال لامرأته: طلقي نفسك: صحت نية الثلاث؛ لأن المصدر ها هنا ثابت لغةً؛ لأن الأمر فعل مستقبل وضع لطلب الفعل، فكان مختصراً من الكلام على نحو سائر الأفعال، فصار مذكوراً لغةً، فاحتمل الكلَّ والأقل، كسائر أسماء الأجناس. وأما طلقت: فنفس الفعل، ونفس الفعل في حال وجوده: لا يتعدد بالعزيمة، وذلك مثل قول الرجل: إن خرجتُ فعبدي حر: أنه تصح نية السفر؛ لأن ذِكْرَ الفعل لغةً: ذكر للمصدر، فأما المكان فثابت اقتضاء
Deleted but considered as mentioned: disconnected from what is mentioned. Like His saying Exalted: 'And ask the town' [Yusuf 82]: that 'the people' is deleted by way of abbreviation in language; because there is no ambiguity. Do you not see that when 'the people' is mentioned, the addition transfers from the town to the people? And the implied (muqtada) is for realizing the implicant, not for transferring it. Similarly, his saying, peace be upon him: 'My community has been forgiven for error and forgetfulness': since its apparent meaning is impossible, the ruling was implied and deleted, until when the implied appears, the verb transfers from the apparent. Also his saying: 'Actions are but by intentions': so the generality of this hadith is not dropped because of iqtida', but because the deleted is from common nouns, as previously mentioned. And what is deleted by abbreviation while being linguistically established is general, without dispute; because abbreviation is one of the two ways of language. As for iqtida', it is a necessary legal matter like the permissibility of carrion out of necessity; it does not extend beyond it. For this we said about one who says to his wife 'You are divorced' and intends three: his intention is invalid; because what is mentioned is a description of the woman, and the divorce that occurs is introduced by iqtida', but it is necessary, having no generality; because what is mentioned is the woman with her attributes, and he intended generality of what he did not utter, and generality is a property of order, and the infinitive was not linguistically established here, because the description indicates the infinitive established by the described in language, so that the description from the speaker is based on it. As for making the description established by the describer by its reality to correct his description, it is a legal matter, not linguistic. Likewise, 'I hit' is based on a past infinitive, and 'I divorced you' requires an infinitive from the speaker, so it is legal. As for ba'in (irrevocable divorce) and similar: like your saying 'divorced', in that it is a single description requiring the occurrence, except that ba'in connects to the woman immediately, and its connection has two aspects: severance returning to ownership, and severance returning to permissibility, so the requisite is multiplied by the multiplicity of the required due to possibility, so specifying it is valid. As for 'divorced' (without article), it does not connect to the woman immediately; because its ruling regarding ownership is suspended on condition and its ruling regarding permissibility is suspended on completion of the number, and its ruling immediately is the establishment of the cause, and that is not diverse, so the required is not diversified except by means of number, so number becomes the original. And when he says to his wife 'Divorce yourself,' the intention of three is valid; because the infinitive here is linguistically established; because the command is a future verb placed for requesting the action, so it is abbreviated from speech similar to other verbs, so it becomes mentioned linguistically, and thus it can encompass all and less, like other generic nouns. As for 'I divorced' (past tense), it is the very action, and the very action in its occurrence is not multiple by intention. And this is like a man saying 'If I go out, my slave is free': that the intention of travel is valid; because the mention of the verb in language is mention of the infinitive; as for the place, it is established by iqtida'.
Source pages p. 310

Commentary
This passage from al-Bazdawi's Usul discusses the concept of iqtida' (necessary implication) and its limits. The author begins by distinguishing between two types of omitted elements: 1) those omitted by linguistic abbreviation (ikhtisar) which are linguistically established and thus retain generality, and 2) those omitted by iqtida' (legal necessity) which are not linguistically established and thus do not create generality. He illustrates with Quranic and hadith examples: in the verse 'Ask the town' (Surah Yusuf), the word 'people' is omitted by abbreviation; the meaning is clear without ambiguity. Similarly, the hadith about forgiveness of error and forgetfulness cannot be taken literally (error and forgetfulness themselves are not real actions), so a ruling is implied. The hadith 'Actions are but by intentions' does not have its generality negated by iqtida', but because the omitted word ('intention' or similar) is a common noun that is linguistically established. The key principle: iqtida' is a legal necessity, like eating carrion when starving, and cannot be extended beyond the specific need. The author then applies this to divorce law: if a man says 'You are divorced' intending three divorces, his intention is invalid because the word 'divorced' is a description (na't) and the action of divorce is implied only by legal necessity, not linguistically. The description refers to the woman's attribute, and the number is not linguistically implied. In contrast, if he says 'Divorce yourself' (command), the intention of three is valid because the verb itself implies the action (infinitive) linguistically, so it can encompass multiple instances. The distinction hinges on whether the omitted element is linguistically established or only legally necessary. The passage also discusses ba'in (irrevocable divorce) and past tense verbs, showing that iqtida' is a restricted tool.

Key Terms
الاقتضاء (iqtida'): Necessary legal implication of an omitted element for validity of the utterance, not linguistically established.
الاختصار (ikhtisar): Linguistic abbreviation where the omitted element is understood by convention and retains generality.
المصدر (masdar): Verbal noun; the action implied by a verb or description.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, iqtida' is compared to which legal concept?
A) Eating carrion out of necessity
B) Using analogy (qiyas)
C) Seeking concession (rukhsah)
D) Abrogation (naskh)

Key Takeaway
Iqtida' is a necessary legal implication, not a linguistic convention; it cannot be generalized.

Applying Iqtida' to Divorce: The Validity of Intention for Triple Talaq
Applying iqtida' to divorce cases

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the distinction between ikhtisar and iqtida' to the Hanafi ruling on triple talaq and evaluate why intention of three divorces is invalid when using the description 'divorced' but valid when using a command.

Core Idea
The description 'divorced' (na't) implies divorce only by iqtida', not linguistically, so the number cannot be generalized; the command implies the action linguistically, allowing generalization.

Building on the concept of iqtida', this passage applies it to divorce law. The key is whether the omitted element (the number of divorces) is linguistically implied or only legally necessary. The type of utterance makes the difference between valid and invalid intention.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The text contrasts the statement 'You are divorced' (أنت طالق) with the command 'Divorce yourself' (طلقي نفسك). In the first, the word 'divorced' is a description; in the second, it is a verb command.
2
Analyze the linguistic nature of 'أنت طالق'
The word 'طالق' is a description (na't) of the woman. The action of divorce is not explicitly mentioned; it is implied by legal necessity (iqtida'). Since the infinitive (masdar) is not linguistically established, the implication is limited.
The text says 'المصدر هنا ثابتاً لغةً' because the description does not directly indicate the action; it relies on the described.
3
Analyze the linguistic nature of 'طلقي نفسك'
The command 'Divorce yourself' is a verb (imperative). Verbs imply their infinitive linguistically, so the masdar 'divorce' is established linguistically. This allows the action to encompass multiple instances by intention.
The text says 'المصدر هنا ثابت لغةً' because the verb is placed for requesting the action.
4
Consider contrast with past tense and ba'in
Past tense 'طلقت' (I divorced) does not allow multiple intentions because it refers to a single past action. For 'بائن' (irrevocable divorce), the text discusses how the two aspects of severance (ownership and permissibility) allow specification of intention.
