Refutation of the Claim that Specific Naming Excludes Other Cases from the Ruling
Refuting the view that naming restricts rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi argument against the claim that textual naming restricts the ruling and apply the principle that rulings are extended by cause ('illah).

Core Idea
Specific naming does not restrict a ruling; the cause ('illah) generalizes the ruling to all similar cases.

Some scholars argue that when a text names a specific thing, the ruling applies only to that thing and cannot be extended. The Hanafis reject this, holding that the ruling is established by the text's indication (dalalah), which includes the cause. This passage presents a logical and textual refutation of the exclusivity claim, using the hadith 'al-ma'u min al-ma' and the Quranic verse on justice as key examples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opposing claim
The opposing claim is that when a text names a specific thing by its proper name (ism alam), the ruling applies only to that named instance. Their evidence includes the hadith 'al-ma'u min al-ma' which the Ansar understood as restricting ghusl to cases where water (semen) is emitted.
2
State the Hanafi position
The Hanafi position is that the specificity of the name does not restrict the ruling. The ruling is based on the cause ('illah) that can be derived from the text, and that cause extends the ruling to all similar cases.
3
Examine the Hanafi proofs
The Hanafis provide logical arguments: if the text itself prevented extension, it would contradict the consensus on ta'lil (legal reasoning by cause). They also cite the Quranic verse 'fala tazlimu fihinna anfusakum' which prohibits injustice generally, despite mentioning sacred months specifically, showing that a general ruling is not restricted by its specific occasion.
4
Understand the counter-argument and its refutation
The opponent might argue that the text does not encompass other cases, so it cannot be used to prove the ruling for them. The Hanafi response is that extension is not by the text's wording but by its cause; the text does not prevent extension either. The ruling in the named case is established by the text, but negation in other cases is not implied.
5
Recognize the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
The dispute affects many fiqh rulings. For example, ghusl becomes obligatory due to sexual intercourse even without emission, whereas the opponent might require emission based on the literal hadith. The Hanafi ruling extends to all causes that share the same 'illah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ففسدت نية مكان، دون مكان.
ولا يلزم إذا حلف لا يساكن فلاناً، ونوى السكنى في بيت واحد : يصح، والمكان ثابت اقتضاء؛ لأن تعيين المكان لغو، حتى لا تصح نيته لو نوى بيتاً بعينه، لكن نية جُمَلة البيوت تصح؛ لأنه راجع إلى تكميل فعل المساكنة ؛ لأنها مفاعلة وإنما تتحقق بين اثنين على الكمال إذا جمعهما بيت واحد لكن اليمين وقعت على الدار، وهذا قاصر عادةً، فصحت نية الكامل، والمساكنة ثابتة لغةً، فصح تكميلها.
ولا يلزم عليه رجل قال الصغير : هذا ولدي، فجاءت أم الصغير بعد موت المقر ، فصدقته، وهي أم معروفة أنها تأخذ الميراث، وما ثبت الفراش إلا مقتضى النسب ؛ لأن النكاح ثبت بينهما مقتضى النسب، فكان مثل ثبوت البيع في قوله : أعتق عبدك عني بألف درهم. ولكن المقتضى غير متنوع، فيصير في حال بقائه : مثل النكاح المعقود قصداً.
والثابت بدلالة النص : لا يحتمل الخصوص أيضاً؛ لأن معنى النص إذا ثبت كونه علةً : لا يحتمل أن يكون غير علة ، وأما الثابت بإشارة النص : فيصلح أن يكون عاماً يُخص.
ومن الناس مَن عَمِل بالنصوص بوجوه أخر، هي فاسدة عندنا.
من ذلك : أنهم قالوا: إن النص على الشيء باسمه العلم: يدل على الخصوص.
قالوا: وذلك مثل قوله عليه الصلاة والسلام «الماء من الماء» : فهم الأنصار رضي الله عنهم من ذلك أن الغُسل لا يجب بالإكسال)؛ لعدم الماء.
وقلنا نحن : هذا باطل، وذلك كثير في الكتاب والسنة.
قال الله تعالى: ﴿ذَلِكَ الدِّينُ الْقَيِّمُ فَلَا تَظْلِمُوا فِيهِنَّ أَنفُسَكُمْ﴾ والظلم حرام في كل وقت.
ولأنه يقال له: إن أردت أن هذا الحكم غير ثابت في غير المسمى بالنص فكذلك عندنا ؛ لأن حكم النص في غيره لا يثبت به، بل بعلة النص. وإن عنى به أنه لا يثبت فيه بكون النص مانعاً : فهذا غلط ظاهر ؛ لأن النص لم يتناوله، فكيف يمنع؟
ولأنه لإيجاب الحكم في المسمى، فكيف يوجِبُ النفي وهو ضده؟ وقد أجمع الفقهاء على جواز التعليل ولو كان لخصوص الاسم أثر بالمنع في غيره : لصار التعليل على مضادة النص، وهو باطل.
So the intention of a place without a place becomes invalid. And it is not necessary if he swore 'I will not cohabit with so-and-so' and intended cohabitation in one house: it is valid, and the place is established by implication; because specifying the place is meaningless, so his intention would not be valid if he intended a specific house, but his intention of a group of houses is valid; because it goes back to completing the act of cohabitation; because it is mutual interaction and it only materializes completely between two when one house gathers them, but the oath fell on the house, and this is customarily deficient, so the intention of completion is valid, and cohabitation is established linguistically, so its completion is valid. And it is not necessary in the case of a man who said to a child: 'This is my son,' then the child's mother came after the death of the one who made the acknowledgment, and she confirmed it, and she is a known mother that she takes inheritance, and what established the bed (of marriage) except for the implication of lineage; because marriage was established between them by the implication of lineage, so it is like the establishment of sale in his statement: 'Free your slave for me for a thousand dirhams.' But the implied thing is not varied, so in its remaining state it becomes like a marriage contracted intentionally. And that which is established by the indication of the text does not admit of specification either; because the meaning of the text, when it is established as a cause, does not admit of being other than a cause. And as for what is established by the allusion of the text, it is suitable to be general and then specified. And some people have acted upon texts in other ways, which are invalid according to us. Among that: they said that the text on a thing by its proper name indicates specification. They said: and that is like his saying, peace be upon him, 'Water from water': the Ansar, may Allah be pleased with them, understood from that that ghusl is not obligatory due to [emission?] because of lack of water. And we said: this is false, and such [examples] are many in the Book and Sunnah. Allah said: 'That is the correct religion, so do not wrong yourselves therein' (Tawba 36) while injustice is forbidden at all times. And because it is said to him: if you mean that this ruling is not established for other than what is named by the text, then similarly according to us; because the ruling of the text is not established for other than it by the text itself, but by the cause of the text. And if he means that it is not established in it because the text prevents, then this is a manifest error; because the text did not encompass it, so how can it prevent? And because the text necessitates the ruling in the named thing, so how does it necessitate negation which is its opposite? And the jurists have agreed on the permissibility of legal reasoning (ta'lil), and if the specificity of the name had an effect of preventing in other cases, then legal reasoning would become contradictory to the text, and that is false.
Source pages p. 312

Commentary
This passage is a usul dispute refuting the claim that when a text names a specific thing, the ruling applies only to that named thing and cannot be extended to others. The author, following the Hanafi school, argues that the ruling is established by the indication (dalalah) of the text, which includes the cause ('illah). Therefore, the ruling applies to all cases that share that cause. The passage begins with two fiqh examples—one about oaths regarding cohabitation and another about acknowledgment of lineage—to illustrate how legal implications operate beyond literal words. Then it states the principle: what is established by the text's indication (dalalat al-nass) cannot be restricted to a specific instance because the meaning of the text is a cause. In contrast, what is established by allusion (isharah) can be general and then specified. The author then criticizes some scholars who claim that naming something by its proper name (ism 'alam) indicates exclusivity. They give the example of the hadith 'al-ma'u min al-ma' (water from water), which the Ansar understood to mean that ghusl is only required when water (semen) is emitted, ignoring other causes. The Hanafi response is that this understanding is false. They cite the Quranic verse 'do not wrong yourselves' which is general, even though it appears in a specific context. They provide logical arguments: the ruling in the named case is by the text itself, but extension to others is by the cause, not by the text's explicit wording. To claim that the text prevents extension is absurd because the text does not address those other cases. Moreover, if the specificity of the name prevented extension, then legal reasoning (ta'lil) would contradict the text, which is impossible. The passage concludes that jurists unanimously accept ta'lil, so the claim of exclusivity is invalid.

Key Terms
دلالة النص (dalalat al-nass): The indication of the text that establishes a ruling, which may be through explicit wording or through implied meaning (like the cause).
علة (‘illah): The effective cause or ratio legis that justifies extending a ruling from the named case to similar cases.
خصوص الاسم (khusus al-ism): The claim that naming a specific thing in a text restricts the ruling to that thing alone.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view presented in this passage, what is the effect of specifying a particular name (e.g., 'water') in a text on the ruling's applicability?
A) It restricts the ruling exclusively to the named instance.
B) It has no effect; the ruling applies based on the cause, so it includes all cases sharing that cause.
C) It makes the ruling applicable only to the named instance unless another text specifies otherwise.
D) It renders the ruling invalid for any other instance.

Key Takeaway
The ruling extends by cause ('illah), not by the literal name in the text.

The Effective Description and Its Negating Effect
Analyzing the principle of description as a condition-like negator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify an effective description in a legal text and explain its negating effect according to Hanafi usul.

Core Idea
A description in a legal text acts like a condition, negating the ruling for other cases.

This passage from Usul al-Bazdawi introduces a key Hanafi principle: when a ruling is tied to a descriptive phrase, that description can limit the ruling to the described case and negate it for others. This is distinct from a cause ('illa), which only establishes the ruling without implying negation. The highest degree of an effective description is that it functions as a cause, but the cause itself does not negate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the concept of wasf
Understand that the description (wasf) is a phrase that modifies a noun. In legal texts, it can have an effect of objection (i'tirad), similar to a condition.
The description is not the same as a cause ('illa).
2
Contrast wasf with 'illa
A cause establishes the ruling itself (e.g., theft for amputation). It does not negate the ruling when absent. A description, however, presupposes the ruling would apply by the mere name, and then restricts it to the described case.
Example: 'from among your believing slave women' – the description 'believing' restricts permission to believing slave women.
3
Analyze the Quranic example
Quran 4:25: 'min fatayatiku al-mu'minat'. Hanafis argue this implies that marrying non-believing (Kitabi) slave women is not prohibited by this verse, because the description negates the ruling for non-described cases.
The dalala is zanni because the verse is about permission, not prohibition.
4
Distinguish effective vs. non-effective descriptions
Only when the ruling would otherwise apply by the mere name does the description have a negating effect. If the description is the cause itself, it does not negate (e.g., 'the thief' is the cause, not a description that restricts).
Highest degree of wasf is when it is also a cause, but still the cause itself does not negate.
5
Apply the principle
Use the principle to evaluate other texts: does a descriptive phrase limit the ruling? Check if the ruling would be established without the description.
Cross-reference with conjunction debate in Lesson 2.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مؤخراً ونافياً حكم الإيجاب، والوصفُ لولا هو لكان الحكم ثابتاً بمطلق الاسم أيضاً، فصار للوصف أثر الاعتراض، بمنزلة الشرط، فأُلحق به. وهذا بخلاف العلة؛ لأنها لابتداء الإيجاب، لا للاعتراض على ما يوجِبُ، فصار بمنزلة الاسم العلم، فتعلق بها الوجود، ولم تُوجِب العدم عند عدمها.
ولنا: أن أقصى درجات الوصف إذا كان مؤثراً: أن يكون علة الحكم، مثل السارق والزاني، ولا أثر للعلة في النفي.
ومثال هذا أيضاً : قوله تعالى: {مِن فَتَيَاتِكُمُ الْمُؤْمِنَاتِ} [النساء / 25] : فهذا لا يوجب تحريم نكاح الأمة الكتابية عندنا؛ لما قلنا.
* ولا يلزم على هذا الأصل: ما قال أصحابنا في الدعوى: في أمة ولدت ثلاثة أولاد في بطون مختلفة، فادعى المولى نسب الأكبر: إن نسب من بعده لا يثبت فيُجعَل تخصيصه نفياً، ولولا ذلك لثبت؛ لأنهما وَلَدَا أم ولده.
وقال في الشهادات والدعوى : إذا قال شهود الميراث: لا نعلم له وارثاً في أرض كذا إن هذه الشهادة لا تُقبل عند أبي يوسف ومحمد رحمهما الله ، ويُجعَل النفي في مكان كذا : إثباتاً في غيره.
أما في المسألة الأولى: فلم يثبتِ النفي بالخصوص، لكن لأن التزام النسب عند ظهور دليله واجب شرعاً، والتبرأ عند ظهور دليله واجبٌ أيضاً، والالتزام بالبيان فرض صيانةً عن النفي: فصار السكوت عند لزوم البيان لو كان ثابتاً نفياً ؛ حملاً لأمره على الصلاح، حتى لا يصير تاركاً للفرض.
وفي مسألة الشهادة زاد الشهود ما لا حاجة إليه، وفيه شبهة وبالشبهة تُرد الشهادة وبمثلها لا يصح إثبات الأحكام.
وقال أبو حنيفة رحمه الله : هذا سكوت في غير موضع الحاجة؛ لأن ذكر المكان غيرُ واجب، وذكرُ المكان يحتمل الاحتراز عن المجازفة. ومن ذلك أن القرآن في النظم يوجب القرآن في الحكم عند بعضهم مثل قول بعضهم في قوله تعالى : {وَأَقِيمُوا الصَّلَاةَ وَآتُوا الزَّكَاةَ} [البقرة / 110] : إن القرآن يوجب أن لا تجب على الصبي الزكاة، وقالوا: لأن العطف يوجب الشركة، واعتبروا بالجملة الناقصة.
وقلنا نحن إن عطف الجملة على الجملة في اللغة: لا يوجب الشركة ؛ لأن الشركة إنما وجبت بينهما لافتقار الجملة الناقصة إلى ما تتم به ، فإذا تم بنفسه : لم تجب الشركة إلا فيما يفتقر إليه.
وهذا أكثر في كتاب الله تعالى من أن يُحصى.
ولهذا قلنا في قول الرجل : إن دخلت الدار فأنتِ طالق وعبدي هذا حر: إن العتق يتعلق بالشرط وإن كان تاماً ؛ لأنه في حكم التعليق قاصر. وعلى هذا قلنا في قول الله تعالى: {فَاجْلِدُوهُمْ ثَمَانِينَ جَلْدَةً وَلَا تَقْبَلُوا لَهُمْ شَهَادَةً أَبَدًا} [النور / 4] : إن قوله تعالى : فاجلدوهم: جزاء، وقوله: {وَلَا تَقْبَلُوا لَهُمْ شَهَادَةً} : وإن كان تاماً، ولكنه من حيث إنه يصلح جزاء واحداً:
Delaying and negating the ruling of obligation, and the description—if not for it, the ruling would also be established by the mere name—so the description has the effect of objection, like a condition, and is thus annexed to it. This is contrary to the cause ('illa) because it is for initiating obligation, not for objecting to what is obligatory, so it is like a proper noun; existence is attached to it, and it does not necessitate non-existence when it is absent. Our position: that the highest degree of description, if it is effective, is that it is a cause of the ruling, like 'the thief' and 'the adulterer', and the cause has no effect in negation. An example of this is also His statement, the Exalted: {from among your believing slave women} [Quran 4:25]. This does not necessitate prohibition of marrying a Kitabi slave woman according to us, for what we have said. And this principle is not invalidated by what our companions said in litigation: concerning a slave woman who gave birth to three children in different pregnancies, and the master claimed lineage of the eldest—that the lineage of those after him is not established, and his specification is made a negation, and otherwise it would be established because they are children of his umm walad. And he said in testimony and claims: if the witnesses of inheritance say, 'We do not know of any heir for him in such-and-such land,' this testimony is not accepted according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on them), and the negation concerning that place is made an affirmation concerning others. As for the first issue: the negation is not established by the specification, but because accepting lineage when its evidence appears is obligatory by law, and disavowing it when its evidence appears is also obligatory, and clarifying is a duty to guard against negation; so silence when clarification is necessary—if it were established as negation—would require interpreting his matter as proper conduct, so that he does not become one who abandons the duty. And in the issue of testimony, the witnesses added something unnecessary, and it contains uncertainty, and due to uncertainty testimony is rejected, and by such means legal rulings cannot be established. Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him) said: This is silence in a situation where there is no need, because mentioning the place is not obligatory, and mentioning the place may be to avoid exaggeration. And from that is that the Qur'an in its construction necessitates correlation in ruling according to some, such as their statement concerning His statement, the Exalted: {and establish prayer and give zakah} [Quran 2:110]: that the Qur'an necessitates that zakah is not obligatory upon a child, and they said: because conjunction necessitates partnership, and they relied on the incomplete sentence. And we said: conjunction of a complete sentence to a complete sentence in language does not necessitate partnership, because partnership is only established between them due to the incomplete sentence's need for completion, so if it is complete in itself, partnership is not obligatory except in what it needs. And this is too abundant in the Book of Allah to be counted. For this reason we said concerning a man's statement: 'If you enter the house, you are divorced and this slave of mine is free'—that manumission is contingent on the condition even if it is complete, because it is deficient in the ruling of suspension. And on this basis we said concerning the statement of Allah, the Exalted: {then flog them eighty lashes and do not accept their testimony ever} [Quran 24:4]—that His statement 'flog them' is the consequence, and His statement 'and do not accept their testimony'—even if it is complete, but from the aspect that it can be a single consequence.
Source pages p. 315

Commentary
This passage is a discussion from Usul al-Bazdawi on the principle that a descriptive phrase (wasf) in a legal text can have a negating effect, meaning that the ruling applies only to the described case and not to others, similar to the effect of a condition (shart). The author contrasts this with a cause ('illa), which establishes the ruling itself and does not imply negation when absent. The highest degree of an effective description is that it functions as a cause (like 'thief' for amputation), but still the cause itself does not negate. The passage provides several examples: Qur'an 4:25 permitting marriage to believing slave women, which according to the Hanafis implies that marrying a Kitabi (scriptural) slave woman is not prohibited by this verse (since only believing ones are mentioned). The author then addresses potential counterexamples: a case where a slave woman gives birth to three children and the master claims lineage of the eldest, and a case where inheritance witnesses specify a place. The Hanafi position distinguishes between situations where silence requires clarification and where it does not. The passage also refutes the Shafi'i argument that conjunction (عطف) in Qur'anic sentences necessarily implies partnership in ruling, using the example of 'establish prayer and give zakah' (argued to show zakah is not obligatory on a child). The author states that conjunction of complete sentences does not create partnership; only incomplete sentences require it. This is applied to other contexts like conditional divorce and manumission, and to the verse on flogging and rejecting testimony for qadhf. The overall lesson is that descriptive limitations and conjunction in legal texts must be interpreted carefully; the Hanafi usul approach emphasizes that a description can negate the ruling for other cases, and conjunction does not always imply shared rulings.

Key Terms
الوصف (al-wasf): A descriptive phrase that, when attached to a name, can negate the ruling for other cases (like a condition)
العلة (al-‘illa): The effective cause that establishes the ruling, not intended for negation
عطف الجملة (‘atf al-jumla): Conjunction of sentences; the author argues it does not create partnership in ruling unless the sentence is incomplete

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi usul principle discussed, what is the effect of an effective description (wasf mu'aththir)?
A) It establishes the ruling for all cases similar to the described case.
B) It acts like a condition, negating the ruling for cases not described.
C) It has no legal effect at all.
D) It only affirms the ruling for the described case without implying anything else.

Key Takeaway
An effective description in a legal text limits the ruling to the described case and negates it for others, functioning like a condition.

Conjunction of Sentences and Its Legal Implications
Refuting the Shafi'i argument that conjunction implies shared rulings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that conjunction of complete sentences does not imply partnership in ruling, and distinguish it from the Shafi'i view.

Core Idea
Conjunction of complete sentences does not create shared rulings; only incomplete sentences do.

This section of the passage addresses an important usul dispute: whether the conjunction (عطف) of two sentences in the Quran implies they share the same legal conditions. The Hanafis argue that only incomplete sentences (e.g., dependent clauses) require partnership, while complete sentences are independent. This has practical implications, such as whether zakah is obligatory on a child if prayer is not obligatory.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Shafi'i argument
The Shafi'is (referred to as 'some') argue that conjunction of two sentences in the Quran implies partnership in ruling, using the example of 'wa aqimu al-salah wa atu al-zakah' (Quran 2:110). They claim that since prayer is not obligatory on a child, zakah must also not be obligatory.
This is based on the principle that conjunction (عطف) creates joint involvement (شركة).
2
Understand the Hanafi response
Hanafis argue that conjunction of complete sentences does not create partnership. Partnership only arises when the second sentence is incomplete and depends on the first for completion.
The word 'wa' in Arabic can be for mere conjunction without implying shared ruling.
3
Analyze the examples given
The passage provides additional examples: a man's statement 'If you enter the house, you are divorced and this slave of mine is free' – the freeing is also conditioned on entry even though the sentence is complete. And Quran 24:4 on flogging and rejecting testimony – both are consequences of the same action.
These show that even complete sentences can be linked to the same condition or cause.
4
Extract the rule
The Hanafi rule: عطف الجملة التامة على الجملة التامة لا يوجب الشركة. Conjunction of a complete sentence to a complete sentence does not necessitate partnership.
This is a linguistic principle.
5
Apply to a new case
Consider Quran 24:2: 'The fornicatress and the fornicator, flog each of them one hundred lashes.' If someone argues that this implies the conditions for flogging apply equally to both, the Hanafi response would be that the conjunction here is of nouns (not sentences) and might indicate shared ruling, but the principle of sentences is different.
The lesson focuses on sentence conjunction, not word conjunction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مؤخراً ونافياً حكم الإيجاب، والوصفُ لولا هو لكان الحكم ثابتاً بمطلق الاسم أيضاً، فصار للوصف أثر الاعتراض، بمنزلة الشرط، فأُلحق به. وهذا بخلاف العلة؛ لأنها لابتداء الإيجاب، لا للاعتراض على ما يوجِبُ، فصار بمنزلة الاسم العلم، فتعلق بها الوجود، ولم تُوجِب العدم عند عدمها.
ولنا: أن أقصى درجات الوصف إذا كان مؤثراً: أن يكون علة الحكم، مثل السارق والزاني، ولا أثر للعلة في النفي.
ومثال هذا أيضاً : قوله تعالى: {مِن فَتَيَاتِكُمُ الْمُؤْمِنَاتِ} [النساء / 25] : فهذا لا يوجب تحريم نكاح الأمة الكتابية عندنا؛ لما قلنا.
* ولا يلزم على هذا الأصل: ما قال أصحابنا في الدعوى: في أمة ولدت ثلاثة أولاد في بطون مختلفة، فادعى المولى نسب الأكبر: إن نسب من بعده لا يثبت فيُجعَل تخصيصه نفياً، ولولا ذلك لثبت؛ لأنهما وَلَدَا أم ولده.
وقال في الشهادات والدعوى : إذا قال شهود الميراث: لا نعلم له وارثاً في أرض كذا إن هذه الشهادة لا تُقبل عند أبي يوسف ومحمد رحمهما الله ، ويُجعَل النفي في مكان كذا : إثباتاً في غيره.
أما في المسألة الأولى: فلم يثبتِ النفي بالخصوص، لكن لأن التزام النسب عند ظهور دليله واجب شرعاً، والتبرأ عند ظهور دليله واجبٌ أيضاً، والالتزام بالبيان فرض صيانةً عن النفي: فصار السكوت عند لزوم البيان لو كان ثابتاً نفياً ؛ حملاً لأمره على الصلاح، حتى لا يصير تاركاً للفرض.
وفي مسألة الشهادة زاد الشهود ما لا حاجة إليه، وفيه شبهة وبالشبهة تُرد الشهادة وبمثلها لا يصح إثبات الأحكام.
وقال أبو حنيفة رحمه الله : هذا سكوت في غير موضع الحاجة؛ لأن ذكر المكان غيرُ واجب، وذكرُ المكان يحتمل الاحتراز عن المجازفة. ومن ذلك أن القرآن في النظم يوجب القرآن في الحكم عند بعضهم مثل قول بعضهم في قوله تعالى : {وَأَقِيمُوا الصَّلَاةَ وَآتُوا الزَّكَاةَ} [البقرة / 110] : إن القرآن يوجب أن لا تجب على الصبي الزكاة، وقالوا: لأن العطف يوجب الشركة، واعتبروا بالجملة الناقصة.
وقلنا نحن إن عطف الجملة على الجملة في اللغة: لا يوجب الشركة ؛ لأن الشركة إنما وجبت بينهما لافتقار الجملة الناقصة إلى ما تتم به ، فإذا تم بنفسه : لم تجب الشركة إلا فيما يفتقر إليه.
وهذا أكثر في كتاب الله تعالى من أن يُحصى.
ولهذا قلنا في قول الرجل : إن دخلت الدار فأنتِ طالق وعبدي هذا حر: إن العتق يتعلق بالشرط وإن كان تاماً ؛ لأنه في حكم التعليق قاصر. وعلى هذا قلنا في قول الله تعالى: {فَاجْلِدُوهُمْ ثَمَانِينَ جَلْدَةً وَلَا تَقْبَلُوا لَهُمْ شَهَادَةً أَبَدًا} [النور / 4] : إن قوله تعالى : فاجلدوهم: جزاء، وقوله: {وَلَا تَقْبَلُوا لَهُمْ شَهَادَةً} : وإن كان تاماً، ولكنه من حيث إنه يصلح جزاء واحداً:
Delaying and negating the ruling of obligation, and the description—if not for it, the ruling would also be established by the mere name—so the description has the effect of objection, like a condition, and is thus annexed to it. This is contrary to the cause ('illa) because it is for initiating obligation, not for objecting to what is obligatory, so it is like a proper noun; existence is attached to it, and it does not necessitate non-existence when it is absent. Our position: that the highest degree of description, if it is effective, is that it is a cause of the ruling, like 'the thief' and 'the adulterer', and the cause has no effect in negation. An example of this is also His statement, the Exalted: {from among your believing slave women} [Quran 4:25]. This does not necessitate prohibition of marrying a Kitabi slave woman according to us, for what we have said. And this principle is not invalidated by what our companions said in litigation: concerning a slave woman who gave birth to three children in different pregnancies, and the master claimed lineage of the eldest—that the lineage of those after him is not established, and his specification is made a negation, and otherwise it would be established because they are children of his umm walad. And he said in testimony and claims: if the witnesses of inheritance say, 'We do not know of any heir for him in such-and-such land,' this testimony is not accepted according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on them), and the negation concerning that place is made an affirmation concerning others. As for the first issue: the negation is not established by the specification, but because accepting lineage when its evidence appears is obligatory by law, and disavowing it when its evidence appears is also obligatory, and clarifying is a duty to guard against negation; so silence when clarification is necessary—if it were established as negation—would require interpreting his matter as proper conduct, so that he does not become one who abandons the duty. And in the issue of testimony, the witnesses added something unnecessary, and it contains uncertainty, and due to uncertainty testimony is rejected, and by such means legal rulings cannot be established. Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him) said: This is silence in a situation where there is no need, because mentioning the place is not obligatory, and mentioning the place may be to avoid exaggeration. And from that is that the Qur'an in its construction necessitates correlation in ruling according to some, such as their statement concerning His statement, the Exalted: {and establish prayer and give zakah} [Quran 2:110]: that the Qur'an necessitates that zakah is not obligatory upon a child, and they said: because conjunction necessitates partnership, and they relied on the incomplete sentence. And we said: conjunction of a complete sentence to a complete sentence in language does not necessitate partnership, because partnership is only established between them due to the incomplete sentence's need for completion, so if it is complete in itself, partnership is not obligatory except in what it needs. And this is too abundant in the Book of Allah to be counted. For this reason we said concerning a man's statement: 'If you enter the house, you are divorced and this slave of mine is free'—that manumission is contingent on the condition even if it is complete, because it is deficient in the ruling of suspension. And on this basis we said concerning the statement of Allah, the Exalted: {then flog them eighty lashes and do not accept their testimony ever} [Quran 24:4]—that His statement 'flog them' is the consequence, and His statement 'and do not accept their testimony'—even if it is complete, but from the aspect that it can be a single consequence.
Source pages p. 315

Commentary
This passage is a discussion from Usul al-Bazdawi on the principle that a descriptive phrase (wasf) in a legal text can have a negating effect, meaning that the ruling applies only to the described case and not to others, similar to the effect of a condition (shart). The author contrasts this with a cause ('illa), which establishes the ruling itself and does not imply negation when absent. The highest degree of an effective description is that it functions as a cause (like 'thief' for amputation), but still the cause itself does not negate. The passage provides several examples: Qur'an 4:25 permitting marriage to believing slave women, which according to the Hanafis implies that marrying a Kitabi (scriptural) slave woman is not prohibited by this verse (since only believing ones are mentioned). The author then addresses potential counterexamples: a case where a slave woman gives birth to three children and the master claims lineage of the eldest, and a case where inheritance witnesses specify a place. The Hanafi position distinguishes between situations where silence requires clarification and where it does not. The passage also refutes the Shafi'i argument that conjunction (عطف) in Qur'anic sentences necessarily implies partnership in ruling, using the example of 'establish prayer and give zakah' (argued to show zakah is not obligatory on a child). The author states that conjunction of complete sentences does not create partnership; only incomplete sentences require it. This is applied to other contexts like conditional divorce and manumission, and to the verse on flogging and rejecting testimony for qadhf. The overall lesson is that descriptive limitations and conjunction in legal texts must be interpreted carefully; the Hanafi usul approach emphasizes that a description can negate the ruling for other cases, and conjunction does not always imply shared rulings.

Key Terms
عطف الجملة (‘atf al-jumla): Conjunction of sentences; the author argues it does not create partnership in ruling unless the sentence is incomplete
الشركة (al-shirka): Partnership or joint involvement in a ruling; the claim that two rulings share the same conditions
الجملة التامة (al-jumla al-tammah): A complete sentence that does not depend on another for its meaning

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi usul principle discussed, what does conjunction of two complete sentences imply?
A) They share all legal conditions.
B) They are independent rulings unless context indicates otherwise.
C) The second sentence is always dependent on the first.
D) It creates a new combined ruling.

Key Takeaway
Conjunction of complete sentences does not create shared legal conditions; each sentence is independently interpreted.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
beginner
6 min
The Principle that a General (ʿĀmm) is Not Restricted by Its Cause
Refuting the claim that general texts are limited to their specific causes of revelation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that a general text is not restricted by its cause and apply it to examples from Quranic verses and hadith.

Core Idea
A general text applies universally; its specific cause does not restrict its ruling.

One of the foundational principles of Hanafi usul al-fiqh is that a general (ʿāmm) text retains its generality even if it was revealed or enacted for a specific reason (sabab). Some scholars have argued that the context of revelation should limit the application, but the Hanafis firmly reject this. In this lesson, we will examine the textual basis for this principle and see how it is applied across various legal topics.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Claim Being Refuted
The passage begins by stating an opposing view: that a general text is restricted by its cause. Recognize that this is the claim the Hanafis are refuting.
The phrase 'قول بعضهم' refers to an unnamed group, often identified as those who hold the opposite view.
2
Understand the Hanafi Response
The text declares this view as 'bāṭil' (false) and gives the reason: the text itself is silent (sākit) about its cause, and silence cannot be used as evidence (ḥujja) to limit the generality.
This is a rational argument (dalīl ʿaqlī): absence of proof does not prove restriction.
3
Examine the Examples Provided
The passage mentions 'ẓihār' and 'liʿān', both of which have specific causes in revelation but the rulings derived from them are applied generally. This illustrates the principle in practice.
Ẓihār: Surah al-Mujadila (58:1-4) revealed about Khawlah bint Thaʿlabah. Liʿān: Surah al-Nur (24:6-9) revealed about a specific incident. Both are applied to all similar cases.
4
Grasp the Thamarah al-Khilaf (Practical Consequence)
If the opposing view were accepted, many general rulings would become narrowly applicable only to their original contexts, contradicting the practice of the Companions and jurists.
The practical difference is that the Hanafi position ensures the broad applicability of Qur'anic and Sunnah rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن ذلك : قول بعضهم : إن العام يختص بسببه. وهذا عندنا باطل؛ لأن النص ساكت عن سببه، والسكوت لا يكون حجةً، ألا يُرى أن عامة الحوادث مثل الظهار واللعان وغير ذلك مقيدة بأسباب، ولم تختص بها. وردت
And from that is the saying of some: that the general (ʿāmm) is restricted by its cause. And this, according to us, is false; because the text is silent about its cause, and silence is not a proof. Do you not see that most occurrences like ẓihār, liʿān, and others are tied to causes, yet they are not restricted to them? And they were rejected.
Source pages p. 317

Commentary
This passage presents a fundamental Hanafi usul principle: a general text (ʿāmm) is not restricted to the specific circumstance (sabab) that occasioned it. The text itself does not mention the cause, and silence (sukūt) cannot be used as evidence to restrict the generality. The Hanafis argue that many legal topics (like ẓihār and liʿān) have specific causes in revelation but the rulings derived from them are general and apply to all similar cases. The phrase 'وردت' likely means 'and (these claims) have been rejected'.

Key Terms
العام (al-ʿāmm): A word that encompasses all individuals of its category without restriction.
السبب (al-sabab): The occasion or cause for a revelation or ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, what is the status of a general text in relation to its cause (sabab)?
A) The general is restricted to its cause.
B) The general is not restricted by its cause; it applies universally.
C) The general applies only if the cause is explicitly mentioned in the text.
D) The general is abrogated by its cause.

Key Takeaway
A general text is not restricted by its specific cause; its ruling applies to all individuals included under its generality unless there is explicit evidence of specification.

The Hanafi Classification of Sentences and Their Relation to Cause (Sabab)
Hanafi classification of sentences and refutation of Shafi'i's takhsis bi al-sabab

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish the four types of Hanafi sentence classifications related to a cause, and apply them to analyze Quranic and Hadith texts for determining whether a ruling is restricted to its specific occasion.

Core Idea
A general statement is not restricted by its cause; only conditional consequences (jaza') are tied to their specific occasion.

In this passage from Usul al-Bazdawi, the author critiques Imam al-Shafi'i's interpretation of Quranic verses from Surah al-Nur, and then establishes the Hanafi rule that a general statement (amm) is not restricted to its specific cause (sabab). To understand this, he classifies sentences into four types based on their grammatical relationship to a cause. This classification is essential for correctly deriving rulings from the Quran and Sunnah.

Guided Path
1
Understand the dispute
Identify the core disagreement: Does a general ruling revealed due to a specific incident apply only to that incident (Shafi'i) or is it general (Hanafi)?
The Hanafi position is that the text is silent about its cause, and silence is not a proof.
2
Identify the four types of sentences
Learn the four categories: (1) Jaza' (conditional consequence) – specific to its cause; (2) Ghayr mustaqill (dependent clause) – does not stand alone; (3) Jawab (answer that may also be independent); (4) Ibtida' (independent statement that may be built upon preceding).
Memorize these four as a framework for textual analysis.
3
Apply to Quranic examples
Analyze the verses from Surah al-Nur (24:4). The first verse (wa alladhina yarmun... fa in lam ya'tu... fa in 'udhtum... wa la taqbalu lahum shahadatan abadan) is a conditional consequence (jaza'), so its ruling is tied to the cause (qadhf) and condition (inability to produce evidence). The second verse (wa ula'ika humu al-fasiqun) is a narrative statement, not a jaza', thus not restricted to a cause.
Note the use of fa' and waw as indicators of connection.
4
Apply to Hadith examples
The hadith of the Prophet's forgetfulness prostration (with fa') and Ma'iz's stoning (with fa') are conditional consequences, so they are specific to their causes (forgetfulness and confession of zina).
Contrast with general rulings like 'The fornicator shall be flogged' which are not tied to a specific incident.
5
Derive the ruling
Conclude that only type 1 (jaza') is restricted to its cause; all other types are general and apply universally unless there is explicit takhsis. This is the Hanafi principle.
This classification prevents erroneous restriction of general rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مفتقر إلى الشرط، فجعل ملحقاً بالأول. ألا يُرى أن جرح الشهادة إيلام كالضرب، وألا يُرى أنه فُوض إلى الأئمة. فأما قوله: ﴿ وَأُولَئِكَ هُمُ الْفَاسِقُونَ ﴾ النور / ٤ : فلا يصلح جزاء؛ لأن الجزاء ما يُقام ابتداء بولاية الإمام. وأما الحكاية عن حال قائمة : فلا فاعتُبر تمامها بصيغتها، فكانت في حق الجزاء: في حكم الجملة المبتدأة، مثل قوله تعالى: ﴿وَيَمْحُ اللَّهُ الْبَاطِلَ﴾ الشورى / ٢٤ ، ومثل قوله تعالى: ﴿وَنُقِرُّ فِي الْأَرْحَامِ مَا نَشَاءُ﴾ الحج / ٥ ، ﴿وَيَتُوبُ اللهُ عَلَى مَن يَشَاءُ﴾ التوبة / ١٥. والشافعي رحمه الله قَطَعَ قولَه : ﴿ وَلَا نَقْبَلُوا لَهُمْ﴾ النور / ٤ ، مع قيام دليل الاتصال، ووَصَلَ قولَه : ﴿وَأُولَئِكَ هُمُ الْفَاسِقُونَ﴾ النور / ٤ ، بما قبله، مع قيام دليل الانفصال، وكل ذلك غلط. وقلنا نحن بصيغة الكلام: إن القذف سبب، والعجز عن البينة شرطٌ بصفة التراخي، والردُّ حد مشارِك للجلد ؛ لأنه عطف بالواو، والعجز عطف بـ: ثم. ومن ذلك : قولُ بعضهم : إن العام يختص بسببه. وهذا عندنا باطل؛ لأن النص ساكت عن سببه، والسكوت لا يكون حجةً، ألا يُرى أن عامة الحوادث مثل الظهار واللعان وغير ذلك مقيدة بأسباب، ولم تختص بها. وهذه الجملة عندنا على أربعة أوجه : الوجه الأول : ما خرج مخرج الجزاء، فيختص بسببه. والثاني : ما لا يستقل بنفسه. والثالث : ما خرج مخرج الجواب، واحتمل الابتداء. والرابع : ما زيد على قدر الجواب فكان ابتداء يحتمل البناء. - أما الأول فمثل ما روي عن النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام «أنه سها، فسجد». وروي أن ماعزاً زنى فرجم، والفاء: للجزاء، فتعلق بالأول، على ما مر بيانه. وأما الثاني : فمثل الرجل يقول لآخر: أليس لي عليك كذا، فيقول: بلى، أو يقول : أكان كذا؟ فيقول : نعم : يُجعَل إقراراً. وكذلك إذا قال: أجل: هذا أصل: بلى، ونعم: أن يكون : بلى: بناءً على النفي في الابتداء، مع الاستفهام، ونعم المحض الاستفهام، وأجل يجمعهما. لذلك. وقد يُستعملان في غير الاستفهام على إدراج الاستفهام، أو مستعاراً
Dependent on the condition, so it is attached to the first. Do you not see that wounding testimony is a pain like beating, and do you not see that it is delegated to the imams. As for His statement: {And those are the disobedient} [al-Nur 24:4] it is not suitable as a conditional consequence; because the conditional consequence is that which is established initially by the authority of the imam. As for the narration of a standing situation: its completion was not considered by its form, so it is, regarding the conditional consequence, in the ruling of an independent sentence, like His statement: {And Allah erases the false} [al-Shura 42:24], and like His statement: {And We settle in the wombs what We will} [al-Hajj 22:5], {And Allah turns in forgiveness to whom He wills} [al-Tawba 9:15]. Al-Shafi‘i, may Allah have mercy on him, disconnected His statement: {And do not accept from them} [al-Nur 24:4], despite the existence of evidence of connection, and connected His statement: {And those are the disobedient} [al-Nur 24:4] with what preceded it, despite the existence of evidence of disconnection, and all of that is wrong. And we said according to the phrasing of speech: indeed, false accusation (qadhf) is a cause, and inability to produce evidence is a condition characterized by delay, and rejection is a punishment sharing with flogging because it is conjoined by waw, and inability is conjoined by thumma. And from that: the statement of some: that the general is specified by its cause. And this, according to us, is invalid; because the text is silent about its cause, and silence is not a proof. Do you not see that most incidents such as zihar, li‘an, and others are restricted by causes, but they are not specified by them? And these sentences according to us are of four aspects: The first aspect: what comes in the form of a conditional consequence, so it is specified by its cause. The second: what does not stand independently. The third: what comes in the form of an answer, but could potentially be an independent beginning. The fourth: what exceeds the amount of the answer, so it is an independent beginning that may possibly be built [upon the preceding]. As for the first, it is like what was narrated from the Prophet, peace and blessings be upon him, “that he forgot, so he prostrated.” And it was narrated that Ma‘iz committed adultery and was stoned, and the fa’ is for the conditional consequence, so it is attached to the first, as has been explained. As for the second: like the man saying to another: “Do I not have such and such upon you?” and he says: “Yes (bala),” or he says: “Was it such and such?” and he says: “Yes (na‘am)”; it is considered an acknowledgment. Likewise, if he says: “Indeed (ajal).” This is the basis: that bala is built upon negation at the beginning, accompanied by interrogation, and na‘am is purely for interrogation, and ajal combines both. Therefore, and they may be used in non-interrogation to include interrogation, or metaphorically.
Source pages p. 317

Commentary
This passage from Usul al-Bazdawi addresses the Hanafi principle regarding whether a general statement (amm) is restricted to its specific cause (sabab). The text begins by critiquing Imam al-Shafi‘i’s interpretation of two verses from Surah al-Nur (24:4). Shafi‘i disconnected the verse “and do not accept from them” from the preceding conditional clause, despite evidence of connection, and connected the verse “and those are the disobedient” despite evidence of disconnection. The Hanafis argue that the first verse (about qadhf) is a conditional consequence (jaza‘) because false accusation is the cause and the inability to produce evidence is a delayed condition. The second verse is a narrative statement, not a jaza‘, hence not restricted by a cause. The author then states the rule that a general is not specified by its cause because the text is silent about the cause, and silence is not a proof. He gives examples of zihar and li‘an, which are restricted by causes but not confined to those incidents. Finally, he presents the four-fold Hanafi classification of sentences regarding attachment to a cause: (1) conditional consequence (jaza‘) – specific to its cause; (2) dependent clause (ghayr mustaqill) – does not stand alone; (3) answer (jawab) – may be independent; (4) surplus beyond the answer – independent but may be connected. Examples from hadith illustrate the first type: the Prophet’s forgetfulness prostration and Ma‘iz’s stoning are conditional consequences tied to their causes. The second type is exemplified by affirmative responses (bala, na‘am, ajal) in legal contexts, which are treated as acknowledgments because they are dependent on the preceding question. This analysis is critical for correctly deriving rulings from revealed texts, as misidentifying sentence types leads to legal errors.

Key Terms
عام (amm): A word that encompasses all individuals of its category without limitation.
سبب (sabab): The specific occasion, question, or event that prompted the revelation or utterance of a ruling.
جزاء (jaza'): The apodosis or consequence clause in a conditional sentence (e.g., 'if...then...').
جواب (jawab): An answer or response to a question or condition.
ابتداء (ibtida'): An independent statement that is not grammatically attached to a preceding clause.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafis, which type of sentence is restricted to its specific cause?
A) An independent narrative sentence (ibtida')
B) A sentence that does not stand alone (ghayr mustaqill)
C) A conditional consequence (jaza')
D) An answer (jawab) that can also be independent

Key Takeaway
Only conditional consequences (jaza') are restricted to their cause; other sentence types are not, and silence of the text is not proof.

Carrying the Absolute to the Restricted by Indication
Presenting the Shafi'i view on applying a restricted ruling to an absolute one via indication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Shafi'i rule of carrying the absolute (mutlaq) to the restricted (muqayyad) by indication and distinguish it from the Hanafi position.

Core Idea
When a single incident has both a mutlaq and a muqayyad text, the muqayyad takes precedence because it speaks while the mutlaq is silent.

In this passage, al-Bazdawi presents a rule attributed to Imam al-Shafi'i concerning how to reconcile an absolute (mutlaq) and a restricted (muqayyad) text when both refer to the same incident. The Shafi'i position is that the restricted ruling must be carried over to the absolute by way of indication (dalala). Understanding this rule will help you identify a key point of difference between the Hanafi and Shafi'i usul methodologies.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule
Read the passage and identify the Shafi'i rule: the absolute (mutlaq) is carried to the restricted (muqayyad) in a single incident by way of indication.
2
Understand the rationale
The passage gives two reasons: (1) a single thing cannot be both absolute and restricted simultaneously, and (2) the absolute is silent (sākit) while the restricted is explicit (nāṭiq), making the restricted more appropriate.
3
Examine the example
The hadith 'On five camels, a sheep' is used as an illustration. This hadith restricts the zakat obligation to a specific number and type. According to this rule, any absolute command about zakat on camels would be interpreted in light of this restriction.
The example shows how the rule works in practice.
4
Contrast with Hanafi approach
In Hanafi usul, a mutlaq is applied in its absolute sense unless there is a clear indication (qarīna) that it should be restricted. The Hanafi school does not automatically carry a muqayyad to a mutlaq even in the same incident unless the linkage is explicitly established.
This distinction is crucial for understanding cross-madhhab differences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأبعد من هذه الجملة ما قال الشافعي رحمه الله : من حَمْل المطلق على المقيد في حادثة واحدة بطريق الدلالة؛ لأن الشيء الواحد لا يكون مطلقاً ومقيّداً مع ذلك، والمطلق ساكت، والمقيد ناطق: فكان أولى. كما قيل في قوله عليه الصلاة والسلام في خمس من الإبل : شاة).
And further from these words is what al-Shafi'i said: The carrying of the absolute upon the restricted in a single incident by way of indication; because a single thing cannot be both absolute and restricted simultaneously, and the absolute is silent while the restricted is explicit, so it is more appropriate. As was said in his statement, peace be upon him, concerning five camels: a sheep.
Source pages p. 322

Commentary
This passage presents a usul rule from Imam al-Shafi'i: when a single incident or command appears in both an absolute (mutlaq) form and a restricted (muqayyad) form, the restricted ruling is applied to the absolute by way of indication (dalala). The reason is that a single thing cannot be both absolute and restricted; the absolute is silent (i.e., does not specify), while the restricted is explicit (natiq). Thus the restricted takes precedence. The example given is the hadith: 'On five camels, a sheep' (i.e., zakat on five camels is one sheep). This hadith is restricted to a specific number and type, and it can be used to interpret other absolute statements about zakat. The author of the text (al-Bazdawi) likely distances the Hanafi position from this, as indicated by the opening phrase 'وأبعد من هذه الجملة' (further from these words), suggesting that the Hanafi school may not fully accept this application without further conditions.

Key Terms
مطلق (muṭlaq): An unqualified or absolute expression that does not have a restriction (qayd) attached to it.
مقيد (muqayyad): A qualified or restricted expression that limits the absolute to a specific number, type, condition, etc.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Shafi'i rule as stated, why does the restricted (muqayyad) take precedence over the absolute (mutlaq) in a single incident?
A) Because the absolute is ambiguous and the restricted clarifies it
B) Because the restricted is more recent in time
C) Because the absolute is silent while the restricted is explicit
D) Because the absolute is always interpreted figuratively

Key Takeaway
The Shafi'i rule holds that when an absolute and restricted text address the same incident, the restricted text takes precedence by indication because it is explicit while the absolute is silent.

Combining Texts: The Sabab vs Hukm Distinction in Hanafi Usul
Analyzing why Hanafis combine two hadiths in sadaqat al-fitr but not in kaffarat al-yamin

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule for combining two texts: determine whether the texts pertain to the sabab (cause) or the hukm (ruling), and then state whether both apply or the restricted overrides.

Core Idea
Texts on the sabab can be both absolute and restricted; texts on the hukm cannot — restriction voids absoluteness.

In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, reconciling seemingly contradictory texts often hinges on whether they address the ruling itself or its cause. This lesson explores a classic example: two hadiths on sadaqat al-fitr (combined) versus two readings on kaffarat al-yamin (not combined). Mastering this distinction sharpens your handling of mutlaq and muqayyad texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two texts in each case
For sadaqat al-fitr: one hadith says 'pay on behalf of every free and slave' (absolute), another adds 'from the Muslims' (restricted). For kaffarat al-yamin: the standard reading says 'fast three days' (absolute), Ibn Mas'ud's reading says 'fast three consecutive days' (restricted).
Both pairs represent mutlaq and muqayyad texts.
2
Determine the object of qualification
Ask: does the restriction modify the ruling itself (e.g., how to fast) or the cause triggering the ruling (e.g., who must pay)? In sadaqat al-fitr, 'from the Muslims' modifies the categories of persons (the sabab). In kaffarat al-yamin, 'consecutive' modifies the act of fasting (the hukm).
This is the critical analytical step.
3
Apply the combining rule
If the restriction applies to the sabab, both texts are valid and applied together. If it applies to the hukm, the restricted version overrides the absolute because a single ruling cannot accept contradictory descriptions.
Hanafis combine for sadaqat al-fitr but not for kaffarat al-yamin.
4
Explain the underlying rationale
The hukm (e.g., fasting) is a single existent act; it cannot be both consecutive and non-consecutive simultaneously. The sabab (e.g., categories of persons) can be both general and specific without contradiction.
This reflects the Hanafi usul principle: 'al-hukm la yaqbil al-addad, al-sabab yaqbil aydan.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يلزم عليه ما قلنا في صدقة الفطر: إن النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام قال: «أدُّوا عن كل حر وعبد ... »(۱): مطلقاً. وقال في حديث آخر (۲): «عن كل حر وعبد من المسلمين». وعملنا نحن بهما. بخلاف كفارة اليمين : فإنا لم نجمع بين قراءة عبد الله بن مسعود رضي الله عنه وبين القراءة المعروفة ؛ ليجوز الأمران. والفرق بينهما : أن النصين في كفارة اليمين وَرَدَا في الحكم، والحكم هو الصوم، والصوم في وجوده لا يقبل وصفين متضادين، فإذا ثبت تقييده : بطل إطلاقه. وفي صدقة الفطر : دخل النصان على السبب، ولا مزاحمة في الأسباب، فوجب الجمع.
And it does not necessitate against what we said regarding zakat al-fitr: that the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Pay on behalf of every free and slave...' (1) absolutely. And he said in another hadith (2): 'On behalf of every free and slave from the Muslims.' And we act by both. Contrary to the expiation of an oath: for we did not combine between the recitation of Abdullah ibn Mas'ud (may Allah be pleased with him) and the well-known recitation to permit both matters. And the difference between them: that the two texts in the expiation of an oath came concerning the ruling, and the ruling is fasting, and fasting in its existence does not accept two contradictory descriptions. So when its restriction is established, its absoluteness becomes void. And in zakat al-fitr: the two texts entered upon the cause, and there is no conflict in causes, so combination is obligatory.
Source pages p. 326

Commentary
This passage is a Hanafi textual analysis demonstrating how to reconcile two seemingly conflicting hadiths. The author explains that for zakat al-fitr, two hadiths exist: one absolute (on every free and slave, without qualification) and one restricted (from the Muslims). The Hanafis act by both, applying each according to its context. In contrast, for the expiation of an oath, they do not combine the reading of Ibn Mas'ud (which adds 'consecutive' to the fast) with the standard reading because both texts relate to the ruling itself (fasting), which cannot simultaneously be both restricted and unrestricted. Once restriction is confirmed, the absolute version is void. However, in zakat al-fitr, the texts relate to the cause (sabab) of the obligation (i.e., the categories of persons), which can accommodate multiple descriptions without contradiction, hence both are applied.

Key Terms
حكم (hukm): The legal ruling itself (e.g., obligation to fast) that applies to an act.
سبب (sabab): The cause or occasion for a ruling, which can be described in multiple ways without conflict.
إطلاق (itlaq): Absolute or unqualified expression of a ruling without restriction.
تقييد (taqyid): Restriction or qualification of a ruling by an additional condition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why do Hanafis combine the two hadiths on zakat al-fitr but not the two readings on kaffarat al-yamin?
A) Because the hadiths on zakat al-fitr are both authentic, while those on kaffarat al-yamin are not.
B) Because in zakat al-fitr the texts relate to the cause (sabab), which can accept multiple descriptions, while in kaffarat al-yamin they relate to the ruling (hukm), which cannot.
C) Because the Hanafi school always gives preference to the later hadith.
D) Because the two texts on zakat al-fitr are both from the Prophet, while the readings of kaffarat al-yamin are from Ibn Mas'ud.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, the key to combining or choosing between mutlaq and muqayyad texts is whether the qualification applies to the ruling (hukm) or its cause (sabab).

Fard vs Wajib: The Certainty Divide
Defining wajib and disputing Shafi'i on obligation categories

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi distinction between fard and wajib and apply it to identify whether a denier is excommunicated (takfir) based on the certainty of the evidence.

Core Idea
Fard requires certain proof; wajib suffices with probable proof—thus the ruling on the denier differs.

In Hanafi usul, not all obligations are alike. Some are established by definitive evidence (qat‘i) and others by probable evidence (zanni). This distinction carries weighty consequences, especially regarding the status of one who denies them. In this passage, the author explains the difference and defends the Hanafi position against Imam al-Shafi‘i, who conflates all obligations under one category. Let us trace the evidence and logic behind this pivotal principle.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi Claim
The text defines two categories: fard (from qat‘i evidence) and wajib (from zanni evidence). Notice that both are binding in action, but the certainty of knowledge differs.
Wajib denier is not a disbeliever unless he scorns the evidence; fard denier is a disbeliever.
2
Examine the Shafi‘i Objection
Al-Shafi‘i rejects the division and counts all obligations as fard. The Hanafi response argues that denying either the name or the ruling is invalid because the evidence types are indisputably different.
The dispute is not merely terminological; it affects practical rulings like takfir.
3
Analyze the Proof from Evidence Types
The author contrasts the Quranic verse (general recitation, qat‘i) with the solitary hadith specifying Fatihah (zanni). The verse establishes the fard of recitation; the hadith makes Fatihah wajib, complementing the fard without changing it.
This shows how a general qat‘i text and a specific zanni text coexist.
4
Apply to Other Examples
The text lists other wajib rulings: straightening in bowing (ta‘dil), tawaf with purification, sa‘i, and delaying Maghrib at Muzdalifah. All are based on solitary reports, hence wajib not fard.
If someone rejects these, he is a sinner but not a disbeliever.
5
Determine the Thamarah al-Khilaf (Practical Consequence)
The difference matters in takfir: denier of a wajib (like Fatihah) is not excommunicated, whereas denier of a fard (like general recitation) is. This is the operational outcome of the usul dispute.
The Hanafi position is called 'the straight path'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإسلام وعملاً بالبدن، وهو من أركان الشرائع، ويُكفَرُ جاحده، ويُفَسَّق تاركه بلا عذر. وأما حكم الوجوب : فلزومه عملاً، بمنزلة الفرض، لا علماً على اليقين؛ لما في دليله من الشبهة حتى لا يُكفّر جاحده، ويُفسق تاركه إذا استخف بأخبار الآحاد ، فأما متأولاً : فلا. وأنكر الشافعي رحمه الله هذا القسم، وألحقه بالفرائض. فقلنا: إن أنكر الاسم: فلا معنى له بعد إقامة الدليل على أنه يُخالف اسم الفريضة. وإن أنكر الحكم بطل إنكاره أيضاً؛ لأن الدلائل نوعان: ما لا شبهة فيه من الكتاب والسنة، وما فيه شبهة، وهذا أمر لا يُنكَر، وإذا ثبت تفاوت الدليل : لم يُنكَر تفاوت الحكم. وبيان ذلك : أن النص الذي لا شبهة فيه : أوجب قراءة القرآن في الصلاة، وهو قوله تعالى: ﴿فَاقْرَهُواْ مَا تَيَسَّرَ مِنَ الْقُرْءَانِ ﴾ المزمل / ١٠ ، وخبر الواحد وفيه شبهةً : عَيَّن الفاتحة، فلم يجز تغيير الأول بالثاني، بل يجب العمل بالثاني على أنه مكمل لحكم الأول، مع قرار الأول، وذلك فيما قلنا. (۱) باب العزيمة والرخصة قوله : وخبر الواحد عيّن الفاتحة: عن عبادة بن الصامت أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قال : لا صلاة لمن لم يقرأ بفاتحة الكتاب». رواه الجماعة، وفي لفظ: لا تجزىء صلاةٌ لمن لم يقرأ بفاتحة الكتاب». رواه الدارقطني، وقال: إسناد صحيح. وكذلك الكتاب : أوجب الركوع، وخبر الواحد أوجب التعديل). وكذلك الطواف مع الطهارة (٢). فمَن ردَّ خبر الواحد : فقد ضل عن سواء السبيل. ومَن سواه بالكتاب والسنة المتواترة فقد أخطأ في رفعه عن منزلته، وَوَضَعَ الأعلى عن منزلته ، وإنما الطريق المستقيم: ما قلنا. وكذلك السعي في الحج والعمرة) ، وما أشبه ذلك. وكذلك تأخير المغرب إلى العشاء بالمزدلفة واجب، ثبت بخبر الواحد)، فإذا صلى في الطريق: أُمر بالإعادة عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد رحمهما الله ؛ عملاً بخبر الواحد. (۱) قوله : وخبر الواحد أوجب التعديل : هو حديث أبي هريرة المتقدم في باب بيان معرفة أحكام الخصوص [ص۱۰۸]. (۲) قوله : وكذلك الطواف مع الطهارة : تقدم في أحكام الخصوص [ص١٠٩]. (۳) قوله : وكذلك السعي عن صفية قالت : أخبرني نسوة من بني عبد الدار اللاتي أدركن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم قلن : دخلنا دار ابن أبي حسين، رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم يطوف بين الصفا والمروة والناس بين يديه، وهو وراءهم، وهو يسعى حتى أرى ركبته من شدة ما يسعى وهو يقول: اسعوا فإن الله تعالى كتب عليكم السعي . رواه الدارقطني . قال ابن عبد الهادي : له إسناد صحيح. (٤) قوله : ثبت بخبر الواحد : هو ما أسامة بن زيد قال : رَدِفتُ رسولَ الله صلى الله عليه وسلم من عرفات، فلما بلغ الشُّعب الأيسر الذي دون المزدلفة: أناخ ، فبال، ثم جاء فصببت عليه الوضوء، فتوضأ وضوء خفيفاً، ثم قلت الصلاة؟ قال: الصلاة أمامك». متفق عليه.
Islam and action with the body, and it is among the pillars of the laws, and its denier is disbelieved, and its abandoner without excuse is considered sinful. As for the ruling of obligation (wujub): it is required in action, similar to fard, but not in certain knowledge, because its evidence contains uncertainty; thus its denier is not disbelieved, and its abandoner is considered sinful if he scorns the reports of individuals, but if he makes interpretation, then not. Al-Shafi‘i, may Allah have mercy on him, denied this division and subsumed it under the obligations. We said: if he denies the name, there is no meaning after establishing evidence that it differs from the name of fard. If he denies the ruling, his denial is also invalid, because evidences are two types: that which has no uncertainty from the Book and Sunnah, and that which has uncertainty, and this is undeniable. And when the disparity of evidence is established, the disparity of ruling is not denied. The explanation of that: the text that has no uncertainty required recitation of the Quran in prayer, which is His statement: 'So recite what is easy from the Quran' (al-Muzzammil 20), and the solitary report (khabar al-wahid) which has uncertainty specified the Fatihah; thus it is not permissible to change the first with the second, rather it is necessary to act upon the second as complementing the ruling of the first, with the first remaining, as we have said. (1) Section of Azimah and Rukhsah. He said: The solitary report that specified the Fatihah: from ‘Ubadah ibn al-Samit that the Prophet (sallallahu ‘alayhi wa sallam) said: 'No prayer for the one who does not recite the Fatihah of the Book.' Narrated by the group. In a wording: 'A prayer is not sufficient for one who does not recite the Fatihah of the Book.' Narrated by al-Daraqutni, and he said: Its chain is authentic. Similarly, the Book required bowing, and the solitary report required making it level. Similarly, tawaf with purification. Whoever rejects the solitary report has gone astray from the straight path. And whoever equates it with the Book and Mutawatir Sunnah has erred in raising it from its station and has lowered the higher from its station. And the straight path is only what we have said. Similarly, the sa‘i in Hajj and ‘Umrah, and the like. Similarly, delaying Maghrib to ‘Isha at Muzdalifah is wajib, established by solitary report. If one prays on the way, he is ordered to repeat according to Abu Hanifah and Muhammad, may Allah have mercy on them, acting upon the solitary report. (1) He said: 'The solitary report required making it level' is the hadith of Abu Hurayrah previously mentioned in the chapter on elaboration of rulings of particularity [p.108]. (2) He said: 'Similarly, tawaf with purification' is previously mentioned in rulings of particularity [p.109]. (3) He said: 'Similarly, sa‘i' from Safiyyah who said: Women from Banu ‘Abd al-Dar who met the Messenger of Allah (sallallahu ‘alayhi wa sallam) told me. They said: We entered the house of Ibn Abi Husayn, and the Messenger of Allah (sallallahu ‘alayhi wa sallam) was performing sa‘i between Safa and Marwah, the people before him and he behind them, and he was running so that I saw his knee due to the intensity of his running, and he was saying: 'Run, for Allah has prescribed sa‘i upon you.' Narrated by al-Daraqutni. Ibn ‘Abd al-Hadi said: Its chain is authentic. (4) He said: 'Established by solitary report' is what Usamah ibn Zayd said: I rode behind the Messenger of Allah (sallallahu ‘alayhi wa sallam) from ‘Arafat, and when he reached the left mountain pass before Muzdalifah, he made his camel kneel, then urinated, then came and I poured water for his ablution, and he performed a light ablution. Then I said: The prayer? He said: 'The prayer is ahead of you.' Agreed upon.
Source pages p. 330

Commentary
This passage is a Hanafi usul definition combined with a dispute against Imam al-Shafi‘i. The author defines two categories: fard (established by certain evidence) and wajib (established by evidence with uncertainty, i.e., khabar al-wahid). Wajib is binding in action (the same consequences as fard for performance/abandonment), but its denier is not considered a disbeliever because the evidence is not qat‘i. Al-Shafi‘i rejected this distinction and considered all obligations as fard. The Hanafi response is based on the two types of evidence: qat‘i (Book and Mutawatir) and zanni (khabar al-wahid). Since the evidence differs, the legal categories differ. The author provides examples: the Quran commands general recitation, but a solitary hadith specifies the Fatihah as obligatory—this is wajib, not fard. Similarly, bowing is fard, but straightening it (ta‘dil) is wajib from a solitary hadith. Tawaf with wudu, sa‘i, and delaying Maghrib at Muzdalifah are all wajib based on solitary reports. The author strongly defends khabar al-wahid against those who reject it absolutely or equate it with Mutawatir, saying the correct path is to give it its proper intermediate station. The footnotes provide the hadith references for each example.

Key Terms
الوجوب (al-wujub): Obligation that is binding in action but not in certainty of knowledge; established by evidence with uncertainty (zanni), such as khabar al-wahid.
الفرض (al-fard): Obligation established by certain evidence (qat‘i), such as the Quran or Mutawatir Sunnah; its denier is a disbeliever.
خبر الواحد (khabar al-wahid): A report transmitted by one or a few narrators, not reaching tawatur; its proof is zanni and cannot establish certainty (yaqin).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is the denier of wajib (established by khabar al-wahid) not considered a disbeliever?
A) Because wajib is not binding at all.
B) Because its evidence contains shubha (uncertainty), so denying it does not reject a certain matter.
C) Because khabar al-wahid is not a valid proof.
D) Because wajib is only recommended, not obligatory.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, the certainty of evidence (qat‘i vs. zanni) creates two distinct obligation categories—fard and wajib—with different consequences for the denier.

Distinguishing Cause and Condition: Fasting and Zakat al-Fitr
Usul rule on sabab vs shart with examples of fasting and zakat al-fitr

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul criteria of attribution and repetition to identify whether a given factor is a cause (sabab) or a condition (shart) for an act of worship.

Core Idea
A cause repeats with the ruling and is linguistically attributed; a condition does neither.

This passage from al-Bazdawi's Usul al-Fiqh introduces a key Hanafi tool: how to identify the cause behind a ruling. Using fasting and zakat al-fitr, the author shows that a cause is recognized by two signs—attribution and repetition—while a condition lacks both. Understanding this distinction is crucial for deriving rulings in new cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine which worship is being discussed (e.g., fasting Ramadan, zakat al-fitr) and what ruling applies (obligation).
2
Locate the linguistic attribute
Look at how the act is named in the sources (e.g., 'fasting of Ramadan', 'zakat al-fitr'). The preposition or genitive construction often reveals the cause.
Attribution is key: 'sawm Ramadan' attributes the fasting to the month.
3
Check for repetition
Does the obligation multiply or repeat with the supposed cause? For fasting, each day of Ramadan causes its own fast. For zakat, the obligation multiplies with the number of dependents.
Repetition of the ruling with the repetition of the factor indicates causality.
4
Apply the two tests
If the factor is both attributed to the act (linguistically) and the obligation repeats with it, it is a cause. If not, it is a condition.
The passage states: 'And it repeats with its repetition, and is attributed to it, so it is said: fasting of the month of Ramadan, and attribution necessitates causality.'
5
Confirm with performance after delay
A cause allows valid performance even after the command was delayed (e.g., a traveler fasting after the day began). Conditions do not have this feature.
The example of a boy reaching puberty mid-month or a disbeliever embracing Islam shows each day is an independent cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جعل سبباً : كان ظرفاً صالحاً للأداء والليلُ لا يصلح له، فعلم أن اليوم سببه، بدلالة نسبته إليه وتعليقه ،به وتعليقُ الحكم بالشيء شرعاً دليل على أنه سببه ، هذا هو الأصل في الباب، وقد تكرر بتكرره، ونُسب إليه، فقيل : صوم شهر رمضان، والنسبة تقتضي السببية.
وصحَّ الأداء بعده من المسافر، وقد تأخر الخطاب به عنه، ولهذا جب على صبي يبلغ في بعض شهر رمضان، وكافر يُسلم بقدر ما أدركه ؛
لأن كل يوم سبب لصومه، بمنزلة كل وقت من أوقات الصلاة.
وقد مرت أحكام هذا القسم أيضاً.
وسبب وجوب صدقة الفطر على كل مسلم غني: رأس يمونه بولايته عليه، ثبت ذلك بقول النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام: «أدُّوا عن كل حر وعبد (1)
وبقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام : أدوا عمن تمونون»(2).
وبيانه أن كلمة : عن ; لانتزاع الشيء، فتدل على أحد وجهين : إما أن يكون سبباً يُنتزع الحكمُ عنه، أو محلاً يجب الحق عليه، فيؤدى عنه،
وبطل الثاني ; لاستحالة الوجوب على العبد والكافر والفقير، فعلم به أنه سبب، ولذلك يتضاعف الوجوب بتضاعف الرؤوس.
وأما وقت الفطر فشرطه حتى لا يعمل السبب إلا بهذا الشرط، وإنما نُسب إلى الفطر مجازاً، والنسبة تحتمل الاستعارة، فأما تضاعف الوجوب فلا يحتمل الاستعارة.
وبيان قولنا : إن الإضافة تحتمل الاستعارة: ظاهر؛ لأن الشيء يضاف إلى الشرط مجازاً، فأما تَضَاعُفُ الوجوب، فلا يحتمل الاستعارة؛ لأن الوجوب إنما يكون بسبب أو علة، لا يكون بغير ذلك، وهذا لا تتصور فيه الاستعارة، وكذلك وصف المؤنة يُرجّح الرأس في كونه سبباً، وقد بينا معنى المؤنة منه في موضعه.
وسبب وجوب حج البيت أنه يُنسب إليه، ولم يتكرر، قال الله
تعالى: ﴿وَلِلَّهِ عَلَى النَّاسِ حِجُّ الْبَيْتِ ﴾ آل عمران / 97
وأما الوقت : فهو شرط الأداء، بدلالة أنه لا يتكرر بتكرره، غير أن الأداء شرع متفرقاً منقسماً على أمكنة وأزمنة يشتمل عليها جملة وقت الحج، فلم يصح تغييرُ الترتيب كما لا يصح السجود قبل الركوع، فلذلك لم يجز طواف الزيارة قبل يوم النحر، والوقوف قبل يوم عرفة.
وأما الاستطاعة بالمال : فشرط، لا سبب ; لما ذكرنا إنه لا يُنسب إليه، ولا يتكرر بتكرره، ويصح الأداء دونه من الفقراء.
ألا يُرى أنه عبادة بدنية، فلا يصلح المال سبباً لها، ولكنها عبادة
If something is made a cause: it is a valid container for performance, and the night is not valid for it. So it is known that the day is its cause, by indication of its attribution to it and its attachment to it. And the attachment of a ruling to something by law is evidence that it is its cause. This is the principle in the chapter. And it repeats with its repetition, and is attributed to it, so it is said: 'fasting of the month of Ramadan', and attribution necessitates causality. And performance after it is valid from the traveler, even though the command is delayed relative to him. For this reason, it is obligatory upon a boy who reaches puberty in some of the month of Ramadan, and a disbeliever who accepts Islam to the extent of what he caught, because each day is a cause for its fast, like each time of the prayer times. And the rulings of this section have also passed. And the cause of obligation of sadaqat al-fitr upon every wealthy Muslim: a dependent head under his guardianship. That is established by the saying of the Prophet, peace be upon him: 'Give on behalf of every free person and slave' (1). And by his saying, peace be upon him: 'Give on behalf of those whom you support' (2). And its explanation: the word 'an' is for extraction, so it indicates one of two aspects: either it is a cause from which the ruling is extracted, or a locus upon whom the right is obligatory, and then it is paid on his behalf. And the second is invalid because of the impossibility of obligation upon the slave, the disbeliever, and the poor. So it is known by this that it is a cause. And for that reason, the obligation multiples with the multiplicity of heads. As for the time of fitr, it is its condition, so that the cause only operates with this condition. And it is attributed to fitr metaphorically, and attribution can bear metaphor. As for the multiplication of obligation, it does not bear metaphor. And the explanation of our statement: 'the addition can bear metaphor' is apparent, because a thing is added to the condition metaphorically. As for the multiplication of obligation, it does not bear metaphor because obligation only comes about due to a cause or effective cause, not otherwise, and metaphor is not conceivable in this. Likewise, the description of maintenance gives weight to the head being a cause, and we have explained the meaning of maintenance from it in its place. And the cause of obligation of Hajj to the House is that it is attributed to it, and it is not repeated. Allah Most High says: 'And for Allah is the Hajj of the House upon the people' (Aal-e-Imran, 97). As for the time, it is a condition of performance, indicated by the fact that it does not repeat with its repetition. However, the performance is legislated dispersed, divided over places and times that the totality of the time of Hajj encompasses. So changing the order is not valid, just as prostration before bowing is not valid. Therefore, it is not permissible to perform the visitation circumambulation before the Day of Sacrifice, nor standing before the Day of Arafah. As for financial ability, it is a condition, not a cause, for what we mentioned: it is not attributed to it, nor does it repeat with its repetition, and performance is valid without it from the poor. Do you not see that it is a physical worship, so wealth cannot be a cause for it, but it is a worship.
Source pages p. 349

Commentary
This passage is a refined usul principle distinguishing between sabab (cause) and shart (condition) in acts of worship, using three key examples. The author first establishes the rule: when a ruling is attached to something by Islamic law, that thing is a cause. The cause repeats with the repetition of the ruling and is attributed to it (e.g., 'fasting of Ramadan' implies each day is a cause). Performance after the cause is valid even if the command was delayed (e.g., a traveler can fast after the day begins). The example of a boy reaching puberty or a disbeliever converting mid-month shows each day is an independent cause. For zakat al-fitr, the cause is each dependent head one supports, proven by the Prophet's commands to give on behalf of each. The word 'an' (on behalf of) denotes extraction, so it must be either a cause or a locus. The latter is invalid because a slave, disbeliever, or poor person cannot be obligated; thus it is a cause, and the obligation multiplies with the number of dependents. The time of fitr is a condition, not a cause, because it is attributed metaphorically (e.g., 'zakat al-fitr' meaning 'zakat at fitr') and does not cause multiplication. For Hajj, the cause is the House itself, as it is attributed to it (Hajj al-Bayt) and does not repeat (Hajj is once in a lifetime). The time (Hajj season) is a condition for performance, not a cause, as it does not repeat; performance is spread over specific places and times. Financial ability is a condition, not a cause, because it is not attributed to the worship, does not repeat with repetition, and the poor can perform Hajj without it. Since Hajj is a physical worship, wealth cannot be its cause.

Key Terms
سبب (sabab): A cause that directly brings about a ruling, evidenced by attribution and repetition
شرط (shart): A condition for the cause to operate, not itself a cause; does not cause repetition and is not attributed to the act except metaphorically
عن (an): Preposition meaning 'on behalf of', used to extract the ruling; indicates either cause or locus

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT a sign of a cause (sabab) according to the passage?
A) Attribution
B) Repetition
C) Validity of performance after delay
D) The ruling must be explicitly stated

Key Takeaway
The two signs of a cause are attribution and repetition; conditions lack these.
